REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLJC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
CRED1T AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Republic of Cape Verde
Beneficarie:            All 15 local authorities of Cape Verde; Ministries of Economic
Coordination, Finance, Public Administration, Justice and Labor, Internal
Administration, Communications; and Public and Private News Media
Credit Amount:          SDR 5,700,000 (US$ 8.1 million equivalent)
Termis:                 Standard IDA terms, with 40 years maturity
Coordinated Financing:  UNDP: US$ 0.3 million equivalent; Switzerland: 1.0 million; France: US$
1.0 million; EC: US$ 4.0 million.
ProZect (2W3ectives     The major objective of the proposed project is to assist the Government of
and Descrlp.tion:     Cape Verde implement its long-term public sector institutional development
strategy. The project would support improvements in the legal, judicial and
administrative environment for investment and for business operations, and
economic policy analysis and management, through a program of
institutional development and capacity building in public administration,
both at the central and at the municipal levels. It would complement the
ongoing restructuring of the public enterprise sector (Cr. 2377-CV),
transport and infrastructure modernization (Cr. 2466-CV), as well as the
planned Basic Education and Training and Financial/Private Sector
Development Projects, and provide the underpinnings for the delivery of
basic services at the local level to be supported under the proposed Basic
Social Services Project.
The project would be a specific investment credit, operating within a
framework outlined in the Policy Letter. It would consist of five
components: (i) civil service modernization; (ii) municipal strengthening;
(iii) economic management support; (iv) legal and judicial upgrading; and
(v) public procurement reform. They are described below.
The first component will provide assistance to reform and modernize the
civil srice by providing short-term advisory services and equipment to the
Ministry of Public Administration, training for staff from various parts of
the civil service, and training and retraining for voluntary departees from
the civil service. In particular, the component would consist of: (a) the
implementation of improved human resource planning and management
systems (organization planning, performance evaluativa, career planning,
employee development, etc.) to promote and organize vertical and
horizontal mobility of personnel, in direct relation with short and long term



requirements and with the priorities of the various public services and
organization units in response to their new role; (b) implementing a
comprehensive training program directed at managers, administrators and
support staff in public administration, sector policy and analysis, in response
to the new role played by the State In the economy, and to the requirements
of a truly public service-oriented administration; and (c) the design of a civil
service retrenchment implementation strategy, and implementation of
demand-driven training and retraining schemes which would also includejob
search assistance to facilitate redeployment of voluntary departees from the
civil service.
The second component will provide assistance for building local capagity
to garticipate and to manage (decentralization and municipal strengtheing
through the provision of training, equipment, short-term advisory services
and support for operational studies for all 15 municipalities and, where
appropriate, the Ministry of Internal Administration. In particular, the
component would involve: (a) institution-building: further detailing
guidelines for the decentralization process; supporting establishment of
inter-municipal technical support offices (TSOs), to ensure a "critical mass'
through which scarce technical support and equipment can be shared; and
providing the means to facilitate closer contact between the municipal
authorities and the population of the various villages and/or citizens' groups,
and local community associations; (b) developing sound administration and
management practices: planning, budgeting and inventory systems to ensure
transparency and accountability in fiscal and related matters; and local fiscal
administration and revenue mobilization capacity thereby ensuring
sustainability of the decentralization process and improvements in local
services provision; (c) actions in human resources development: helping the
municipalities attract and retain qualified personnel; training within the
framework of a comprehensive program for existing staff; and procedures
for personnel management and their adequate use; and (d) strengthening the
internal organization of the municipalities: rationalizing the organization of
the various municipal services that could eventually become autonomous
services or be contracted out; and the provision of basic equipment to
implement the new systems and the provision of services by the
municipalities.
The third component will support and strenghen economic management
oapacily by assisting with operational studies and public information
campaigns, and providing advisory services, training, equipment, library
support to the Ministries of Economic Coordination, Finance, and
Communications and their major agencies (Statistics, Planning, Public
Finance, Radio, etc.) as well as the private media. The component will
specifically involve: (a) restructuring the national accounts and statistics
system and catching up on preparation of backlog of essential statistics; (b)
strengthening economic policy and impact analysis capacity of the Planning
Directorate General, establishing a multi-year rolling investment program
(PIP), and a pre-investment studies fund (PIP) to relieve the constraints on
public investments caused by the present lack of properly prepared projects,
and reinforce institutional capacity for identifying public investment
proposals and processing these adequately. The PIF would also support the
- ii -



final preparation of the National Envirownental Action Plan (NEAP); (c)
supporting improvement in external debt and public finance management,
aid coordination, and assessing the feasibility of and designing and
implementing, if appropriate, mechanisms for increased public resource
mobilization through expansion of the offering of Treasuiy Bills to the
public at large; (d) Improving the communication of economic and social
policies, including the training of journalists; (e) testing pilot scbemes
designed to facilitate the provision of assistance in various fields of
economic and social development by non-resident Cape Verdeans; (t)
improving intra-government communications through the introduction of
electronic communication systems and training; and (g) designing a shared
data system for government operations.
The fourth component will provide assistance for legal and judicial refoms
and modernizing the justice system through the provision of training,
equipment and materials, textbooks and printing, and advisory services for
the Ministry of Justice and Labor and its major agencies including the
Judiciary, the Rcgistrar of Companies, and the Registrar of Deeds as well
ss legal services for key ministries. The component will include such actions
as: (a) revision of commercial and labor legislation; (b) specialized training
in business law for magistrates and, possibly, other members of the legal
profession; (c) strengthening of the capacity of the Ministry of Justice to
draft, revise and update legislation on a routine basis; (d) establishment of
a specialized jurisdiction(s) and, if need be, arbitration facilities, to dispose
of commercial law disputes; and (e) provision of modern computerized
facilities for specialized legal services dealing with company registry,
notarial services and business activities.
The fifth component will provide assistance for continued reform of public
procurement by providing training, short-term advisory services and
equipment to the Public Procurement Department (PATRIMONIO) and
selected administration departments in GOCV. The component will include,
in particular: (a) selective revision of the existing legislation for
procurement of goods and services; (b) training for the technical staff of
PATRIMONIO as well as the staff In charge of the purchase and
distribution of goods in the various administration departnents of GOCV;
and (c) modernizing the purchasing departments through computerization of,
and training for about 40 staff in, systems for planning, purchasing and
storing of information.
Projec Benefits          The project would assist a business-oriented Government at a critical
and Rids:               time in the implementation of its liberalization program by adapting the
administration and the civil service as well as the judicial system to a new
vision of the role of Government in the economy. It would support the
policy of the Government to create an enabling environment for private
sector development. It would foster efficiency in the public administration,
through the implementation of modern human resource management systems
and procedures. It would also promote greater participation In planning and
managing the development process at the local level, and foster
accountability. It would increase the availability, and reduce the delays
encountered in the preparation and execution, of public investment projects
- Iii -



and increase efficiency in public resource use. Improve;. ent in investment
execution monitoring and in policy and Impact analysis would help the
Government in its efforts to reduce poverty. It would help remove, over
time, the constraints on sound economic management due to a weak
analytical base.
There is some risk that vested interests within the civil service will resist
reform measures as these impact on their entrenched ways of performing
their tasks, and may involve physical relocation of staff to municipal
governments. Given *he positive achievements of the Government to-date
in these fields, and strong grass-root support for decentralization, these risks
are not considered high. There Is a risk that the training of civil servants
and the retraining of voluntary departees from the civil service could be
slowed down by the limited capacity of local training institutions. This risk
is not considered insurmountable because the Government is, In parallel
with this project, embarking on a restructuring of vocationa education for
which it has requested IDA support through the proposed Basic Education
and Training Project. The implementation of the reform program may also
be slowed down by the limited capacity of the Government administration
to design the details of some of the necessary reforms and to monitor their
implementation. These risks are addressed through the strengthening of the
project's execution agencies and the appointment of a coordinator in the
Ministry of Economic Coordination. The Government has also shown a
capacity to monitor complex reforms under its ongoing public enterprise
restructuring program. Finally, implementation of the Government's civil
service retrenchment program (voluntary departure aspects), though not an
explicit part of the proposed project, could lose momentum if the added
strain on the private sector's already limited capacity to absorb new labor
force entrants results in higher unemployment and a political backlash. Ihis
risk will be minimized by an attractive incentive package that provides
monthly income payments for up to three years - just enough to meet
subsistence needs of retrenchees without discouraging them from looking for
a job - in addition to a lump sum distribution at the time of departure. The
Government has already made its budgetary allocations for the payments,
and key donors have expressed interest in providing funding, it the need
arises.
Ehxironfmental Risks:    No environmental risks are foreseen. The project basically involves the
financing of consultant services, office and commurncation equipment and
training. GOCV intends to engage advisory services under the project to
finalize the National Enviromnental Action Plan (NEAP).

fconi Rate of Return: Not applicable.

A. COUNTRY BACKGROUND

1.3   In 1988, the previous Govenment of Cape Verde recognized these shortcoming and adopted an
outward-looking developmet strategy. It failed, however, to filly implement this strategy. Ihe export-
crdented strategy In fact stood liltle chance of success given the failure to define more clearly an appropriate
goverment role in the economy In light of a changing world. A new Govemment took office in April 1991,
following its electio  in the first multi-party elecdons since independence on a platform of reform and
libelization of the economy. Cape Verde's quiet and remarkable political transition went largely unnoticed
in the International community because it coincided with the Gulf war. A first-order activity has been the
fiurher definition of the outward-looking development strategy, aimed at promoting a market economy, and
the undertakdng of a broad range of specific actions towards its implementation. A major priority i the
reform, rationalization and modnization of the public sector. Box 1 presents background on Cape Verde
in a snapshot.
B. MACRO-ECONOMUC CONTEXT AND POLICIES
1.4   Economic growth slowed to 3.7% per annum over 1989-1991 from 6% during the eighties, with
mounting inflationary pressures and intal and enal imbalances. Stagnation of the world economy has
been a factor together with the reduced fore'gn exchange earing capacity of iernationat airport and
maritime services, due to political uncertiies and decreasing international competitiveness. Rainfall deficits
also cubed agricultural output. Endogenous causes, however, strongly contributed to the economic recession.
Foremost among these were structural deficiencies resulting from the excessive reliance on public enterprises
to produce essentia goods ar services for a narrow domestic market. Emerging private initiatve was st;fled
by pervasive regulations, bureaucratic allocation of foreign exchange, and excessive protection of local labor.
Less than adequate investment planning and implementation might also have played a role, together with the
decreasing number of large investment projects which curtailed the inflow of foreign exchange. Above all,
the deterioron of economic performance stemmed from the poor economic and fincial performance of
public enterprises, resulting in lower contributions to budget revenues and increased deficits, escalation of
borrowing and internal and eal arrears, and pressing calls for Government financial help. The decline
of profitability was especially severe for public enterprises In key sectors of the economy: fishing, air and
maritime transport, petroleum import and ditbution, and agro-process,.
1.5   As early as 1986, the Government undertook to recast its economic policy which led to the adoption,
two years later, of the outwardlooking development strategy. The needed follow-up acton on designing new
procedures and enacting detailed legislation was somewhat overlooked during the long and intense political
campaign period that ended late in December 1991, with the election of regional assemblies and town
councils. The new Government confirmed the outward-looking strategy. Action is now being taken to
streamline public dm   on, reduce Government participation In public enteprises through restrcturing,
privatization, or lquidation, enrhance Govaernment capacity to design economic reforms, and integrate sector
priorides into growth-effective Investment plans.
1.6   The Government has itiated a parti transfer of responsibilities to local governing institutons.
Municipal and regional assemblies already enjoy limited power to legislate and raise revenues, but still derive
most of their resources from the Government budget. While there are several compelling arguments for
reditributig responsibilities and resources between the cental government and local authorities (see Section
B.4 below), the sheer distance and ocean waters separating Praia from the outlying islands - in some cases
an hour or more by air - and the rugged terrain of the islands, are reason alone for encouraging the process
of decentralization. If implemented prudently, this process will reWlt in more rapid, adaptive and efficient
responses to the needs of the far flung cizenry, who wiD have better access to decision makers and a
stronger voice in the design and implementation of programs and projects that respond to basic needs. Ihere
-2-



are however potential pitfalls: (I) rerc'rce wit be inefficinty used if administrative, technical and finci
capabilities are not developed at the regional and Township levels; and (ii) mechanisms need to be set up to
ensure resources are allocated in accordance with sound a;tional priorities, transparent formule and equity,
rather than the political clout of local leasd.  Some work remains to be done In streamlining the
decentralization process including some Arter detailing fo trsferrig responsibilities.
1.7   The Government has put a premium on developing a competitive private sector. The prvae  sector
is not yet well-organized ad will require substanti assistance In developing export markets. Much local
attention is currenty focused on the potential response of foreign investors both to the privatzation of some
public enteprises and to the expansion of key sectors like tourism, fisheries, bang, and export processing.
Joint venture with locai investors could catalyze and strengthen the domestc priva sector, ad options of
this kind are encouraged. Of paramount Importance in the global competition for foreign nvestment is the
modernization of national Infastructure. Cape Verde has inadequate transport, telecommunicains, water
supply, and electricity networks. Removal of many infrastructure bottlenecks is an essential condition for
developing international comparative advantages and improving local standards of living. The 1992 recut
budget, of which two-thirds is plowed into infrstcture and social services (36% and 27% respecIvely),
is evidence of Government's shifting role from direct intervention in production to provision of basic services
and facilitator of private investment and economic growth.
1.8   The Government's stratg (Pat I.D) confronts squarely Cape Verde's skewed ecommy, in whic
export earnings for example, cover only 5% of import needs and it builds on Cape Verde's stability and its
leaders' determination and commitment to the econoic Strategy, one trly -owned- by them. There are real
prospects in building on Cape Verde's service traditon, and on its relatively educated population with stMg
lihn overseas. Tourism tor instance, offers interesting possibilities, when seen In light of the contrast
between the Canary Islands and Cape Verde: 6 million versus 30,000 arrivals in 1991. Fishery Is another
potnidal growth industry - the country disposes of (630,000 sq. km of territorial waters) - but a clear
strategy there is not yet on the horizon.
C. PROSPECTS FOR GROWTH
1.9   In the medium term (1994-1996), asuming sustained application of reforms, includg those in public
sector management, economic growth is projected to average 5.5% a year in real terms. Public investment
would provide the main engine of growth in the short term, wbhe over time the share of export-oriented
activites would increase moderately. Specifically, growth is expected to come from: (i) investments In
infrructure, modernizationof telecommunications, housing and water supply and sewerage systems in urban
areas; i) moderate private investment in fisheries, tourism, services (including banking and teleports), and
light manufg for export; (ii) higher agricultural output provided rainfall is adequate; (lv) contmous
growth in private housing construction and in commerce; and (v) growth in services to international air and
maritme transport, including transhipment. Given the key role that public investment will play, planning
and implementation of the public investment program (PIP) need to be stegthened, and harnessed much
more effectively as an effective insm  for aid coordination.
1.10   Eternal debt servicing was not a major Issue until recently. The extal arrears that acumulated
in 1989 and 1990 resulted from deferred negotiations with two major donors on rescheduling debt service,
and from failure of some public enterises to service their debt. lbe arrears have been rescheduled and
payments have resumed. Ihe debt service ratio averaged about 11% over 19861990 and is projected to
remain unchanged due to a continuous high share of concessional lending. The improvement of aid
cordinadon is essenti for effective mobilization of extrnal resources toward priority uses, in support of
the outward-looking economic development strategy.
- 3-



1.11  With high unemployment (about 25%) and underemployment, and 3,000 young adults entering the
job market each year, high growth and labor-intensive strategies are a must. Poor job infornation and
employer-employee matchmaking services, and the slow down in public works progrms, ar also sning
the labor market's absorptve capacity. These underlying trends will challenge the Govenmens bold
privatization plans, for virually all 22 wholly-owned and 12 mixed enterprises, employing about 6,400 people
as well as its plans for a reduction in the civil service workforce by about 1,700 persons.
n1.    THE M=Ll sEro
A. CHARACTERISTCS OF THE PUBLIC SECrOR
1.    Overview
2.1   In Cape Verde, the two pillars of the public sector, central and local govermnt, and the public
enterprises together they employ more than 19,000 people. The country's public sector has managed the
economy, developed infrastructure, provided social services and established and managed public sector
entrapises for power, water and telecommunications utilities, and for light manufacturing, ship building, food
production and processing, and maritime and air transport. It has played a dominant role in the eonomy of
Cape Verde, pady because the private sector was small bnd lacking i entre u   capacity and capital
or assets for borrowing. Ihe sheer depth and breath of the public sector's interventions in economic, fiscal,
production and social development activities produced a numerically large public administion and 36 public
eterpises.
2.2    Consistent with the Government's efforts to roll back its role in the economy, It has embarked on a
far-reaching - and thus far successful - public enterprise restructuing program, a process whih IDA is
assting. Facts and figures on the public enterprise sector are in Annex 3. Streamining, moderizing, an d
srengthening the capacity of the public sector proper' is a central objective of GOCV and would be cruci
for the success of its economic reform program. For an in-depth discussion of the public sector, see Annexes
3 through 6. The discussion that follows oudines the stucture and organization of public administation and
sketches the proffle of the Civil Service.
2.    Structure and Orgnization of Public Admhinstration Sector
2.3   Im.u for Reform  Public adminstmon in Cape Verde has a long history. t is wellorgnized
and established, dedicated and respected but lacks proper facilities and equipment, and efective procedures
and practices for modern management and human resources development. Its capacity for policy-ming and
impact analysis is also weak. Unlike the case of many other Sub-Saharan Afiican counties, the impetus for
reform is not fiscal pressure. Ihis represents a special opportunity to retool the Civil Servie with more
modern instruments of human resource management, which will enable it to fillfili a new service-oriented
mission in support of the State as facilitator rather than manager of economic growth. Cost-ontainmt in
Cape Verde is not an end in itself, which could undermine capacity-building, but an abiding concern in the
larger effort to raise efficiency and pay for a more adaptive and skilled Civil Servce.
2.4   Strucbre. Ihe central government apparatus comprises 13 ministies and the Office of the Prime
Minister, which includes the department for youth and social promotion, the Agency for Promotion of
' Henceforth in this report, the terms public sector", -public sector proper', and -public

references would be to the 'public enterprise sector.'

xpors (PROMX) and tie Ministry of Defence (and its amed forces of 125 strog). At the local levd,

the changing economic and social situation in the country, the structure of the Government was realined in
March 1993. The Ministers of Justice and Labor, and Finance and Planning, were elevated to the rank of
Minsters of State, with the latter assuming responsibility for a new Ministry of Economic Coordination
(MEC). Finance became a separate ministry.
2.5  OQ animg. The organizational scture of the 13 mistries is similar and typically Acludes the
Cabinet of the Minister and a Secretary of State (in five Ministries) who oversees the work of the Ministy.
'Me Secretary of State is assisted by one or more Director(s) General in charge of departents, and by
Directors who manage units. Most ministries have departments of studies and research, or planning, to
coordinate and carry out analytical work. Each ministry, except Economic Coordination, has a Director of
Administrative Services, heading up a department responsible for personnel management, fiace, fadiities
and procurement.
3.    Profile of the Civil Service
2.6   In 1992, the total wage bill of the public administration amounted to 52 percent of total recurt
expenditures. Ihis is not high when compared to many countries of the region (except The Gambia) and
esewhere.
2.7   EmplomentTrends. The size of the Civil Service in Cape Verde remained fairly stable from 1975
to 1985, growing from 6,800 to 7,800 persons during the period, or by slightly less than 15 percent. In the
five years between 1985 and 1990, however, it increased rapidly from 7,800 to 11,300 employees, or by
almost half. During this five-year period, the highest rate of growth took place in the last two years (1988-
90). On a yearly basis, between 1975 and 1985, employment in the civil service expanded at an average rate
of less than 2% per annum, much slower than economic growth (6%) and population growth (2.7%). In the
following five years (1985-1990) this employment grew by 9% a year-about 1.5 times the GDP growth rate.
Further details are in Annex 4-B, Charts 1 and 2. Most of the increase took place before the first multi-party
elections.
2.8   Pending a thorough assessment of the impact of its policy on Public Administran, and a better
uderstnding of the strengths and weaknesses of the civil service, the new Government, in 1992, froze all
new recruitment, except for professional-level staff on a selective basis. In a second phase, the goverment
inends to selectively bring down the size of the civil service, and improve the adequacy of its personnel in
line with the new role of the State. Between 1990 and 1993, the size of the civil service acaly slighldy
decreased from 11,300 to 11,172 through a combination of natural attrition and freeze in the recritment of
almost all categories of personnel and, in particular, the lower level and unskilled. A classification of civi
servants by category is in Annexes 3 and 4.
2.9   Pay and Expenditure Trenhds. Unlike the patten found in many Sub-Saharan African countries, real
wages have kept pace with inflation, and the pay structure has become somewhat less compressed. In Janay
1989, with the amnua consumer price index rising 4.4 percent over the previous twelve months, the
Govenment graed  a 17 percent across-the-board pay increase for all but highlevel managers, whose
salaries were increased by 17 percent to 28 percent. This was followed by another 17 percent increase in
1991, and a further adjustment in 1992 owing to a regrading exercise. Whereas the before-tax ratio of the
base pay of amanager in Grade A to that of an unskilled worker in Grade U was 4.1:1 in 1987, that ratio
had Improved to 5.1:1 by 1992 (MF, 1993). If the salaries of directors-general are included, the
compression ratio stands at about 7.1:1. Positions are graded and paid on the basis of well-defined points
for job content and responsibility, and the salary structure, published for all to see in the Official Gazette,
Is free from the distortions of excessive fringe benefits.
- 5-



2.10   The sie of the civil service wage bill is less an issue than the share of budgetary resources allocated
to the facilities, equipment and other goods and services civil servants need to be productive. Pay increases,
combined with the rapid rise in employment of unskilled workers in the run-up to the multi-party elections,
contibuted to a rising wage bill, which represented 52 percent of recurrent expenditures in 1992 and 7.5 per
of GDP, compared to 44 percent in 1988. The increase in the wage bi1l as a share of recurrent expenditure
has been offset largely by decreases in the share of transfers to public enterprises and local government, and
inrest payments. At the same time, outlays on goods and services have not improved much, falling as a
share of recurrent expenditure from 7.4 percent in 1988 to 6.3 percent in 1992, although they are expected
to rise again to 7.5 percent In 1993 
2.11   Oualificatton Profile. Of the 11,172 civil service employees, 3,355 persons, or one-third of the total
(43 percent if teachers are excluded), were office and service employees of low professional qualification.
A high proportion of those are in the "servente" category (i.e. cleaners, office messengers, and such) which,
by itself, groups more than 10 percent of all civil service employees. Another 737 persons, or 6.5 percent
(10 percent if excluding teachers) were blue-collar workers. Excluding the Ministry of Education, only 1,625
civil servants, or 13 percent of the total (20 percent excluding teachers) were in civil service grades requiring
a level of education of high school or above.
2.12   Of the 5,507 recruitment in the last five years, 1691 persons, or 30 percent of the total, were blue-
and whie-collar workers of the lowest civil service classification. Another 2,508 persons, or 45 percen,
were integrated in the civil service as teachers, but close to half that number in the classification open to those
possessing only the lowest minimum qualification required to teach at primary school level. Only 670 of the
new civil servants, or 12 percent, belonged to the various classification of the civil service requiring a
minimum of high school-level education.
2.13   As a consequence, the recent increase of Its size actually contributed to decrease the average level
of qualification of public servants, just when the new economic policy of the government will require a
significant improvement in professionalism, both in technical and managerial terms, of the civil service. The
level of education of public servants is shown in Table 1 below:

2.14 &ge Djstution. The civil service of Cape Verde is young. Two thousand and fifty (2,050) people,
or 29 percent of those for which age data is available, are of less than 29 yeas of age. Another 3,060, or
43 percent, are of between 30 and 39 years of age. Although the mandatory retirement age in Cape Verde
is 65 years (with an option to retire at 60 years of age for those with 34 years in the civil service), 46 persons
of over 70 years of age were still employed by the civil service in March 1993, either because they occupied
an essenti position for which no qualified replacement could yet be found and hired, or because they
belonged to one of the lowest income categories and their contract was renewed at their own request.
Annex 4 provides a detailed breakdown of the age profile of civil servants.
2.15  Qender Di. Women and men are more or less equally represented in the Public
Administration in Cape Verde, with respectively 46 and 54 percent of the total civil servants. While women
are under-represented in, or totally absent from, cerain grades, this does not reflect a deliberate
discriminatory policy but rather the nature of the jobs associated with the grade (blue-collar for instance).
Further, an analysis by grade shows that Cape Verde Is fairly advanced by any standard in promoting women
to managerial positions. Women account for slighdy less than 50 percent of the middle management position
('Technico"), 25 percent of the top civil servants ("Dirigente*) and 50 percent of the Cabinet positions ("De
Quadro Especial"). (See Chart 6 in Annex 4-B).
B. MAJOR ISSUES IN PUBLIC SECTOR PERFORMANCE
1.    Institutional Environment for Civil Service Reforms
2.16 Evidence from Africa and other regions shows that technical interventions to improve the efficiency
and effectiveness of public administration fail outright, or produce meager and unsustainable results, when
undertaken in a non-conducive environment. Of paramount importance is the governance system (i.e., role
of the State vis-a-vis the civil society, rule of law, accountability), as it impacts (i) on the performance of the
bureaucracy in the design and implementation of effective economic and social development policies, and (ii)
on its commitment to deliver a high level of quality in public service. The environment for civil service
reform in Cape Verde was thoroughly assessed (see Annex 4) and the results point conclusively to an
auspicious environment for the implementation of reforms of a technical nature that would improve the
operation of Cape Verde's public administration. The assessment found that on-the-job training, and a more
flexible and dynamic human resource management system, were two areas of improvement most likely to
yield significant results in the short and long term.
2.    Modernization of Human Resource Management
2.17 The system and techniques of public personnel management in Cape Verde are fairly advanced by
Sub-Saharan Africa standards. Nevertheless, they are essentily instruments of control, better suited to
administring  and moitoring compliance with rules and procedures than to developing, managing and
deploying human resources efficiently and productively. To achieve a better fit with the State's human
resource needs, the modernization of human resource management in the Civil Service is now high on the
reform agenda: the creation of a framework for private-sector-led growth and investment, the making of
sound public investments in infrastructure (with private partners where possible), and the cost- and quality-
conscious delivery of basic service; all require significant changes in the ways public personnel are managed.
2.18  Several of these choges have already been made over the past two years (see Box 2). In a signal
of the premium the Government puts on human resource management, this function, previously handled by
a Secretary of State, was upgraded to cabinet rank, with the creation of a Amiistry of Public Adminison.
A computerized personnel information system, linked to the payroll system in the Ministry of Finance, was
greatly improved. Two decrees issued in July 1992 substantiaUy revised the basic rules of civil service
- 7-



employment, making them simpler, more flexible and transparent (Decreto-Lel No. 86/92 and 87192, July
16, 1992). The pay, classification and grading system was revamped, with fewer classification categories
and some decompression of the pay stucture. An examination system regulating between-rade promotions
was introduced. Recruitment rules and career streams for technical, administrative and managerial staff were
clearly specified, and new mechanisms to promote horizontal and vertical mobiliby of persomel were
developed. Notwithstanding these Impressive changes, much remains to be done. Recruitment and training,
and organizational planning and career management, need to be strengthened to attract and retain a sufficient
pool of qualified personnel, and to manage and deploy more efficiently existing human resources.

2.21   Peoe  adZin Personnel evaluation of a sort is reguarly and transparenty conducted in the
civil service. Standard evaluation forms including well documented criteria are used, and the results are made
aailable to the evaluee, who has a right to appeal to the level immediately above his/her supervisor in case
of disagreement. A mininmum Ievel of performance, as reported in the evaluation system, is required for
promotion to a higher step within the same grade. Promotion from one grade to another rests on the results
of standard tests administeredt within each Ministry. The present evaluation system, however, is primarily
designed to assess work attitudes (e.g., discipline and assiduity), rather thian results achieved, and the
strengths and weakonesses of a particular incumbent in a given position. As a result, it does not provide the
kind of infionmation required for career planning and the identification of individual training needs.

flow of information is lacking. The institutional framework for managing statistics needs restructing and
streamlining and, the mountain of backlog of statistical work needs to be organized and processed. Among
other things, actions are required to: (i) restructure the statistics department to provide it greater auttomy
so that it can, among other things, attract and retain qualified staff and take decisions quicldy; (ii) organize
and publish statistics regularly; (iii) build up operational statistics in such areas as commerce, housing,
employment, etc.; (iv) review the basis and methodology of the national accounts given the implications for
the level and condition of external assistance; (v) develop demographic and social statistics; and (vi) train
statistics and other government staff.
2.24 Plpming and Conununications. In the area of economic planning, the systems for investment analysis
and planning, macro-economic modelling and forecasting need to be strengthened. The weaknesses are quite
evident in existing documents such as the National Development Plan and the Public Investment Program
(PIP). The capacity to make policy is also hampered by communications weaknesses at two levels: intra-
governmental and between the government and the public at large. At the intra-governmental level, rapid
communications are of essence in a fast changing world. They can be accelerated in two ways: electronic
communications of information among key personnel in the government; and the development of a shared
data base among ministries that would ensure consistency and rapid flows of data across Government. The
development of a shared data base will be a long and painstaking activity, but well worth the effort. As for
improved communications between government and society, with the aim of increasing participation of society
at large in decision-making, the issues are discussed in Sections I (C) and (D) below.
4.    Municipal DevelopmentlLocl Government Capacity
2.25   Cape Verde's decentralization efforts are consistent with its overall economic reform program. An
important feature of that broader policy framework are principles concerning the changing roles and
responsibilities of the Government and the expanding role of the private sector, both in the sense of private
commercial entrepreneurship and voluntary community initiatives. This requires the public sector to limit
and reorient its role to: (i) establishing appropriate policies and regulations for the efficient and effective
operation of market mechanisms; (ii) developing human resources; and (iii) facilitating the construction and
miaintenance of appropriate physical infrastructure and facilitating the provision of appropriate (i.e. demand-
driven and quality) services. Cape Verde's decentralization policy is a key element of this new approach.
Its objectives are essentially four-fold: (i) to facilitate access of all Cape Verdeans to political and economic
decision-makers; (ii) to increase direct popular participation and control in the design and implementation of
programs that meet basic needs (e.g., health and sanitation, urban infrastructure, and socW services); (iii)
to deploy resources more efficiently, through a better distribution of tasks between central and local
governments, and facilitation of local private provision of public services; and (iv) to improve manageria and
administrative efficiency by developing the capacity to respond more rapidly and flexibly to unique
circumstances of local communities.
2.26 Following the Presidential and Legislative elections of 1990, the Government enacted Decree-Law
n° 52-A/90 of July 4, 1990 (Legislaao Autirquica). which built upon the framework for decentralization
defined in the Municipal Basic Law passed in 1989 (Law n° 47A/189), but never actually implemented.
Democratic municipal elections were then held for the first time since independence on December 15, 1991.
The elections created Municipal Assemblies (Assembleia Municipal) as the legislative branch of local
government, and Municipal Council (Clmara Municipal) headed by a Mayor (Presidente da Chmara
Municipal), as the executive branch of local government. The whole process elicited an enthusiastic response
from the population.
2.27   Since the elections, the Mayors and Assemblies have risen to the challenge of providing the local
population with a decent array of services with only limited revenues, and critIcal shortages of skilled
- 10-



persoe and equipment. However, expectations for the delivery of a wider range, of higher quaity services
to the local population are rising. The resources of the nmnicipalities (Box 4), as well as their managemet
capabilities, urgendy need to be improved to ensure the success of Cape Verde's pursuit of grass-roots
pardcipatory democracy.
2.28  While Decree-Law 52-A/90 establishes the legal basis for decentralization, the actual transfer of
responsibilities and powers from the central government needs to be phased in gradually to ensure an orderly
and effective process, with due regard to the gradual build-up of the fincial, human and managerial capacity
of the Municipalities to face their new responsibilities. In particular, building up their capacity to employ
sound economic and financial criteria in the selection and implementation of activities, and the management
and control of revenues and expenditures, will require time and signfficant investmerts in training, tehnical
assistance and equipment. On behalf of the GOCV, the Ministry of Internal Administration (MIA) is charged
with assisting the local and central government units with the organization and transfer of functions and
resources from decentralized agencies of central Ministries to the Municipalities. It is also responsible for
monitoring the timely implementation of decentralization.

S.    Legal and Judidal Context
(a)    Legislative Texts
2.29  Cmmerciat Code. Inherited from the Portuguese colonial era, the Commercial Code dates back to
the 19th century. By all accounts, it is totally outdated and ill-suited to serve the needs of modern-day
business activities. The law governing companies is also contained in the Code and has hardly evolved during
the past cent. Ihe Code needs to be revised as a matter of urgency; so do the procedures that it embodies
with regard to the establishent of companies.
2.30  Labor L&Wslgha1jw   Labor legislation is of more recent origin but, like the Commercial Code, it Is
considered to be outdated. It was conceived in an era when concer for workers' rights far outweighed any
desire for growth and development. Like the Commercial Code, the existing labor legislation is regarded
as a potential obstacle to development. The legislation as a whole is complex and ill-understood; so are the
provisions thereof which relate to dismissal and severance pay. An intermediate revision of the Code has
recendy been concluded. The reform has removed unduly restrictive provisions governing such mate  as
fixed-term contracts and part-dme employment.

economic development. The Code of Civil Procedure is relatively old and outdated, as are the procedures
that it precribes. The legislative provisions govening notarlal acts, as well as those relating to the
companies' and propert registers, are unduly complex and rigid, and have the effect of delaying and
complicating commercial and business transactions.
(b)    Ilentiidopn
2.32   The Ministry of Justice shares responsibility for administeing the justice system  with the
Procrador-GeraI, or PG's Office, and the tribunals. The Ministry of Justice consists of four main
Directorates, including one Directorate which is responsible for studies, legislative drafting, documentation
and information (DGELDI) and another which is responsible for notarial services and companies, property
and other registries (DGRNI). The other two Directorates, which are responsible for judicial affairs and
prison services, respectively, are not relevant for present purposes and are not discussed any fiurher herein.
2.33   DGELDI. DGELDI has overall responsibility to underae legal reform, and to provide advice and
guidance generally to all other ministries on legislative proposals. In practice, for lack of adequate capacity,
it has never been in a position to perform this role. It is currently headed by a Director-General, who is
assisted by a couple of junior lawyers, neither of whom has any particular expertise in the legislative drafting
process, for which DGELDI was created in the first place.
2.34   Most of the legal officers of the Ministry of Justice are degee-holders, without post-graduate
professional qualifications. Accordigly, their status Is somewhat lower than that of magistrates; so ^re their
wages and benefts. Due to budgetary constraints, they operate in tight and difficult conditions. They are
poorly paid and in-equipped to perform the critical responsibilities that are vested in them. Thus, it has
proved more and more difficult to retain staff in the Directorate on a regular basis. Most of them simply
leave for better and more lucrative opportunities or continue only on a part-ime or consultancy basis.
2.35   DORMI. The DGRNI has exclusive responsibility for providing notarial services to the public at
large. It authenticates business traons, including those relating to the incorporation of companies. The
Directorate doubles up as the companies' and property register. It is also responsible for civil status acts and
for the provision of personal identification cards.
2.36   Because DGRNI performs all its services manually, it consutes a major bottleneck for business
transactions. Nothtanding the brave atempts by Its personnel to expedite the processing of transactions,
unavoidably, there are delays both in the provision of notadal services and in the subsequent process of
registration. Irrespective of any attempt to streamline the process of establishment of companies, there is a
clear need to computerize the system and, thereby, achieve substantial gains in the processing time for
business activities.
2.37 Tribunals. Tne courts' system consists of an appellate jurisdicion and a jurisdiction of first instance.
The Supreme Court exercises appellate jurisdiction, whilst first instance jurisdiction belongs by and large to
the regional and sub-regionra courts. In Praa, as in Sao Vicente and Santa Catarina, there are separate
judges for civil and cdmia cases. In all other courts of first instance, the same judge is competent to hear
and dispose of all cases, civil and criminal alike. There is presenty no speciaized jurisdiction for
commercial or industrial cases.
2.38   Judges as a rle are not properly trained, particularly for commercial and business law cases. Ihey
are poorly paid and generally iU-equipped to perform their functions. Like other Government employees,
they have experienced the harsh realities of budget cuts. Although their conditions are marginally better than
- 13-



those of legal officers of the Ministry of Justice, many continue to leave for the bar and other more lucrative
oppormuities.
6.     Management of Public Assets and Public Procurement
2.39   A recent review comraissioned by the GOCV found that the basic legislation govening public
procurement and the system for the acquisition of goods and services by various public organizations, are
long overdue for rationalization and streamlining. The problems can be grouped into three broad areas:
legislation, skills training, and functioning of the system. Tne legislation governing the procurement system
is out of date in some areas and increasingly unsuited to Cape Verde's curent realities. The system includes
nine principal laws, one dating back to 1901 (Royal Order), another to 1932 and so on. The last of the laws
dates back to 1977. Several factors have complicated the application of these laws by the purchasing
departments, namely: (i) the fragmented nature of the regulations governing procurement and inadequate
knowledge or even total ignorance of them by a considerable number of officials who should be applying
them; (Ii) threshoads in terms of amounts for contracts, for which signature of very senior officials is
required, have become quite derisory; (iii) the authorities or decision-making bodies referred to in the laws
have completely changed (with independence, etc); (iv) there are omissions in important areas that are
covered by modern legislation, relating to the new requirements imposed by the nature of the modern
equipment that the public administration is now using; and (v) procedures are excessively bureaucratic and
completely out of line with the momentum of the economic changes in recent years.
2.40  The General Directorates of Administration in various ministries purchase the goods and services
required for their departmental operations. The General Directorate of the Budget in the Ministry of Finance
verifies the legality of the expenditure and its conformity with budget rules. Specifically, its task is to check,
sete and authorize payment and to record it in the accounts of the State. In reality, though, the principle
of competition is not scrupulously observed. Tk3 general practice is simply to seek a quotation from the
market, without any recourse to procedures that would ensure a transparent process and give the State, as
purchaser, the benefits of competition. There is also no effective central planning, rationalization or
coordination of government purchases, although significant benefits could be obtained from centralization and
grouping of some acquisitions into lots, and from better departmental coordination. The potential gains are
not insignificant in the 1993 budget year, with reference only to outlays contained in the operating budget,
expenditure on goods and services is projected at US$7 million.
C. CONSTRAINTS AND OPPORTUNlTlES IN THE
MODERNIZATION OF THE PUBLIC SECTOR
1.    Introduction
A.41 The reform of the public sector will be challenging: There are three potential constraints that conld
slow down implementation: (i) resistance of the civil servants to change their entrenched ways of working;
(ii) insufficient capacity of local institutions to train civil servants to improve their skills; and (iii) inadequate
coordination in implementing the reform. At the same time, there are several opportunities present in the
country that make success likely. First, the need for reform has been debated extensively and intensively in
parliament and at the polls. ITere is a strong sense of purpose and agreement among the key actors in the
Government and Parliament that the reform must be effectively implemented. Both the public and private
press have opened a wide discussion about various aspects of the reform which is helping to shape public
opinion in a constructive and positive way. Third, there is a strong feeling in the municipalities that greater
autonomy in fiscal and financial affairs will be good for the people and for the economic development of the
different regions of the country. FinaRly, the Government has not shied away from implementing tough
reorm measures: public enterprise restructuring; stabilization of public finances; imposing for the first time
income taxes on civil servants.
- 14-



2.    Constraints
2.42   Resistance to Change. Generally speaking, civil servants are sfill in a reflective mood, digesting and
discussing changes the reforms may bring to work practices. This discussion is taking place in the context
of the longstanding tradition and good reputation of the civil service. Nevertheless, cvil servants are well
organized and have active unions to represent their interest. It is therefore possible that the civil service as
a whole or in groups may decide to resist the reform.
2.43   At this time, however, the general consensus view in Govemment and among higher-level civil
servants is that the majority of civil servants will not resist the reform or attempt to block it in an organizeC
way. The reason for this assumption is that civil servants of Cape Verde have a special sense of duty and
esprit de corps* that stiffen their will to face difficult times and make the best of them. Also, the public
debate that is going on has helped to clarify the necessity of the reform and has convinced most civil servants
that implementation will be transparent and fair. Moreover, Cape Verdean society has in the past been very
receptive to modernizadon, which is the centerpiece of the reform, and recognized that there is a "price to
pay' for modernizing the public sector. Finally, the majority of the civil servants know that they wi1l be
given choices to stay, transfer or leave the services. Ihey know that they will get training and possibly new
tools such as computers which will improve their capacity and may make their work easier and more
interesting.
2.44   Limited Capacity for Training. One of the premises of the reform program is that the capacity of
civil servants can be improved through a systematic, targeted, demanddriven training program linked to
improved systems for management of human resources (career development, promotions, salaries, etc). Ite
Government has prepared a training program for the core staff of each of the thirteen ministries, specifically
targeted at creating the skills required by the managers, supervisors and administrative staff. An estimwated
800 civil servants are proposed to be trained over a four year period. The program has been appraised and
the evaluation suggests that: (a) if a number of the existing centers can be strengthened; (b) the private
(proprietary) training centers can be utilized to execute some of the training; and (c) the Government would
agree to extend incentives to civil servants who enroll in training, then the effort to Improve capacity through
rainig is likely to succeed. The Government has taken measures to meet these conditions.
2.45 Coordinating Capacity. The reform program is comprehensive, it includes many agencies of the
Government, all the municipalities, the judiciary and the press. The Government has proposed a simple
system for the implementation and coordination of the reforms supported by the project where the Ministry
of Economic Coordination (MEC) would establish a coordinating unit and each affected agency would have
a small task force responsible for implementation of its respective component. The unit at MEC would
coordinate with the task frces in a regular and systematic mane through bi-weeldy meetings and special
reviews if necessary. This system for coordination and implementation would be adequate if the various
agencies have suitable tools, such as computer-based planning and monitoring programs available and the staff
will have been trained to use such software effectively. Adequate software is now available and provisions
will be made in the project for the introduction of this monitoring 8-,ftware. In addition, a high-level advisory
Board has been established to oversee the pace and direction of reform implementation.
3.    Opportunities
2.46 Public MMpprt. The timing and environment for the reform is opportune. The Government has
launched a steady campaign of informing the people of impending changes that are necessary to address the
difficult economic and social issues facing the county. Support for the ideas have been tested at the polls
in recent elections. The public debate about the Government's program has reached the point where
constiuencies are now asking for actions and results at speeds th  may be unrealistic. One of the first
actions the Government has embarked upon is the privatiaon of public enterprises. Despite some painfol
- 15-



short-term consequences such as the possible retrenchment of managers and workers, the general public has
accepted the need for the privadzation program. The reform of the public sector proper, is a logical
complement to the privatization program and the decision of the Goverment to go ahead with it at thi tm
is opportune and is already receiving the support of the general public. The reform program includes a
number of measures that respond to the demand of Leveral constltuentgroups (i.e., local business community,
municipalities, journalists, core staff In civil service and NGOs) in the country. In particuar, there is the
strong sUpport for the municipal development proposals, for the press renewal program and for the suggested
Improvements of the legal and judicial environment.
2.47  Building the Capact  he Press. The proposed assistance to the press is responding to the demand
of journaists and readers for better information and more openness and accountability by government. The
press Is struggling with very old, obsolete equipment and primitive facilities but has found a willing audience.
People are willing and ready to pa; for newspapers and the press in general is keen to work to improve their
services. The window of opportunity to respond to this demand is now open but journaists and readers will
lose interest and become disillusioned and dissatisfied if the Govermnent does not deliver the promised
support.
2.48   SuM= ftm Business. Finally, the business community is increasingly ready to play a larger role
in the country but feels handicapped by the archaic business law system inherited from Portugal. They
applaud the initiative by the Government to review the legal environment for business and are ready to work
with the legal professionals and the courts to simplify the system. Cape Verde is fortunte in that it has a
pool of well educated law professionals although their experience and experdse in business law is limited.
I the Government succeeds to mobilize the legal specialists, and finds a way to systematicaUy consult with
a broad section of the business and legal communities (as it is now doing) then the -. Ann process will be
implemented quickly and effectively.
D. GOVERNMENT'S STRATEGY AND REFORM EFFORTS
2.49   Overall Strate   . Government's overall economic reform program is aimed at rnforming the
economy from a public sector-dominated to an outward-looking economy, in which private investments in
telecommunications, transhipment, tourism, offshore banking, and light manufacuring for exports, are
expected to gradually increase and eventually lead economic growth. The Government's strategy postulates
fundamental shifts in policies and institutions and confronts squarely Cape Verde's extreme resource poverty
and skewed economy. It builds on Cape Verde's stability and is underpinned by a program truly owned
by Cape Verde. The public sector reform and capacity building program translates that strategy into a series
of inter-connected activities (Annexes 1 and 6) designed to help bring about tha economic and soci
transfornmation of the country.
2.50 The Government realizes gmat to ensure the sustainability of stabilization measures introduced in 1991
including curbs on credit to public enterprises, controls on budgetary expenditures, maintenance of positive
interest rates and mobilization of higher tax revenues, it needs to improve the capacity of the public sector
in economic and fiscal management. The same is true for the implementation and susinabiity of structural
reforms such as trade liberalization, simplification of the tax system, removal of state monopolies in services
and commerce, price liberalization and legal and regulatory reform. Success and sustainability however, also
require securing the support of various constituencies and the people at large. The Government recognizes
this as demonstrated by its efforts to strengthen the press and involve municipalities and private sector in the
decision-making process.
2.51 Under the public sector reform program, the objective of improving economic and fiscal management,
moderning public administration and the justice system, will be pursued through complementary top-down
and bottom-up actions: promotion of development at the grasroots level; 9Ad broad-based paricipation of
- 16-



constituencies and the population in the reform. The linkages between the actions and the strategy are
evidenced by the Govemment's plans for the proposed project and are discussed below. The Government's
objectives and strategy for public sector reform were published in the Official Gazette soon after it took office
in 1991 (Resolucao No. 611V191 in Boletim Oficial No. 31 of August 8, 1991). A policy statement on public
sector reform, is found In Annex 1.
2.52   jarigj  Coacft and Broadening Democdzaon. The effective implementation of the economic
transformation program requires improvement of the capacity of the public administration in economic and
fiscal management and inprovement of the legal and judicial environment. To generate public support for
the reforms requires broadening the democratization process through delegating more power and
responsibilities to the regional municipal governments and strengthening the capacity of the press to inform
the public about government policies.
2.53 Tmproving the Capacity of the &blIc Administration. ITe weaknesses in public administration
became quite apparent in the face of a deteriorating economic performance, declining public investments and
demand for maritime and transport services, and worsening performance of the public sector enterprises. To
address these problems, the Government has begun to undertake a comprehensive program to improve the
quality of its economic and fiscal management, the efficiency of public administration and the privatization
of public sector enterprises. Specifically, the Government has embarked on a program to reform the
ministries responsible for economic and fiscal planning and management and has created a new ministry to
oversee and coordinate economic policy and development, the Minisry of Economic Coordination. It has
alo reorganized the Ministry of Fmance and is in the process of strengthening the capacity of the public
fnance, budgeting and internal auditing departments of that ministry. In addition, the Government has
prepared a plan to modernize the sectoral ministries through a reform of the civil service, improving
personnel management and training civil servants to be Implemented under the proposed project.
2.54 The Legal Environment for Private Sector Development. To facilitate and promote private sector
nvestment and growth in general, the Government has also embarked on a reform of the legal environment
for business. A detailed operationral review of the legal environment for business has been completed and
an action plan recommended. The reform will include furter streamlining of labor legisla,on, and
simplification of legislation on entry and exit of businesses. 'Te development of the private sector will be
further assisted by the establishment of a competitive commercial banking system and expansion of the market
for trading in treasury notes, and eventually stocks and bonds.
2.55 Sup=ort for the Reform Program. The planning and execution of the reform program requires the
support and commitment of the political parties, parliament, civil servaus, political and other constituencies
and the public. To this end, the Government has begun a program of streamining relations with the press
for the purpose of reaching a wider audience for the information it is making available about the reform
program and facilitating public discussion and scrutiny from constituencies and the public at large. Plans to
assist the printed (newspapers) and electronics press (radio and television) in geuing access to Government
officials and information, acquire better equipment and modern prinng and broadcasting technologies are
included in the Government's reform program.
2.56   Strengtbening the Role of Ijnicipaljimt. At the core of the Government's effort to build local
capacity is a program for decentralization of Government functions and services to the municipalities, which
has been prepared by the Ministry of Internal Administration. It calls for more equitable distribution of
resources to municipalities on the basis of transparent criteria. It also calls for a greater role of the
municipalities in the development and maintenance of utilkles and other infrastructure and the provision of
soc  services. As for strengthening the municipalities, the Government has already identified the needs to
be supported under the proposed project. It has reviewed key issues such as revenue sharing and the
allocation of expenditure responsibilities in accordance with capacity. Several of the larger municipalities
- 17-



have prepared comprehensive proposals regarding their new roles and the resources and capacity building that
are required to take on these roles. The proposals have been assessed and many are considered sound. Plans
for the allocation of resources, and recruitment and training of municipal staff will be supported under the
proposed project.
2.57   Two issues are of partiular concern to the Government, as it prepares to reinforce the process of
decentralization and the transformation of the role of the municipalities: (i) relations between the
municipalities and the central administration; and (ii) relations between the municipalities and the various
groups and constituencies in the communities. For that reason the decentralization plan contains proposals
for the development of a common set of procedures for fiscal and financial functions, and for the provision
of social services, to ensure an efficient exchange of information, and adequate monitoring and auditing. At
the same time, more autonomy for the municipalities must be accompanied by greater responsiveness to local
political forces, if the needs of constituents and local communities are to be met. The Government believes
that regiona economic and social development must -be driven by grass root level moveiments and public
participation in the identification of priorities and the formulation of social and economic policies and
programs. The Government is therefore encouraging the municipalities to foster the development of local
associations and organizations as a means of generating community self-help programs and defining local
projects that cater to the needs of the poor and less privileged groups in the society, particularly women and
youth. Assistance will be provided under the proposed project in support of those efforts.
E. IDA INVOLVEMENT
2.58   Lessons    ed. In addition to the Privatization TA project (Cr. 2377-CV), which has performed
very well to date, IDA has supported other public sector reforms (including public enterprises) with good
results. Assistance to the ENAPOR (Cr. 1322-CV), ELECTRA and TRANSCOR (Cr. 1954-CV) enterprises
produced significant improvements in their quality of service and overall operational efficiency and
performance. One of the keys to success so far of the Privatization TA project has been the active role
played by an independent advisory Board. A similar Board has been established under the proposed project.
The proposed project is in response to the commitment displayed by the Government to undertake a major
institutional transformation in terms of the roles of, and interactions within the State and the private sector
which ensures ownership. The environment for carying out the reforms is conducive. Finally, experience
from similar operations has shown that advisory services are much more effective when provided in
conjunction with a reform program that has already been launched and to which the Government is fully
committed.
2.59   Rationale. In the past, the Government relied to a large extent on bilateral assistance, but did litte
to coordinate the assistance of donors. Moreover, much of the assistance did not address institutional
weaknesses. The Govermnent and the donors are aware of these shortcomings. With the encouragement of
the donors, it has prepared the institutional development program and deliberately sought IDA's assistance
to implement the programs. The proposed project follows up on and complements the Privatization TA
project, which has helped lay some of the foundations for the project through studies such as the one on the
legal business environment. The project would also complement other ongoing or planned IDA-supported
projects in the infrastructure, financial, and social sectors, which together with the reform of the public sector
(mcluding public enterprises), are the pillars of the Bank's assistance strategy for Cape Verde. Those projects
all ain at assisting the GOCV to create the conditions for the private sector to become the engine of
sugtainable economic growth and to improve the well-being of its citizenry. Capacity building in public
adminitration, both at the central and local levels, was identfied in the recent Country Economic
Memorandum (June 1993) and Country Assistance Strategy paper (March 1993) as the necessary keystone
in the consolidation and acceleration of past and present reforms, and in the passage to a new phase of
economic and social development. The CEM identified the severe constraints to economic management
bescase of a weak analytical base which also resulted in hardened terms for assistance by several donors.
- 18-



Finally, as a privileged partner in Cape Verde's economic restructuring program, and one that recognizes that
the sustainabiity of reform depends on the capacity of implementation, IDA is well positioned to extend its
dWogue to the medium- and long-term process of modemizing the public sector's management of the
economy through institutional dc ielopment and capacity building.
m.   THfE PROJEI            
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES, SCOPE AND DESCRIPTION
1. Program and Project Objectives
3.1    Effective economic management and the transformation of any economy and society in the direction
proposed by Cape Verde not only requires good policy, but strong institutions, an appropriate legal and
judicial framework, a sound analytical base, and skilled individuals to implement policies and to communicate
them throughout the administration and to the public at large. The principal objective of the actions to be
financed by the proposed IDA credit is to support the Government's broader program to transform and
modernize key public institutions, the laws supporting them, and the personnel staffing them, thereby helping
sustain the economic and social reform program which the Government initiated. As outlined in the Policy
Letter (Annex 1), parts of this larger program are already underway or are being considered for support
through coordinated financing from a number of other bilateral and multilateral donors. Specifically, the
project, prepared on the basis of extensive diagnostic assessment of institutional capacity undertaken mostly
by GOCV, with some support from donors and the Bank, would support improvements in economic policy
analysis and management, and in the legal, judicial and administrative environment for investment and for
business operations through a program of institutional development and capacity building in public
administration, both at the central and municipal levels.
2. Project Description
3.2 Introduction and Summary of Features. The proposed credit will finance a five-year time slice
consisting of selected components of the program. Actions to be financed by the IDA credit are limited to
those which can be effectively completed within five years, and which will result in lasting improvements in
key areas of civil service performance, the administrative and legal environment for investment and business
transactions, and the capacity of the grassroots level to sustain development. To this end, the project aims
to increase effectiveness, efficiency, and transparency in public sector functions that are critical to the long-
term success of the overall economic and social reform program. Global program targets and activities are
in Annex 9-B. Annex w-A contains monitoring and performance indicators for the proposed project, fully
covering the first three years (as needed, additional targets would be set for the last two years during the
project mid-term review). Agreement was reached on these during nggtjg1iQna. Specifically, the credit will
finance five components:
a) Modernization of the Civil Service (US$.80 million). The credit will finance short-term advisory
services, studies, computers and software to modernize human resources management by the
Ministry of Public Administration (MPA); training in critical skill areas organized and
coordinated by MPA for line ministry staff; and retraining and redeployment services also
managed by MPA, for participants in civil service's Voluntary Departure Progrm;
b) Building Local Capacity to Participate and to Manage (Decentralization and Municipal
Strenathening} (USS2.26 million). Through financing for short-term advisory services and
operational studies, equipment, and training for all 15 municipalities and (where appropriate), the
- 19-



Ministy of Inteal Admnstration, the credit will assist the Govertment in implementing its
decentralization policy, i strengtenig the municipalities' capacity to mne eonomic and
social activities, and in involving the populaion more in the development process;
c) Strengthening Economic Management Capacit (US$2.15 million). The credit will finace
operational studies, public information campaigns, advisory services, training, equipment, and
library support for the Ministries of Economic Coordination, Finance, and Communications and
their major agencies (Statistics, Planning, Public Finance, Radio, etc.), as well as the private
media. It will assist GOCV with the organizadon and gradual processing of the mountain of
backlog of statistical work, the restructuring of the national accounts and statistics system, and
the transformation of the statistics department into an Institute of Statistics with sufficient
autonomy. A long-term adviser will be provided to help with the transformation. In addition it
will support the development of an electronic communications system within the GOCV, as well
as an action plan for a shared data base for government operations. It would help establish a pre-
investment studies fund (PIF) to relieve present constrain  on the lack of properly prepared
investment projects. This fund would support the final preparation of the National Environmental
Action Plan (NEAP). Support will also be provided to strengthen the Public Finance Department
which is responsible for extnal debt, public finance management, design of instruments for
public resairce mobilization, and aid coordination. Options for the greater involvement of non-
resident Cape Verdeans in the development process of the country would be reviewed and
supported by this component;
d) Leal and Judicial Reform and Modemization of the Justice System (USS1.98 million). Support
will include financing training, equipment, and materials, essential publications and printing, and
advisory services for the Mfmistry of Justice and Labor and its major agencies including the
Judiciary, the Registrar of Companies, and the Registrar of Deeds. Ihe component will help
strengthen legal services for key ministries and such actions as improved commercial and labor
legislation, training in business law for magistrates, and establishment of arbitration facilities to
dispose of commercial law disputes; and
e) Public Procurement Reform (US$0.38 million). This componen wil finance training for about
40 staff short-term advisory services and equipment to help modernize the Public Procurement
and Assets Management Department (PATRIMONIO) and selected Departments of Administrative
Services in the ministries. Selective revision of legislation on procurement will be undertaken
and instittional procedures to improve the public procurement process will be streamlined and
strengthened.
Sample terms of reference for advisory services and studies have been prepared and are in Annexes 4, 5 and
8. Understandings were reached on these during negotiato. A key design aspect of the project is that most
advisory services will be short-term, with long-term resident assistance strictly limited to activities such as
the establishment of the National Institte of Statistics (see component (c) above).
3. Detailed Project Features
3.3   The project is described in more detail below. Further and in-depth discussion of the major
components are in Annexes 4, 5, 7 and 8. Main activities, expected impacts and timing targets are detailed
in matrix form in Annex 9.
