(a)Id'- MogMeion of the Civil Serv.
(i) Human Resources Mement. The Goverment has identified orgiMin and Mw Wo
,lnnn as the most critical area to be addressed under the project, as the changing role of the State
requires a significant adaptation of the civil service. Accordingly, a medtodology and the instruments
conducive to quantitative and qualitative analysis of the present and forecasted demand and supply
of personnel in public administration are essential to provide the data required for the development
of comprehensive strategies and action-plans in the areas of recruitment, training, productivity
improvement, career and succession plans, redeployment and altemative means of delivering services.
Short-term advisory services from personnel management specialists will be financed under the
project to: (a) assist the Ministry of Public Administration with the design and/or adaptation of
instruments and methods for organization design, human resources evaluation, career and succession
planming, and training needs assessments; (b) recommend the selection of one of the many well-
designed software packages which exist on the market to manage orgaization and human resource
planning and control; (c) develop an action-plan for the implementation of the new Instruments and
methods; and (d) provide in-house training in the use of the new instruments for the staff of the
Ministry of Public Administration and for personnel officers in the Ministries and other Government
agencies. Recipients of this training would, in turn, train supervisors and managers throughout the
central government;
(ii)   Civil Sevice Training. Civil servants enjoy a good reputation dating back to the colonial
period, when Cape Verdeans were frequentiy seconded to administrative positions in various
Portuguese colonies. However, the efficiency and effectiveness of the civil service today is
constrained by a shortage of administrative, technical, professional and managerial skills. Also,
unmanaged growth in government employment in the 1985-90 period diluted the average skill level
of civil servants. Under the civil service modernization component, an assessment was made of the
technical and professional capacity of civil servants in six ministries: Ministies of Education and
Sports; Fishery, Agriculture and Rural Development; Health; Infrastructure and Transport; Public
Administration; and Tourism, Industry and Commerce. The level of technical and professional skills
is either relatively low or in short supply in these ministries (Annex 4). The Govermnent has begun
to address the need for mid- and higher-level relevant professional skills. The skills development and
uaining is being done in the context of foreign-aided development projects executed by the ministries.
For example, staff of the  imisry of Education and Sports are receiving training in education-specific
skills under the Bank-supported Primary Education Upgrading Project (Cr. 1853-CV). ITis trining
wiUl be continued and expanded in the proposed BET project. Similarly, the management staff of the
Ministry of Infrastructure and Transport will receive training in relevant skills under the Transport
and infrastructure Project (Cr.2466-CV). The staff of the Labor Department of the Ministy of
Justice and Labor will also be receiving professional training under the BET project. In addition, the
Ministries of Public Administrion and Internal Administration have on-going arrangements with
their sister ministries in Portugal for professional staff training. Under the proposed project,
technical and professional tMning for the staff of the Ministries of Finance, Economic Coordination,
Communications, Justice, municipalities, etc wfll be supported under the respective components.
Under the civil service modernization component, training wil be provided for three levels of
administrative staff in the six line ministries mentioned above and possibly others as the need is
letermined: managers, spervisors and administrative support personnel. The training plan (Annex
9) contains training in communications and computer-related skills for administrative support staff
and first-line supervisors, and training in project management, budget and accounting and personel
management for supervisors and managers. Most of the traiing would be done locally at the Center
for Administrive Training and Development (CENFA), which was established in 1984 for the
purpose of raising the administrative capacity of civil servants. CENFA already offers a set of basic
administatve training programs, taught by civil servants, but the Government proposes to adjust the
- 21 -



program offerings to tailor them better to the skill deficiencies of administrative and managemet
staff. 'Thus, to strengthen the administrative and management skills and capacity of civil servants In
the participadng line ministries and possibly others, the training, to be designed and implemented with
assistance under this component, would include:
(a) for the Ministry of Education and Sports, five local training programs for: 18 managers and
supervisors in planning, budgeting and accounting and word processing; eight administators
and inspectors in informatics and supervision; and, for 36 support staff in building
maintenance;
(b) for the Ministry of Fishery, Agriculture and Rural Development, local training programs for:
36 managers in planning, HRD and project management; 48 supervisors in informatics and
management of agriculture and public works projects; and, 180 administrative staff in
informatics, communications, and accounting;
(c) for the Ministry of Health, local training for: 24 managers in budgeting, HRD and Project
management and a seminar in Policies and financing of National Health programs; 84
supervisors in maintenance, and accounting; and, 48 administrators in informatics, and
communications;
(d) for the Miistry of Infrastructure and Transport local training for 24 administrative staff in

(e) for the Ministry of Public Administration local training for: 4 managers in HRI) and Project
Management; 8 supervisors in HRD Management, and public administration management;
and for 6 administrators in informatics, and communications; and
(t) for the Mimistry of Tourism, Industry and Commerce training for: 6 managers in Project and

Administration, and Communication Skills; and 12 administrators in inormatics, and
communications; and
Qii)   Retrenchment Program Support and Retraining. The Government has defined a civil
service retrenchment program. The program has oeen discussed by the Social Consultative Council
(made up of labor unions, employers and other interest groups), approved by the Council of Ministers
and the Parliament and publicly announced (Annex 9). The program was discussed during
negotiations and transmitted to IDA as a condition of presentation of the proposed Droject to hie
lBad. The program comprises: a voluntary departure program (VDP) aimed at reducing the mnmber
of lower-level staff in three categories of the civil service - auxiiar, operario, administrativo - by
about 1,600 Cn two phases); an early retirement program (ERP) aimed at about 400 civil servants;
and retraining and assistance to VDP participants to help them reintegrate into the formal and
informal sectors. Under the Government's plans, those who opt for the VDP would receive a lump
sm payment and monthly subsistence payments for up to three years (both totalling no more than
the one lump sup payment they would have received under traditional schemes). The proposed credit
would not provide funding for payments under the VDP and ERP. The Government has made its
budgetary allocations for the payments, Switzerland has allocated assistance and other key donors
have expressed interest in providing funding if the need arises. Under this component therefore
support would be provided: a) to finalize the details of the various Incentive packages under the VDP,
with a view to arriving at a final decision on least cost/best result combination; to review the poteal
supply of jobs exisdng in the formal and informal secors for the categories of personnel targeted
under the VDP; to explore the potential for creation by retrenched staff of small and micro-
-22 -



enterprises, and identify the type of assistance to be (ffered to facilitate their start-up (including
conting out services previously delivered by Government); to develop mechanisms for monitoring
and control of the VDP; and to assist any needed preparatory activities to mobilize additional donor
funding for the VDP; and b) to implement a comprehensive demand-driven retraining program
directed at VDP and ERP participants including: a primary education completion package for about
1,000 VDP participants; a lower-secondary education completion package for 300; a one-year
subsidized and on-the-job training package for about 300; and a business advisory program for
perhaps 200 VDPIERP participants who qualify. Various primary and lower-secondary educadon
completion programs are presely provided in Cape Verde by various public and private institutions
and these can be adapted to meet the needs of the VDP. The business advisory package would be
available to participants who are able to produce a business plan that meets minimum standards of
viability, affordability and sustainability. Participants whose business plans meet those standards
could elect to receive specified levels of assistance, support and training from business counselors -
some of who may be recruited on a voluntary basis from the business community and non-resident
Cape Verdeans (NRCV-see component C) - in three areas: marketing, basic finance and book-
keeping for small businesses. The Government intends to allow participants with qualifying business
plans to convert the monthly payment portion of their VDP payment package into a block payment
for the start-up of their businesses;
(b) Building Local Canacit (Decentralization and Municipal Strengthening).
This component covers several actions designed to help the municipalities to efficiently and effectively
discharge not only their present responsibilities but to execute the new ones foreseen in the
framework of the Constitution, which assigns to the municipalities responsibility for all that has to
do with local population needs. The project will provide support in the following four areas: a)
institution building; b) administration and management; c) human resources development and
management; and d) internal organizational strengthening.
(i)   Institution Building. This would include: a) further detailing, where necessary, guidelines
for the decentralization process (e.g. revenue sharing formulae, responsibility for maintenance of
certain public infrastructure, etc); b) preparation and implementation of action plans for the
establishment, for groups of municipalities, of inter-municipal technical support offices (rSOs), in
an effort to ensure a "critical mass' for sharing which will allow for a rationally and economicatly
sound use of scarce technical support and equipment; and c) equipment, materials and logistical
support for intra-municipal deconcentration, thereby facilitating closer contact between the municipal
authorities and the population of the various villages and/or citizens' groups, and local community
associations. A number of operational studies, for which terms of reference appear in Annex 5-D
will be prepared;
(ii) Administration and Management: Technical assistance to atl municipalities to: a) design
and implement planning, budgeting and inventory systems, to ensure transparency, accountability and
adequate monitoring of fiscal and related matters and effective expenditure management; and b)
evaluate options and develop local fiscal administration and revenue mobilization capacity, in order
to ensure the susinability of the decentralization process and of local services provision;
(iii) Human Resources Development and Management: a) technical assistance to qualitatively
and quantitatively identify necessary staff for each municipality and to design actions to help attract
and retain qualified personnel; b) preparation of a training needs assessment for each municipality;
c) design and implementaton of training sessions, within the framework of a comprehensive program,
for municipal staff, managers and elected officials and covering various areas (Annex 9). The
program wiUl provide about 150 people with training in such areas as management, organization,

training seminars for various interest groups, material and equipment support for the Documentation
and Information Center for Development (CDID) of the Minisbtry of Economic Coordination, etc.
Cognizat of the deficiencies in economic journalism, the Governmen has given high priority to
providing journalists from both the public and private media with better understanding of economic
subjects. Thus the project would help strengthen the understanding of those who analyze, intepret
and mold public opinion on issues of economic and social policy. About 40 journalists would receive
training under the project. Most of the training would be in the form of seminars organized locally
by specialists. Some training abroad could also be supported especially those organized in
colaboratin with other counties. Fmally, the project would assist with the provision of basic
computer equipment, msatals and communication eqipment to the public and private media;
(ii)   Plnning Support. The proposed project would provide support in a number of areas among
which: a) preparation of multi-year rolling public investment program (PIP). The rolling PIP is
expected to become one of the key instruments of aid coordination given Cape Verde's high
dependence on external aid for its investment projects; b) establishment of a pre-investment studies
fund (PIF) including the preparation of the legal insuments establishing the fund, development of
procedur  for its use, seed money to start-up the fund and the carrying out of priority studies under
the fund. Support for the final preparation of the NEAP will be provided under the PIP; c)
preparation of a planning manual adapted to Cape Verde's realities as part of efforts to develop local
capacity for strategic planning; d) training (both short and long term) for the staff of the Planning
- 24 -



Directorate General (DGP) as well as for staff of the various planning and studies offices In the
Ministries. A total of about 30 staff will receive training. The specific training activities are
described in Annex 8'A; and e) provision of equipment to the DGP including an integrated computer
system and training of the staff in the use of these computers.
(lii)   National Aounting and Statistia Systan Restruduring.  The success of the
Government's program to strengthen economic management through institutional development depend
very much on the availability and quality of economic, social and financial data. At presen, an
appreciable amount of economic and social data is either deficient or out of date. Also, much
information has been collected but these need to be organized, processed and analyzed. Much has
been accomplished in the last two years-the 1990 population and housing census, the 1988 NatIonal
Accounts, etc, but a lot remains to be done. Methodologies for processing the information for
National Accounts also need to be reviewed. These deficiencies have affected Cape Verde negatively
in other ways. Because of methodological problems national accounts have been overestimated
leading to hardening of terms by lenders and donors who have assumed Cape Verde to be richer than
it is. Cape Verde's donors have committed themselves to providing support for data collection,
management, analysis, and economic forecasting. France (technical assistance), the ADB (technical
assistance and some equipment) and the UNFPA (some training and tedhnical assistance) are
providing significant assistance in accordance with a long-term strategy and program developed by
the Government with the help of donors and shown in matrix form in Annex 9. MDA support would
complement these donor assistance and will cover five major areas: a) insttutional development to
strengthen the collection and management of economic and social data, the Government will
transform the General Directorate of Statistics (DOE) into an autonomous institution, the National
Institute of Statistics MNE), with the ability to recruit and retain qualified staff and providing
statistical services under contract to government departments and instituions. The proposed project
will support the creation of the INE (Annex 9-A); b) national accous: the organization ad gradual
processing of the mountain of backlog of National Accounts including the production of accounts for
1989-1992 and possibly those for the following years; and review of the basis and methodology of
national accounts including specific survey needs; c) devel== of demoghi and social stascs:
follow-up of UNFPA assistance including data analysis of the 1990 general census (oopulation and
housing); preparation of emigration and immigration statistics; environment statistics; and a sedes
of economic and poverty-focussed surveys as part of Government's efforts to get a better handle on
and deal with the social dimensions of adjustment; d) training: the design and implementation of a
training program in statistics for staff of DGE/INE and of the studies/research departments of the
various ministries and the Central Bank as well as the Planning Directorate of the Ministry of
Economic Coordination. Traning will include on-the-job, extenal short-term, extnal long-tm
and in country seminars and workshops; and e) equipment and materials to help with the training,
and the transformation of the DGE;
(iv)   Public lnaee Support. The project wil provide support in three areas: a) improed public
debt management including the computerization of the debt management system, and training of staff
involved in its monitoring; b) improvement in aid coordination including the development an
implementation of institutional procedures for aid management and execution (roles of the Ministries
of Economic Coordination, Finance (Treasury), Secretariat of State for Cooperation and the
beneficiary institutions); and c) public resource mobilization: as part of efforts to mobilize domestic
resources and savings to finance development activities, the project will assist with the expansion of
the market for treasury bills. At present these T-Bills are limited to institutions but initial studies
have shown hat the public, particularly emigrant savers, would be interested in these. An operational
study to assess the feasibility of this expansion will be undertaken and if feasible, the mechnisms and
market circuit for its implementation will be organized with assistance under the project;

(d) LIS  and Judicial Reform and Moderpization of Jusce SysteM.
Ihe Governmentes institudonal development and public sector modeion program focuss
broadly on the legal and judicial context in addition to emphasizing reforms which most directly affect
business and commercial activity. A coordinating committee, headed by the Director General of
Studies, Legislation and Information (DGELDI) at the Ministry of Justice, and consisting of Ministry
officials, judges, private legal practitioners and a non-resident Cape Verdean (NRCV) providing
assistance on a voluntary basis to the Ministry of Justice prepared the proposed legal and judicial
component. The preparation work benefitted from the active participation of the Minister of Justice
himself. Under the Privatization TA Project (Cr. 2377-CV), the Government commissioned a study
of the legal and judicial business environment in Cape Verde. The study examined the legal and
judicial framework for Cape Verde, from a business perspective, identified issues that need to be
addressed, and contributed to the groundwork for the preparation of the proposed project. As
discussed in more detail below, the proposed project component will finance: legislative reform and
institutional development for the Ministry of Justice; establishment of specialized courts;
modernization of the notary and registry system; judicial information strengthening; and professional
training and specialization for magistrates and other legal practitioners.
(1)    Legislative Reform. Four key pieces of legislation would be fully revised: a) Labor Code;
b) Commercial Code; c) Registry and Notarial legislation; and d) Civil Procedural legislation. These
laws all have an impact upon commercial and business activities in the country. Revision
(uintemediate revision) of the Labor Code is already underway with the first phase having been
cmpleted in December 1993. This was aimed at removing the most flagrant restrictions such as the
provisions on fixedterm employment and the procedures for employment and dismissal. ITe follow
up revisions would be supported under the proposed project. The Government has taken steps to
strengthen the capacity of the DGELDI, by assigning more lawyers and experienced magistrates, to
undertake law reform and to provide advice and guidance to other Government Ministries on
legislative proposals. Under the project DGELDI would be provided with appropriate materials and
equipment that it requires to be In a position to discharge its functions. Specialized short-term
advisory services wfll also be made available to DGELDI during the project period, as and when
needed, to help supervise the legislative drafting activities, and to train DGELDI legal staff. To
ensure continuity in the legislative reform process, the Government has taken measures to contain the
high rate of staff turnover in the Ministry of Justice as a whole and, in particular, within DOELDI.
Magistrates can now be redeployed from the tribunals and the PG's Office to fill up positions within
DGELDL In order to enhance the eareer prospects of legal officers of the Ministry of Justice, the
Government will, under this component, take measures (e.g. amending rules governing the
recruitment of magistrates, locally-sponsored training programs combining practical training and
courses at the Center for Judicial Training to be established by Government by end-1995 with
assistance from Portugal) to allow them to qualify as magistrates otherwlse than by having to follow
the single option of professional training program in Portugal. Other means of attracting competen
legal staff to occupy key positions in the Ministry of Justice would also be reviewed. I order to help
supervise the process of revision of the various pieces of legislation, the committee that prepared this
project component bas been expanded to include a wide cross-section of business representatives,
business law  xerts, labor unions, employers and other interested officials. It will help review draft
strategy papers and legislative proposals, as and when they are submitted to it by DGELDI. Ihis
expanded committee will not be expected to draft or initiate legislation;
(i)   Specia&zation of Courts. In order to streamline the flow of comnercial and induswtrial
litigation at the regional level, and thus ease the pressure resulting from the growing volume of such
litigation, the project wil assist with the creation of a separate commercial jurisdiction within each
of the regional courts, as well as separate industrial tribunals. In the initial stages, however, such

C. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
1.    Project Management and Coordination
3.8   Bank sector work in the field of instional developmentrTA has concluded that the management of
institution,1  --forms should be an integral part of day-to-day responsibilities of the ministries concerned.
Based on tL. .xperience, the specific program for each component has been largely formulated by and wil
be implemented by the ministries and agencies themselves. Although the project is in part intended to addrs
the weaknesses in institutional capacity in these ministries and entiies, Cape Verde's administration is
recognized on the whole as being relatively capable, and as noted In par. 3.15, portfolio management has
consistently been among the most highly rated In the Sahel. This day-to.da management and implementation
of each component would be the responsibility of the concerned ministries and enthies, as follows: the civl
eNice component would be managed by the Mistry of Public Adminisrton; the mudicpal
strengthening component would be managed by the Mistry of Inal Admin on in close
coilaboaion with the municipalities; the econoc management component would largely be under the
Minities of Economic Coordinion, and Finance through the Departmen of Planing, Statstics, Research
Public Finae as appropriate; the legl and judical component would be under the Ministry of Justice and
Labor; and the publie procuremnt reform component would be managed by the Department of Public
Assets in the Miistry of Finance. Ihe Ministry of Economic Coordination, in coUaboaion with the
-30-



Ministry of Communications, would carry out the Improvement of economic and socal poicy
communiations sub-component of the economic management component. Prior to negotiations, the
Government formalized the appointments (pra. 5.1) of managers (project component managers-PCMs) for
each component or major sub-components within the respective agencies. A central Project Coordinator
(PC), with qualifications satisfactory to IDA was also appointed at the Ministry of Economic Coordintion.
The terms of reference of the PC and PCMs are in Annexes 11 and 12 and were are tQ at neggtiations.
3.9 The project's policy measures are integrated in the various components, and a high-level advisory and
monitoring group, comprising representatives of the central and local governments, the private sector and
selected civil groups such as unions, would oversee their implementation. Prior toD resentatinof this project
to the Bard, the Government established the advisory group, the Reform Advisory and Implementation
Monitoring Board (RAIMB) to, among other things, provide policy guidance on the direction and pace of the
public sector reform and modernization program, and to provide advice on the conduct and pace of
implementation of the program wit. a view to ensuring effectiveness, efficiency, transparency and consistency
in the process. The RAIMB is co-chaired by the Minister of State for Economic Coordination and the
Minister of Public Administration. The terms of reference of the RAMB were agred to at ne2etinxiQ  and
are in Annex 13. A small Project Coordinating Unit (PCU), to be established within the Ministry of
Economic Coordination and headed by the PC, would be responsible for day-to-day coordination of project
activities. The PCU would serve as the secretariat for the RAIMB. The establishment and staffing of the
PCU would be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed Credit. Project coordination arrangements are
shown in the Chart in Annex 10.
2.    Annual Work Program, Monitoring, Mid-Term Review and Evaluation
3.10  Project implementaion will be ca.ried out on the basis of annual work programs for each component
and major sub-component, thereby enhancing flexibility in planning and monitoring, and allowing objectives
for each component to be attained independently. Each anmnu work program would include: (J) an
evaluation of the previous years' work program; and (ii) the proposed budget and work program including:
(a) in keeping with the monitoring and performance indicators agreed (Annex 9-A), a statement of
monitorable objectives and a program of activities to be undertaken to achieve those objectives; (b) detailed
budgets and financing plans, including a schedule of proposed procurement contruas to be financed by the
Credit; and (c) training plans. They would all be submitted to the PC who would synthesize them and
prepare a project-wide budget and procurement plan to be submitted annally, prior to the end of the previous
year (October 31), to the RAMB for consideration/advice and then to IDA for review and approval.
Agreement with IIDA on the detailed work program for the first year of the project is a condition of
effl&eness of the proposed Credit. PCMs would be expected to present periodic progress reports on project
implementation, on the basis of which the PCU would furnish concise quarterly progress reports to IDA.
These progress would compare actl and forecast progress. The detailed range of project monitoring
indicators is provided in Annex 9. During negotiations. aFreement was reached with the Government on the
project monitoring and performance indicators and on the submission of quarterly reports no later than 30
days after the end of each of the following quarters: March 31, June 30, and September 30, and an annual
report which would include the December 31 period, to be fiunished within two months of year-end. These
reports would include, Iuer atia, the agreed monitoring and performance indicators. Reporting requirements
would begin with the first full quarter following effectiveness.
3.11  In order to review projectprogress, and if necessary to reorient implementation, it was agmei duin
neS"atiwa that IDA and th( tovernment shall undertake jointly, yearly reviews, beginning October 31,
1994, of the progress in the implementation of the project and that a mid-term review would be scheduled
approximately two-and-a-half years (between thirty and fbrty months) after Effectiveness. In order to ensure
its usefulness and that effective evaluation procedures are established during project implemen tion, his
review will have a major focus on the key performance indicators. It was agree that the Government will

furnish to IDA, for conuents, not later than one month prior to each of the above reviews, a draft report
which will be the basis for the reviews. It was also agreed that the Borrower will prepare and furnish to IDA
a Project Completion Report within six months after the closing date. A Supervision Plan is in Annex 14.
3.    Poment and Disbursement
3.12  Procurement. Procurement under the credit would cover the cost of goods, advisory services for
technical assistance, trainingfretraining and studies, and operational support. The totl cost of goods - largely
computer equipment and software, legal textbools, national accounting publications, vehicles, office
machines, equipment for legal services, would be approximately US$4.07 million. All procurement of goods
will be in accordance with Bank Guidelines dated May 1992. Equipment and software, photocopiers, fax
machines, legal textbooks and furniture totalling about $3 million equivalent would be procured through ICB.
Since adequate local competition exists, and the contracts will be relatively small, smaller items of equipment
(including publication of national statistical texts as well as legal publications for the law library), the total
costing $770,000, would be procured through local competitive bidding (LCB) procedures acceptable to the
Association. LCB documents would be reviewed by the Association, and foreign firms would not be
precluded from participation. Individual contract amounts over $100,000 would however to be procured
through ICB. Vehicles, equipment and other materials (total US$300,000) would be grouped whenever
possible, into bid packages of not less than US$30,000 and procured through international and local shopping
after receipt of price quotations from at least three qualified suppliers. All contracts of US$100,000
equivalent or more will be subject to prior review by the Association, while contracts below will be subject
to upot review by the Association.
3.13   Consultants and technical assistance personnel to be fianced by IDA would be appointed in
accordance with Bank Group Guidelines dated August 1981, and would be employed on terms and conditions
satisfactory to IDA. All of the consulting and advisory services (about 8 sy short term and 1.5 sy long-term)
financed under the project, except for the Project Coordinator and Project Component Managers who might
be individually selected, will be selected on the basis of terms of reference and short-lists acceptable to IDA
and procured according to the World Bank Guidelines on the Use of Consultants. Draft sample terms of
reference (TORs) for all major advisory services and studies were ofirmed during ngoatio. Overall
procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 4. Training costs under the project include some
consultants, equipment and materials for local training, as well as a limited amount for training (mostly short
and refresher courses) abroad.

of hIvitation for selection of consultants, and Sample Consultants Contract modified, as appropriate with
regards to taxes, liabilities and payment terms. Prior to grmnlatiQn to the lgaQd of the proposed project,
the Government discussed with IDA, and adopted, a model consultancy contract including a sample letter of
invitation, sor the recruitment of consultants and advisors under the project.
3.15   As of end FY93, lIDAs portolio of ongoing operations in Cape Verde comprises five projects. The
FY92 ARIS revealed an average overall performance rating of 1.7, primarily because of the difficulties In
contracting the construction of the pedagogical instite under the Primary Education Project (Cr. 1853-CV).
The problems were resolved in early FY93 and implementation has been very sadsfactory. The Industra
Promotionprojectisnow closed. Progressontheremaining three nfzastructureReabilitation, Privaization
TA, and Transport and Inffastructure) has been satisfactory. The FY93 ARPP has revealed an average
overall performance rating of 1.4. The portfolio is problem-free. However, under the soon-to-close
Irastructure Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 1954-VC) four audit reports were due. Three were received but
the one for ELECTRA is now overdue because the auditor closed its Dakar office out of which the audit was
being carried out and thus did not complete the work. A plan has now been worked out with Government
for completion of the report. Receipt by IDA of the outstanding audit report for ELECTRA is a cndition

review undertaken by GOCV which revealed that the procurement system needed to be updated In some
areas, even though the Prdvazation TA project has not been slowed down due to delays in procurement, the
proposed project addresses system-wide procurement reforms In Cape Verde.

3.16   Disbugnen   The project is expected to be implemented over a five year period, as the
Government wishes to proceed rapidly with its public sector reform program and aims to complete all
activiies within that period. Ite success to date in implementing the overall economic reform program
and the already advanced state of a number of the proposed project actions indicate that this can be
accomplished. Accordingly, while the average disbursement profile for such operations in the Africa
Region is seven years, the credit is forecast to be disbursed over six years, and to be closed in December
1998.
3.17 All disbursements of the IDA credit would be based on prior documentation except for consultant
contracts of less than $50,000, contracts for equipment and vehicles of less than $100,000, contracts for
training and retraining, and for operating costs which will be made against statements of expenditures
(SOEs). The Project Coordinator would have responsibility for the preparation of withdrawal applications
for all components. Tie documentation for withdrawals under SOEs would be retained at the Ministry
of Economic Coordination for review by IDA staff during supervision missions and for regular semi-
annual audits.
3.18 To facilitate disbursement, the Government would establish a Special Account in a commercial
bank in Cape Verde to cover IDA's share of eligible expenditures. An initial deposit of US$250,000
equivalent would be made from the proceeds of the proposed credit upon effectiveness. IDA would
replenish the Special Account monthly, upon receipt of proof of incurred eligible expenditures. Details
of the establishment and operations of the Special Account and other disbursement procedures have been
agreed during credit negotiations. An agrment was also reached and evidence provided that adequate
provision for local costs will be inscribed in the annual operating budgets of the Government for the
PCU, for the retraining of voluntary departees from the civil service and any other expenditures for which
the Borrower would be responsible. Ihe estimated disbursements and allocation of the proposed IDA
credit are shown in Table 5 on the following page.

4.    Accoumting and Auditing
3.19  The Ministry of Economic Coordination, through the Project Coordining Unit would establish
and maintain, to the satisfaction of IDA, separate accounts and records to be used exclusively for the
proJect. The accounts will be maintained in accordance with sound and internationally recognized
accountng principles and practices. The Project Coordinating Unit will provide interim and annual
financial statements to reflect the financial performance and position of the project. The project would
also finance the hiring of a local accounting firm to assist the Project Coordinating Unit in establishing
a financial management/accounting system. _Independent auditors acceptable to the Association would
audit the project accounts in accordance with interationally acceptable auditing principles and in such
detail as IDA may request. Establishment of a financial management/accounting system for the project
and the selection of the auditors of the project will be conditions of effe tWeMess. The audits would be
undertaken annually, on the basis of a three year contract, with the report submitted to IDA no later than
three monts after the close of the year. In addition, the Special Account would be audited anually, and
the SOEs semi-annually, and audit reports submitted three months after the end of each audit period.
S.    Environmental Aspects
3.20   Cape Verde's enviroment is austere but the people deal with it bravely. The environment is very
frile, given: i) exposure of its predominantly bare surface to wind and water erosion; and ii) fast
growth of urban areas. lnadequate fresh water supply and lack of sanitaion and satisfactory system for
coilection and disposal of hazardous and solid waste pose immediate health hazards to the fast growing
popuation in the urban areas. Environmental problems are well identified and recognized by the
Goverment. The Government has adopted an active approach in carrying out some concrete actions to
address different aspects of the environment (e.g. allocating increasing financial resources to improve
urban living conditions, massive reforestation efforts, etc.). It has also established an inter-agency
National Council on the Environment (CNMA). CNMA has embarked on the design of a strategy, the
identifition of policy measures coherent with other policy reforms, and on the preparation of a
legiaive package and institutional framework to deal with environmental issues on a more
comprehensive basis. The Bank has assisted this process through the preparation of a Country
Evironmental Strae  Paper (CESP). An integral part of the Government's active stance on the
environment is the preparation of a National Environmenal Action Plan (NEAP) which has already
begun. The Goverment has indicated its intention to engage advisory services under the project, under
the category of pre-iestme  studies, to finalize its NEAP by July 30, 1994 (Annex 9). The project
will also include assistance to build the capacity of the Planning Department of the Ministry of Economic
Coordinatio and the Municipalities in environmental policy and impact analysis. The project does not
present the prospect of any detmental environmental impact.
IV. EROCI BENEM AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS
4.1   The proposed project would assist a business-oriented Government at a critical time in the
implementation of its liberaization program in adapting the adminision and the civil service as well
as the judicial system to a new vision of the role of Government in the economy. It would support the
policy of the Government to create an enabling environment for private sector development through
modernization of the legal and judicial systems. It would foster efficicy in the public administration,
through the Ilemention of modern human resource management systems and procedures. It would
also promote greater participation in planning and managing the development process at the local level,
and foster accountability. It would increase the availability, and reduce the delays encountered in the
- 35 -



pepari  and  ecuton of public investment projects and increase efficiency in public resource use.
Ilprovement in investment exon monitorig and in policy and impact analysis would help the
Government in its efforts to better target and reduce poverty.
B. RISKS
4.2 Ihere Is some risk that vested interests within the civil service will resist reform measures as
these imp  on heir entrenched ways of perfirming their tasks, and may involve physical relocation of
staff to municipal goverments. Given the positive achievements of the Government to-date in these
fields, and strong grass-root support for decentralization, these risks are not considered high. There is
a risk that the training of civil servants and the retainig of voluntary departees from the civil service
could be slowed down by the limited capacity of local tainig institutions. This risk is not considered
insurmountale becaus the Govement is, in parallel with this project, embarking on a restructuring of
vocational education for which it has requested IDA support through the proposed Basic Education and
Traiig Project. The implementation of the reform program may also be slowed down by the limited
capacity of the Government administration to design the detais of some of the necessary reforms and to
monitor their ilementation. These risls are addressed through the strengthening of the project's
execution agencies and the appointment of a coordinator in the Ministry of Economic Coordination. The
Government has also shown a capacit to monitor complex reforms under its ongoing public enterprise
resructuing program. Finally, implementation of the Government's civil service retrenchment program
(voluntary departure aspects), though not an explicit part of the proposed project, could lose momentum
f the added strain on the private sectors already limited capacity to absorb new labor force entrants
results in higher unemployment and a political backlash. This risk will be minimized by an attractive
incentive package that provides montly income payments for up to three years - just enough to meet
subsistence needs of retrenchees without discouraging them from looking for ajob - in addition to a lump
sum distribution at the time of departure. Government has already made its budgetary allocations for the
payments, a key donor has already allocated assistance and other donors have expressed interest in
pxoviding fnding should the need arise.

UNOFFICIAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION
REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
BRIEF STATEMENT OF GOVERNMENT POLICY
I.    INTRODUCTION
The Government's Program, approved by the National Assembly, identifies modernization and capacity
building of Public Administration as one of the key axes of the governmental action. This fundamental option
emanates from a clear perception that the Public Administration is not neutral, capable of either constituting
a factor blocking or promoting development. In order that it transform itself into a factor promoting
development, the Cape Verdean public administration's capacity needs to the built in technical and behavioral
terms, in so that it can contribute positively to the implementation of a global economic and social reform
program able to lead the country to another level of economic and social development.
That is the "raison d'etre' of the Institutional Reform and Capacity Building Program of Public
Administration, among whose general objectives are:
-   Modernization of Public Administration, by transforming its organizations, strengthening its
technical capacity and valorizing its human resources in order to render it capable of supporting
the country's development process;
-  Enhancement of the capacity to formulate, manage, analyze and communicate public policies,
particularly economic policy, through a better capacity building of governmental services in the
economic area;
-  Improvement in the utilization of public resources through the creation of transparent and efficient
management systems of public property and resources;
-  Revision and modernization of the legal system together with the system of justice, with a view
to create a favorable environment for private investment, business confidence, and entrepreneurial
activity in general and to ensure good governance;
- Strengthening of democracy and of popular participation in the development process through
capacity building of Local Municipal Governments as well as consolidation of their autonomy.
II.   REFORM CONTEfX
The Government's economic development program is articulated around some explicitly formulated,
key lines of action, which may be synthesized as follows: a) dynamic and complete insertion of Cape Verde
into the world economy; b) reduction of State role as a direct economic agent in the productive sector; c)
recognition of the key role of the private sector as the engine of growth in the medium term, and of the
leading role of market forces; d) opening to external investmnent; and e) poverty reduction and protection of
the environment.
- 47 -



ANNEX I
Policy Letter
Page 9 of 13
Ibis new orientation challenges past policies and practices and collides directly with the ways in which
existing structures and agencies operate in the country, particularly in the public sector, which were designed
to provide support to an inward looking developmental policy, where the State commanded the economy.
Structural reforms already started by the Government, with support of the World Bank and other
development partners, comprises the following main areas of intervention: a) restructuring of the public
enterprise sector; b) economic liberalization and private sector development; c) financial sector reform; d)
refbrm of the education and training sector; e) infrastructure development; f) public administration institutional
reform; and f) shared responsibility for resource management between the central Government and the various
islands, due to the archipelago nature of the country.
Overall, the reforms under way are aimed at creation of a favorable environment for investment and
private economic activity. Specifically, this is to be accomplished through restructuring and slimming down
of the public enterprises sector, revision of the State's role in the economy, development of market forces,
increased competition, financial system liberalization and diversification of economic financing instruments,
human resource capacity building as well as the creation of structures judged indispensable to the integration
of domestic market and the insertion of Cape Verde into the world economy. The effectiveness and success
of structural reforms is highly dependent on the implementation capacity of the Public Administration itself,
which does not find itself sufficiently well prepared to meet the challenge posed by the new strategy.
Cape Verdean Public Administration reveals characteristics of a tradition-bound organization,
somewhat introverted, overly legalistic, heavy, in a general manner insufficiently trained, and often not
sensitive enough to broader societal changes. Gauging its action from a rational perspective these traits tend
to act as a real barrier to organizational development. These characteristics compel the administration to
function in ritual manner which transforms its organization into a not very effective machine, overlooking the
basic values and objectives at all administrative activity. In addition to the above, Cape Verdean Public
Administration also faces shortage of some technical and material resources to adequately perform its duties.
Obviously, an administration of this kind could constitute a major barrier to economic reforms as well
as the realization of the Government's development program. Therefore, its modernization and capacity
building are paramount and immediate in order that it can positively respond to the demands of a global
reform process.
II.   SPECIIIC OBJECTIVES AND ACTIONS
mIll. Civil Sewvce
Civil Service modemization and capacity building will contribute to an administration that is better
prepared to meet the needs of the general public and of economic agents, more attentive to the specific needs
of its clientele' and outward looking towards the society to which it belongs, facilitating the processes of
change in all spheres of economic and social life.
The specific objectives of the Civil Service reform are the following:
-   development of a system of human resources planning and management that facilitates a policy
of personnel mobility, according to structural and specific public service needs;
- 48 -



ANNEX I
Policy letter
Page 1v of 13
implementation of a broad training program in order to Increase civil servants' technical skills
and adapt their capacity and attitudes to the real needs of an efficient public service;
- reduction in size of the Civil Service towards more adequate levels, compatible with the new State
role, productivity increase, quality improvement of service delivery and motivation of civil
servants.
Important actions have already been taken, in a gradual but systematic way. Thus, a sensitization
campaign has been launched, aiming at informing and raising the awareness of both civil servants and the
public for the need and urgency of the reform; new personnel management instruments have been adopted,
with satisfactory results, namely the new Position, Career and Salary Plan (PCCS), and personnel career
mobility process instruments within administration and regulamentation of examination to earn promotion.
A national bureaucratic simplification plan was developed and is now being implemented in more difficult
areas of the public administration and the general public interface. These are in the areas of customs, taxation
and the office of public notary. Concomitantly, new hiring to the Civil Service has been frozen since January
1993, except for technical personnel, teachers and medical personnel with a view to stabilize the civil service
force. The main elements of the Voluntary Departure Program (PAV) were defined and discussed in the
Social Consultative Council.
Actions with greater impact foreseen in the reform program consist of: a) implementation of a system
of human resources planning and administration, which will involve enhancing the existing data base,
preparation and implementation of human resources performance appraisal instruments and career planning
and identification of training needs, to be carried out from 1994 on; b) preparation and implementation of an
extensive training program involving various Ministries beginning with six, to be carried out in the period of
1994198; c) implementation of the Voluntary Departure Program (PAV), expected to take place in 1994/95;
and d) implementation of a training program specifically directed towards PAV participants.
11.2. Economic Management
The enhancement of the capacity to formulate economic policy and the analysis of its impact permits
the Government to better configure its policy instruments and adjust them to the real needs of the economy
while enhancing the capacity to dialogue and consult with economic agents and social partners.
The specific objectives of this component of the reform program are the following:
- restructuring of the national accounting and statistical system in order to ensure information
needed for managing the economy by State and by economic agents;
-  strengthening of the capacity to formulate economic policy and undertake impact analysis by the
General Directorate of Planning;
-   irmproviemernt of the capacity to coordinate foreign aid, to manage public debt, and to mobilize
internal financial resources;
-   improvement of communication in the areas of economic policy and of intra-governmental
communications.
The preparation of the III National Development Plan was an important focal point to identify
potentis and limitations. The planning system was revised and the first Multi-Annual Public Investment
- 49 -



ANNEX I
Policy Letter
Page 11 of 13
Program (PIP) is being developed. The analysis and publication of the General Population Census has been
completed and a new consumer price index was established.
Within the sphere of reforms, the following partial list of actions are foreseen: a) creation of the
National Statistics Institute, with by-laws giving more independence from the Government; b) timely
preparation and publication of national accounts; c) revision of the national accounts basis and methodology;
d) undertaking of specific sectoral surveys; e) long and short-term training of Statistics staff; f) conclusion
of the Multi-Annual Public Investment Program for 199419f> g) establishment of a pre-investment fund; b)
preparation of a planning manual; i) technical staff training in the area of planning; and j) providing economic
departments with necessary equipment and materials.
11.3. Public Property Management
This area of govemmental activity needs immediate attention in order to increase efficiency in public
resources utilization and overall improvement of State property management. The legislative base of this
reform is under preparation and almost complete.
The leading reform actions foreseen are summarized as follow: a) compilation and updating of all
legislation regulating public procurement, 1993/94; b) training of personnel of the various government
Departments related to public property management and procurement; c) design and implementation of a plan
to computerize the procurement process and property management.
11.4. Legal Reform
The changes to be introduced in the legal and judicial system will benefit from the overall effort of
the civil service reform as a result of the fact that the staff in the system will be better trained and sensitized
to the real needs of the civil society and more motivated by the improvements introduced in the human
resources management within the Administration. In return, a more simplified legal and judicial system,
which facilitates private sector activities will introduce a new dynamism in the relations between the State and
the private sector, and enhancing the guarantees and the confidence of economic agents.
Among the objectives of this component of the reform program, the following are emphasized:
-   creation of a legal environment that is favorable to Increasing economic activity as well as an
institutional environment that favors business development, by designing an efficient
organizational and operating model that efficiently responds to the needs of economic agents;
-   institutionalization of procedures and decision making bodies that ensure expediency, seriousness,
judiciousness and quality, in the decisions affecting economic agents' interest;
-   establishment of a notary public and registry sub-system that assures business legality and grants
security and confidence to the transacting parties;
-   institutional capacity building of the Ministry of Justice and Labor, in order to conceptualize and
monitor the execution of policy measures foreseen in the legislative reform package.
Government actions in the area of Justice is principally centered on legislative reform. Some of the
actions already undertaken include the revision of the Penal Procedural Code, the Commercial Registry, family
legislation, the creation of the Judiciary Police, initiation of the revision of the Labor Code, now underway,
- Sn -



ANNEX I
Policy Letter
Page 12 of 13
along with the law of corporations and the Penal Code.  In the area of judiciary administration and
administration of the registry and offices of the notary public, measures have been adopted with a view to
providing the services with material and human resources indispensable to elevate Justice to the level of the
citizens' demands.
Within the reform process, the following main actions are foreseen: a) conclusion of the revision of
the Labor Code; b) revision of the Commercial and Company Code; c) revision of the legislative procedures;
d) revision of the legislation of registry and offices of the notary; e) specialization of judicial bodies; f)
modernization of registry and notary systems; g) creation of the center for judicial training; h) training of
magistrates and other legal personnel; and i) development of legal information.
m.s. Decentralization and Local Government Enhancement
A well endowed local administration will be better prepared to assume the responsibilities of local
development and more sensitized to contributing to the overall development of the country. At the samne time,
a stronger local administration will be able to relate more effectively with the central administration as well
as with private economic agents.
Ihe objectives of the reform in this area are the following:
-   deepen the decentralization process and bring local communities closer to decision centers;
-   increase efficiency in the use of resources and management capacity of municipalities;
-   improve articulation between the central and local Governments;
- strengthen institutional capacity of all municipalities and increase administrative efficiency of
municipal bodies;
Decentralization has deserved a particular attention of the Government, despite the many difficulties
faced by this new subject matter in national policy. The following actions have been undertaken: a)
organization and carrying out of the first democratic local government elections in the history of the country,
in which political parties and groups of citizens participated freely (1991); b) a substantial increase of the
funds transferred to municipal bodies, which have been multiplied by a factor of 2.6 since 1990; c) effective
transfer and negotiation for transfer of authority, human, material and financial resources from the central to
the local administrations, in the areas of public works, culture, land transportation, commerce, education, and
water resources; d) organization of training activities and capacity building of the locally elected officials.
The following reform actions are anticipated: a) further development of key directives of the
decentralization process and conclusion of the process of transfer of responsibility and competencies; b)
design and implementation of a new planning, budgeting and accounting system of municipal governments;
c) revision of the law of local finances and identification of additional municipal resources aimed at increasing
their independence on the central government; d) development and implementation of an action plan for the
creation of offices for technical support to municipalities; e) providing municipalities with minimum resources
In terms of materials and equipment, needed to carry out their activities; f) design and implementation of a
training program for locally elected officials and for civil servants working in local administrations, in such
areas as municipal management, finances and accounting, project appraisal, human resources organization and
management.
- 51 -



ANNEX I
Policy Letter
Page 13 of 13
IV    ORGANIZATIONAL AND INSIITUTIONAL ISSUES
'he Government assigns the greatest importance to the success of the institutional reform and capacity
building program of the public sector. As such, it considers necessary an effective and efficient management
system of the program, which will assure its timely implementation and adequate coordination. For that
purpose, the Government wIll create, via legislation, a Public Sector Reform Advisory and Implementation
Monitoring Board (RAIMB), co-directed by the Minister of State of Economic Coordination and the Minister
Public Administration and Parliamentary Affairs. This Board will include component managers (PCMs), and
a project coordinator (PC) of the Public Sector Reform and Capacity Building Project to be supported by the
World Bank, and representatives of other public and private sector entities. The Board will monitor the whole
reform process and will support the Government in defining the pace and the direction of the reform.
The Government will also create, by legislative means, a Project Coordinadon Unit (PCU) which will
help develop and coordinate the actions of the various aspects of the program.
The operational management of each component will be under the auspices of each sectoral Ministry.
Each Minstry, will designate a project component manager, responsible for the successful implementation of
the program in the respective sector.
V   CONCLUSION
The public sector reform and capacity building program is consistent with the Government's Program
and will support the Government in the restructuring of a vital sector, whose dynamism is a precondition to
the success of the overall economic reform program and the country's overall development strategy.
The program encompasses modernization of management, methods and processes and will contribute
towards a better capacity of both the central and the local administration. Such a program will enable the
central and local administrations to better perform their respective functions, thus enhancing community and
public participation in the development and implementation of policies.
(Signed)
The Minister of State for Economic Coordination
Jose Tomas Wahnon de C. Veiga
Praia, November 17, 1993.



ANNEX 2
A Historical Perspective
Page 1 of 2
REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
CAPE VERDE: A HISTORICAL PERSPECTIVE^
1.           In the year 1456, a Portuguese sailor named Diego Gomes came upon an uninhabited
archipelago off the west coast of Africa, adjacent to the Cape of Verde. The strategic location of the ten
islands of the archipelago relative to the west coast of Africa, along with the existence of excellent ports
among the islands made them very attractive to the Portuguese for purposes of shipping and trade within the
continent of Africa.
2.           Portugal sent to the Cape Verde Islands many of its and other politically dissident citizens to
inhabit the islands by mixing with West Africans as Portugal participated in the trade of slaves. Out of this
blend of strong willed, diverse people evolved the Cape Verdean population - a creole population for the most
part, with a wide variety of dialects of their language. Because of their location off West Africa relative to
the trade winds, the Cape Verde Islands were central to the European exploration of the continent of Africa.
Cape Verdeans navigated some of the ships that made historic journeys to explore the coasts of Africa and
the rest of the world.
3.           Located in the Sahel region of Africa and victim of its dry winds, Cape Verde has throughout
its history suffered extensive periods of drought and famine, which caused tremendous suffering, pestilence
and death. Between the late 18th century and mid 19th century, well over 100,000 Cape Verdeans died of
starvation caused by three major droughts. As much as 44% of the entire population was lost during the
worst of these droughts. During the turn of the century, as many as 20,000 to 30,000 Cape Verdeans would
die in a given year due to the devastation of the drought.
4.           As a result of this suffering and the difficulty of earning a living in the islands, an estimated
700,000 Cape Verdeans emigrated to the United States, Europe and other countries of Africa during the early
and middle years of the 20th century. But for the exceptional navigating skills of the Cape Verdean sailors,
this massive migration of people would have been impossible. Through many generations, Cape Verdean
emigres have continued to be very loyal to the homeland. This is evidenced by remittances and other links
forged by Cape Verdean emigrant communities.
5.           The strength of the Cape Verdean culture - a strong work ethic and respect for others,
allowed Cape Verdeans who emigrated to establish even during the most difficult economic times of the 20th
century. In the U.S. for example, Cape Verdeans established successful and productive communities in
Massachusetts, Rhode Island and Connecticut. During the 1950's, Cape Verdeans began to relocate in other
parts of the United States, mostly California. Today there are over 500,000 Cape Verdeans in the United
States, many of whom have reached pinnacles of businesses, professions or political office.
- 53 -
Main Source: The Foundation of Cabo Verde, 1993.



ANNEX 2
A Historical Perspective
Page 2 of 2
6.           Cape Verdeans, led by Amilcar Cabral, gained Independence from Portugal on July 5, 1975,
ending 515 years of colonial nrle. On June 30, 1975 the Party for the Independence of Guinea-Bissau and
Cape Verde (PAIGC, later PAICV) was the only political group to run candidates for the first general
election, and Aristides Pereira and Pedro Pires, heroes in the struggle for independence, became President
and Prime Minister, respecdvely.
7.           In January of 1991, Cape Verde held its first multi-party democratic election artl. became one
of the few African countries to establish a democratic government peacefully. The election was won by the
Movement for Democracy paty (MPD), of a liberal bent, anL. Carlos Veiga becane the country's Prime
Minister. In February, 1991 Antonio Manuel Mascarenhas Monteiro was elected President of Cape Verde.

Civil Service Reform

REPUBUC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
Civil SWcice Refmonnad MWoieriaion
1.    BACKGROUND AND ISSUES
nhe Civil Service in Cape Verde is well-rganized, hiard-working and respected; yet it is far from
reaching its potential in teras of efficiency and productivity. There are four principal constraints to a
more efficient and productive Civil Service: (i) overstaffing, especially at the lower ranb; (it) an acute
shortage of technical, administative and managerial skills; (iii) a traditional, control-oriented personnel
management system lacking the capacity to devise and implement human resource management and
development strategies that address the needs of the reforming and decentralizing public sector; and (iv)
Inadequate provision for the facilities, equipment and other supporting Inputs that civil servants need to
be productive on the job.
Unlike the case of many other Sub-Sabaran African countries, the impetus for reform is not
dramatic fiscal pressure. hbis represents a special opportunity to retool the Civil Service with more
modern instruments of human resource management, which will enable it to fulfill a new service-oriented
mission in support of the State as facilitator rather than as manager of economic growth.
Cost-containment in Cape Verde is not an end in itself, which could undermine capacity-building, but an
abiding concen in the larger effort to raise efficiency and pay for a more adaptive and skilled Civil
Service. This effort requires additional resources to be generated pardy by timming excess staff, hiving
off services better performed by the private sector and municipalities, selling or liquidating public
enepries already on the auction block, reorganizing service delivery, expanding the revenue base
through reform of the tax system, already underway, and promoting a burgeoning private sector.
(a) Instutional Enironment for Civil Sece Reform
ITe experience of Civil Service Reform Projects In most of Sub-Sabaran Africa has cleady
demonstrated that the implementation of technical measures to improve the effectiveness and efficiency
of the Public Administration do not produce significant and sustainable results if undertaken in a non-
conducive govermental environment.
For this reason, a detailed assessment of the institutional enviromnent for civil service reform,
including key govenance issues, was conducted in Cape Verde. The results, which are presented in
Annex 4-A provide strong evidence that the envionment is highly conducive to the implementation of
reforms designed to modernize the system for managing and developing human resources in the public
sector.
- 59 -

The structure of the Goveroment comprises the Office of the Prime Minister which includes the
department for youth and social promotion, and 13 Ministries. In addition, there are tS municipalities
supervised by the Ministry of Intal Affairs. The relative size of the various organizational units
comprising the legislative, executive and judiciary branches of central and local governments Is presented
below. The size of the Ministries of Education and of Health reflects how much attention these two
sectors have consistently received from all governments since independence.

Directors who manage units. Each ministry, except Economic Cooperation, has a Director of
Administrative Services. The department of Administrative Services is responsible for personnel
management, finance, facilities and procurement.
To respond more effectively to the changing economic and social situation in the country, the
structure of the Government was changed in March 1993. The Ministers of Justice and Labor, and
Finance and Planning, were elevated to the ranks of Ministers of State, with the latter assuming the
responsibility of a new Ministry of Economic Coordination.
(c) Profile of the Civil Service
S        For the size of its population, Cape Verde's Civil Service is one of the largest in Sub-
Saharan Africa: 2.8 civil servants for every 100 inhabitants compared, for example, with 0.6 in Mali,
0.5 in Niger, and 0.9 in Senegal. In 1992, the total wage bill of the public administration amounted to
C.V. Escudos 2,388 million (approximately US$ 36.2 million), or 52 percent of total recurrent
expenditures.
In the decade immediately following Cape Verde's independence in 1975, civil service
employment expanded at an average rate of less than 2% annually, much slower than the amual growth
of the economy (6%?) and the population (2.5%?) over the same period. From 1985 to 1990, however,
the government, which direcdy controlled and managed most sectors of the economy, loss control over
the payroll. Employment grew at 9% a year - almost double the GDP growth rate - and the ranks of
the Civil Service swelled from 7,800 to close to 11,300. Most of this uncontrolled growth was
concentrated in the lower-paying positions, and much of it occurred in the final two politically-charged
years during the run-up to the first ever multi-party elections. (See Charts I & 2 in Annex 4-B).

ANNEX-4
Civil Service Reform
Page 4 of 20
The newly-elected government in 1991 came to power on a platform of rolling back the role of
the State in the economy to make room for private-sector-led growth. Inheriting an inflated civil service,
the government immediately froze recruitment for all but the most essential categories of staff, pending
a review of the impact of its market-friendly policies on the public sector and a better understanding of
the strengths and weaknesses of the civil service. Several steps were taken to reinvigorate the manpower
control system including the development of a computerized personnel system in the Ministry of Public
Administration, which is tightly linked to the payroll system in the Ministry of Finance, and the
publication of all approved positions in the Official Gazette.
These measures have proved effective. Since 1990, the government has achieved, mostly through
natural attrition, a modest reduction in the size of the Civil Service, which now stands at about 11,200
employees. Having reinforced the system of manpower controls, the government is now in a position
to introduce deeper reforms. In the next phase of reform, the government intends to bring down the size
of the civil service in line with the new role of the State and to improve the fit between personnel and
the State's skill requirements.
Pay Trends. Unlike the pattern found in many Sub-Saharan African countries, real wages have
kept pace with inflation, and the pay structure has become somewhat less compressed. In January 1989,
with the annual consumer price index rising 4.4 percent over the previous twelve months, the
Government granted a 17 percent across-the-board pay increase for all but high-level managers, whose
salaries were increased by 17 to 28 percent. This was followed by another 17 percent increase in 1991,
and a further adjustment in 1992 owing to a regrading exercise. Whereas the before-tax ratio of the base
pay of a manager in Grade A to that of an unskilled worker in Grade U was 4.1:1 in 1987, that ratio had
improved to 5.1:1 by 1992 (IMP, 1993). If the salaries of director-generals are included, the
compression ratio stands at about 7.1:1.
The size of the civil service wage bill is less an issue than the share of budgetary resources
allocated to the facilities, equipment and other goods and services civil servants need to be productive.
Pay increases, combined with the rapid rise in employment of unskilled workers in the run-up to the
multi-party elections, contributed to a rising wage bill, which represented 51.9 percent of recurrent
expenditures in 1992, compared with 43.5 percent in 1988. In 1992, the total wage bill of the central
government amounted to C.V. Escudos 2388 million (approximately US$ 36.2 million), or 52 percent
of total recurrent expenditures, and 7.5 percent of GDP. The increase in the wage bill as a share of
recurrent expenditure has been offset largely by decreases in the share of transfers to public enterprises
and local government, and interest payments. At the same time, outlays on goods and services have not
improved much, falling as a share of recurrent expenditure from 7.4 percent in 1988 to 6.3 percent in
1992, although they are expected to rise again to 7.5 percent in 1993.
Positions are graded and paid on the basis of well-defined points for job content and
responsibility, and the salary structure, published for all to see in the Official Gazette, is free from the
distortions of excessive fringe benefit.
Oualifieation Pronie. The problem of overstaffing in the Civil Service is compounded by an
employment structure that Is heavily skewed toward employees with minimal qualifications. Of the
11,172 civil service employees, 3,355 persons, or one-tird of the total (43 percent if excluding teachers),
- 62 -

were office and service employees of low professional qualification. A high proportion of those are in
the 'servente" category (i.e. cleaners, office messengers, and such) which, by itself, groups more than
10 percent of all civil service emp!oyees. Another 737 persons, or 6.5 percent (10 percent if excluding
teachers) were blue-collar worke.'s. Excluding the Ministry of Education, only 1,625 civil servants, or
13 percent of the total (20 pecent excluding teachers) were in civil service grades requiring a level of
education of high school or above. (See Chart 3 in Annex 4-B).

Of the 5,507 recruitments completed in the last five years in the public administration, 1,691
persons, or 30 percent of the total, were blue- and white-collar workers of the lowest civil service
classification. Another 2,508 persons, or 45 percent, were integrated in the civil service as teachers, but
close to half that number in the classification open to those possessing only the lowest mium
qualification required to teach at prinary school level. Only 670 of the new civil servants, or 12 percent,
belonged to the various classification of the civil service requiring a minimum of high school-level
education.

significant improvement in professionalism, both in technical and managerial tems, of the civil service.

Age, Distribtion. The civil service of Cape Verde is young. 2,050 people, or 29 percent of those
for which age data is available, are of less than 29 years of age. Another 3,060, or 43 percent, are of
between 30 and 39 years of age. Although the mandatory retirement age in Cape Verde is 65 years (with
an option to retire at 60 years of age for those with 34 years in the civil service), 46 persons of over 70
years of age were still employed by the civil service in March 1993, either because they occupied an
essential position for which no qualified replacement could yet be found and hired, or because they
belonged to one of the lowest income categories and their contract was renewed at their own request. (See
Chart 5 in Annex 4-B).

Gender Distrbuton.
Women and men are more or less equally represented in the Public Administon in Cape
Verde, with respectively 46 and 54 percent of the total civil servants. While women are under-epresed
in, or totally absent from, certain grades, this does not reflect a deliberate discAminatory policy but rather
the nature of the jobs associated with the grade (bluecollar for instance). Further, an analysis by grade
shows that Cape Verde is fairly advanced by any standard in promoting women to managerial positions.
Women account for slightly less than 50 percent of the middle management positions ("Technico"), 25
percent of the top civil sevat ("DirigenteW) and 50 percent of the Cabinet positions ("De Quadro
Especial"). (See Chart 6 in Anmex 4-B).

Geogrpical Distribution.
Slightly less than 60 percent of all civil servants work in the island of Santiago. lbis percentage
is large but not disproportionate, as the island accounts for 51 percent of the total population of Cape
Verde, and is the location of the capital city, Praia. In most of the other islands, the proportion of civil
servants is roughly in line with their share of the total population. (See Chart 7 in Annex 4-B). Ihis
teffects the policy of deconcenwaion of essential central government services, such as education, health
and civil works, which has been purs':d of long, due to the scattered and isolated nature of the islands.
In two islands (S. Antao and Fogo), the proportion of civil servants is much lower than their share of the
population. Ihis appears to be mainly due to the fact that these two islands have the largest number of
projects and technical assistance funded by bilateral donors, as a number of services which are provided
by the deconcentrated units of central government ministries in other islands are provided through these
projects in S. Antao and Fogo.
