Stabs of Personnel. Out of 11,172 public administration employees on the payroll, only 1,649
(15%) have full civil servant stats, as characterized by an indefinite duration contract and full benefits
and protection under the civil service status. Another 2,249 (20%), i.e. those with less than 5 years
seniority, were not yet confirmed as civil servants, although enjoying all the benefits of civil servant
status, including almost total employment protection. In addition, 478 full civil servants (4%) assumed
top management position in the public administraion, under a two-year renewable term. Finally, 542 civil
sens (either confirmed or not) (5%) did not fill a budgetary position.
The remaining 6,277 employees (56%) are not civil servants proper. They work under variods
foms of contract for the public administration and usually do not enjoy all of the benefits accruing from
a civil service status. Of these, 2,735 (24%), mostly at middle- and top-management levels, and in
education, are employed under a renewable fixed duration contract of usually two years or more. In
addition, 1,458 persons (13% of the total), mosty blue-ollar workers and office employees, work under
a one year renewable contract, and another 2,084 persons (18.5%) under a six-month to one year
renewable contract. (See Chart 8 in Annex 4-B).
However, all contractu  employees of the Public Administration, whatever the duration of their
fiaed-term contract, 'de facto' enjoy almost complete job security. Except for major breach of discipline,
there is no recent record of a contract not being automatically renewed at expiry.

2.  OBJECrIE OF TE GOVERNMNT
The objectves of the Governmet are tree-fold: (a) reduction of the size of the civil sevice to
levels which are both financially sustinable and compatible with the redesigned role of the State in the
economy, and to increase the productivity of the civil servants through elimination of overstaffing; (b)
implementation of improved buman resoureplanningand managementaytm= to promote and organize
vertical and horizontal mobility of the personnel, in direct relation with the short- and long-term
requirements and with the priorities of the various public services and orgaiztional units in response
to their new role; and, (c) implementation of a comprehensive                   to adapt civil servants'
technical experdse and attides tO the new roles they are expected to play, and tO the requirements of
a truly public service-oriented administration.
MM I   ming to the Challenge at a Modem AdmbnistratIon

determining employment conditiorw and enforcing the labor code. However, this role Is changing gedually as the
Govermnent Is moving slowly towards clvii service reform and prvatzation of its publio enterpfises.
The public administration Is excessiwvely lr  by any standard, with 28 ciii servants per 1000 citizens. In spite of

3. THE RETRENCHMENT PROGRAM
To reach the first objective of rationalization of the size of the civil service, the following
instruments, in this order of priority, are being considered by government: (i) voluntary departure
program (VDP), (ii) early retirement and (iii) termination of contract with compensation.
The objectives of the Government are to significantly reduce the number of civil servants in the
lowest category of Public Administration which is, by far, the most overstaffed. However, this category
also regroups the most vulnerable groups, i.e. those with only the minimum level of literacy, almost no
professional qualifications, and already low levels of income. Their present average net monthly salary
is between CV Esc. 8,200 (US$105) and CV Esc. 15,000 (US$190).
To all extent possible, the Goverrment wants the departures to be voluntary. For this purpose,
it intends to offer an attractive package to provide incentive enough to the potential beneficiaries to leave
a secure job and look for employment opportunities. It will also support demand-driven training and
retraining schemes to facilitate redeployment. Although Government will finance the cost of training/
retraining programs, it will rely primarily on private sector institutions and NGOs for their
implementation. Finally, depending on the actual participation rate in the Voluntary Departure Program
(VDP), Government will encourage the creation by former civil servants of small and micro-enterprises
to which essential services could be contracted out.
Government is keenly aware of the challenge it faces in the implementing the program.
Employment prospects in the formal sector are likely to remain limited until the new economic policy
produces significant results in term of new local and foreign investments and job creation. However,
while not particularly large or buoyant by Sub-Saharan African standards, there are indications that the
informal sector will be able to absorb a fair number of retrenched staff. Its job creation potential,
however, should not be overestimated.
TMe vulnerability of the target groups, and the risks associated with their ability to properly
manage a one-time and, by their standard, large payment on departure, is of serious concern to the
Government. Accordingly, it intends to include in the VDP's incentive package a "safety net' element.
The package offered to potential beneficiaries would combine a "lump-sum" at the time of departure with
monthly payments over a period of time. However, the level and duration of the monthly payments will
be calculated in such a way as to: (i) ensure that the beneficiaries are motivated to immediately and
actively prospect for new job opportunities, while (ii) providing them with a guaranteed level of income
adequate to meet basic subsistence needs during the transition period.
Based on various hypotheses regarding the number of civil servants who might take advantage
of the program and the package which might be offered, the cost of the program has been estimated at
between US$ 2.5 million for 1,000 departures, or 30 percent of the target group, with the least generous
package, and US$ 9.0 million, for 1,700 departures, or 50 percent of the target group, with a most
generous package. The present payroll cost of the target group amounts to US$ 4.9 million annually, and
the initia investment would be recovered from savings in between 3 and 6.5 years. The internal rate of
return has been calculated at between 17 and 29 percent.

Proect Description: Rofrenchment ENgrm
WitQ Comonment. The project will assist the Goveranent through financing for (i) the final
design of an inrplementadon strategy for the Voluntary Departure Program (US$25,000), and (ii) VDP
progra  implnon, including training and retraining schemes (estimaed between US$6 and 8
million).
Consultants will conduct a study to assist the Government finalize its Yolun    D=Mto
PrQgram strategy. The consultants will: (i) evaluate the responsiveness of the target group to various types
of incentive packages, with a view to identifying the least cost/best result combination; (i) review the
potentW supply of jobs existing in the formal and informal sectors for the categories of personnel targeted
under the program; (iii identify the type of training to be offered to potential beneficiaries to prepare
them for the existing and emerging job opportunities in the private sector; (iv) explore the potential for
creation by retrenched staff of small and micro-enterprises, and identify the type of assistance that might
be offered to facilitate their start-up (including contracting-out services previously delivered by
Government); and (v) design the details of the program including the level and type of the final package
to be offered, criteria for participation in the program, detailed costs, and program implementation
procedures and monitoring/control mechanisms. The Terms of Reference for this study are presented in
Annex 4-C. The Ministry of Public Administration will also evaluate the cost of an ealy retirement
s£m  and develop an implementation strategy for the scheme. A simulation model for costing of the
VDP will be used to decide on the compensation package to be offered, and evaluate the amount of
financing required.
Esiimated Cost. The consultants will be able to take advantage of the computerized cost
simulation model already developed with Bank assistance. The level of effort has been estimated at 4
person-months, and its total cost is not expected to exceed US$ 25,000, including travel within Cape
Verde.
Conditions. 'he Government submitted its draft retrenchment implementation strategy,
including both VDP and early retirement scheme thus meeting one of the conditions for Board
Presentation. Agreement was also reached during negotiations on the details of the retrenchment
program, including training/retraining for departees and the total financing to be included under the
project.
4. MANAGEMENT OF HUMAN RESOURCES IN PUBLIC ADMINISTRATION
Alt'hough systems and procedures of personnel "management' in the Civil Service in Cape
Verde are fairly advanced by Sub-Saharan Afican standards, they still remain basically static instruments
better suited to admining rather than to managing the human resources of the public administration.



ANX4
Civil Service Reform
Page 14 of 20
BOX 2  Moderiniz   Civ Srvioe: At th Cutting Edge In Sub-Seheran Africa
Cape Verde Is following the footsteps of OECD governments who are making customer feedback and the
participation of front-line employees In problem-solving key ingredients In serice and productivity improvement
programs. The Civil Service is "istening to Its oustomers,  the public service users, through cerefully-deigned surveys.
It is soliciting ideas from rank-and-file workers by posting in the workplace 'Suggestion Boxes.* which are opened
personally by the Minister of Pubrio Admoinistration in the presence of witnesses. In addition points of direct contact
with the public are also being remodeled to make them more accessible and friendly.
On Its own Iritietive, the Ministry of Public Administration IMPA) designed a questionnaire which will be
administered throughout the archipelago In September 1993 to a random sample of about 400 Cape Verdians. To
ensure meaningful statistical results, and to sharpen the focus of the questions, the MPA pre-tested the questionnaire
with a group of 38 people in the capital, Praia. The anonymity of respondents Is being guarded and survey questions
ae free from bias against criticism of the bureaueracy. The first question in the trial-run, for example, asked
respondents to characterize the time in which the bureaucracy responds to requests: 68 percent replied "excessiveo,
21 percent "normal," and 1 1 percent had no opinion. Referring to the public service bureaucracies they have the most
contact with, only 16 percent thought the services were 'good,' 24 percent 'bad," and 61 percent 'reasonable.'
In a separate question indicative of the shortage of skilled personnel, only eight respondents thought the bureaucrats
they dealt with were competent, and nine respondents said these officials had little knowledge of the function to
which they were assigned.
if the pre-test is any guide. the authorities can take heart in the fact that 76 percent of the respondents reported
Rules of Svice The basic rules of public service employment with regards (i) classification and
grading, (ii) compensation, and (iii) horizontal and vertical mobility of personnel were upgraded in 1992,
to make them simpler, more flexible and more transparent (Decreto-Lei No. 86/92, and 87/92 of July
16, 1992). The number of classification categories was reduced, compensation was reviewed, and a
tsting system was introduced to regulate promotions. However, much remains to be done to ensure an
adequate supply of qualified personnel through recruitment and training, and better management of
existing resources through organizational planning and career development.
Human Resource Information Syste. The basic instrument for controlling employment and
managing personnel is a comprehensive and well-maintained computerized data base covering all civil
servants. Besides providing the means for identifying each employee, information such as budgetary
position, geographical location, classification and contractual status, seniority, age, or basic education can
be retrieved in real time by authorized users. Housed in the Ministry of Public Administration, this multi-
dimensional data base can be used to document the profile of the civil service from the level of work units
to ministries to the entire central government. Recently, the identification of each employee, with his/her
position in the civil service was published in the Official Journal, and this register was linked to the
payroll system. Not only is the data base an excellent tool for strengthening pay and employment
policymaking, but it serves to check against "ghost workers* - a common problem  in developing
countries but one not detected in Cape Verde - and other irregularities.
OrganizationRal RIPIning. Ibe organization chart and description of roles and responsibilities of
all Ministries and Government Agencies, and their constituent units, are published in the Official Gazette
when a modification occurs. The Ministry of Public Administration maintains a proper record of all the
various *cadres organiques'. However, graphical organization charts documenting all the positions, their
budget number, and the incumbents, are not systematically available. Neither the internal structure, nor
- 7 1 -



ANNEX.
Civil Service Reform
Page 15 of 20
the staffing levels, of the Ministries and Government Agencies are based on a thorough assessment of
their past or emerging needs, supported by a well-defined methodology and by standards of comparison
In such areas as productivity measurement, spans of control, or documentation of qualification profiles
fbr the positions.
Job Descripins. Job descriptions for all job categories existing in the civil service have been
prepared and are regularly updated, when required. It is clear, however, that these documents are
prepared for the purpose of grading jobs. In their present fbrm, they cannot properly support a human
resource management system. If they document, in broad and generic terms, the roles and responsibilities
of each job category, they do not provide any position-specific detail. Further, the quWification profUe
attached to each job description mentions only the minimum level of education required for a specific job
category. It does not document personal aptitude and skills, specialized training, or type of experience
required for various positions.
Penrel Evaluation. Personnel evaluation of a sort is regularly and transparently conducted in
the civil service. Standard forms including well documented criteria are used, and the result of the
evaluation is reviewed by the evaluee who has a right to appeal to the level immediately above his/her
supervisor in case of disagreement. A minimum level of performance, as reported in the evaluation
system, is required for promotion to a higher step within the same grade. Promotion from one grade to
another is based on the results of standard tests administered within each Ministry. The present evaluation
system, however, is primarily designed to assess personal traits (e.g., discipline and assiduity) rather than
results achieved and the strengths and weaknesses of a particular incumbent in a given position. As a
result, It is a crude instrument for career planning and for the identification of individual tra:iing needs.
Project Description: Human Resources Management in the Civil-Service
Pect Compe. The project will assist the Ministry of Public Administration, in its role as
manager of the human resources of the civil service on behalf of Government or individual Ministries,
with the design and implementation of modem human resources management systems for the civil service.
The Govermnent has identified ortanization and manpower planning as the most critical area to be
addressed under the project, as the changing role of the State requires a significant adaptation of the civil
service. Accordingly, a methodology and the instruments conducive to quantitative and qualitative analysis
of the present and forecasted demand and supply of personnel in public administration are essential to
provide the data required for the development of comprehensive strategies and action-plans in the areas
of recruitment, training, productivity improvement, career and succession plans, redeployment and
alternative means of delivering services.
Short-term technical assistance frem personnel management specialists will be financed under the
project to: (i) assist the Ministry of Public Administration with the design and/or adaptation of
instruments and methods for organization design, human resources evaluation, career anc succession
planning, and training needs assessments; Cii) recommend the selection of one of the many well-designed
software packages which exist on the market to manage organization and human resource planning and
contmol; (ii) develop an acdon-plan for the implementation of the new instruments and methods; and, (v)
provide In-house training in the use of the new instruments for the staff of the Ministry of Public
Administration and for pronnel offics In the Mistries and other Goverment Agencies. Recipien

of this training would, in turn, train supervisors and managers throughout the central government. Ithe
Terms of Reference of the assistance are provided in Annex 4-D.
EstimatedLC. The level of effort has been estimated at 2 person-months for the design of the

S. CIVIL SERVICE TRAING PROGRAM
Although civil sevants enjoy a good reputation dating back to the colonial period, when Cape
Verdians were frequently seconded to administrative positions in various Porntguese colonies, the
efficiency and effectiveness of the Civil Service today is severely constrained by a shortage of
administrative, technical, professional and managerial skills. Moreover, as noted above, uncontrolled
growth in government employment in the 1985-90 period diluted the average skill level of civil servants.
Only 12 percent of the 5,507 employees hired over the past five years occupy positions requiring at least
a secondary school education.
Technical/Professinal C=kp  With Bank assistance, an assessment was made of the technical
and professional capacity of civil servants in the six ministries included in the civil service reform
component: Ministries of Education and Sports; Fishery, Agriculture and Rural Development; Health;
Infraructure and Transport; Public Administration; and Tourism, Industry and Commerce. The level
of technical and professional skills is relatively low in these ministries. The proportion of staff who have
attended professional or university level education is less than nine percent. While the computerized
personnel information system does not contain details on the specific professional education of civil
servants in relation to the area in which they are working (e.g. agricultural economist or irrigaton
engineer in the Ministry of Fishery, Agriculture and Rural Development), information collected in
bierviews with personnel managers in the line ministries indicates that less than two percent of those civil
servants who have professional or university education, graduated in a technical/professional field related
to their work.
The poor fit between the professional and technical jobs civil servants hold and the formal
education they have received is partly explained by the fact that Cape Verde does not have a university
because of the small size of the university-age cohort. The country has had to rely on foreign
scholarships which have not produced the number and type of professionals required in the public sector
because of aurition, brain drain, and ineffective coordination and management. In addition, the Institute
for Professional and Administrative Training (IFAP), which was established by the Government in 1982
for the purpose of providing professional training, has not been able to develop programs which
corresponded to the skldl needs in the line minitries. IFAP has not had management with the vision and
the analytical capacity to determine current and future requirements for technical and professional skills
In the Government and to prepare and offer programs which would respond to that need.
Existdn and Planned Programs. The Government has begun to address the need for mid- and
higherlevel relevant professional skills. The skills development and training is being done in the context
of feign-aided development projects executed by the line ministries. For example, staff of the Ministry
of Education and Sports are receiving training in education-specific skills under the Bank-supported



ANNEXA
Civil Service Reform
Page 17 of 20
Primary Education Upgrading Project (Cr. 1853-V). This training will be continued and expanded in
a proposed follow-up project. Similarly, the management staff of the Ministry of Infrastucture and
Transport will receive training in relevant skills under the recently signed Transport Infrastructure Project
(Cr.2466CV). The staff of the Labor Department of the Ministry of Justice and Labor will be receiving
professional training under the Proposed Professional Education Support Project. In addition, the
Misries of Public Administration and Internal Administration have on-going arrangements with their
sister ministries in Portugal for professional staff training.
To ensurw: the effectiveness of this on-going professional training of line ministry staff, two issues
- coordination and evaluation - need to be addressed There Is currently no system in place which
allows the periodic monitoring and evaluation of the impact of this training to determine first if the
training is effective and staff are assigned to the pobs for which they were trained, and second if the
professional skills level in the line ministries is reaching the required level. The Government is,
however, planning to establish a national training and employment committee under the Proposed
Professional Education Support Project and one of the tasks of this committee would be to monitor and
evaluate professional education and scholarship training.
In view of the planned and existing support for professional skills trainiing, the Government
inends to focus the training under the civil service reform component on the development of
administrative skills suited to a modern service-oriented civil service. Thus, with the exception of
training for economic planning, statistics, budgeting and accounting, and the judiciary - which is
supported under separate components - professional training of civil servants will not be undertaken in
the Public Sector Reform and Capacity Building Project.
Administrative Canacitv. According to the above-mentioned training needs assessment, civil
servants in the six line ministries have relatively low levels of education. Approximately 25 percent have
completed primary school (six years of schooling), but more than 30 percent have less than primary.
Primary education, however, is not sufficient for the general administrative tasks these civil servants have
to do. It is particularly insufficient in the area of communications: speaking and writing in Portuguese.
Only two percent of the staff have completed high school (twelve years of schooling) and only
19 percent have had some years of secondary schooling. A significant proportion of the civil servants
with some or complete high school education are filling supervisory positions but their education is
inadequate for the planning, budgeting, accounting and record-keeping tasks they have to do in these
positions. As a consequence, accounting standards are low and most line ministries face problems in
balancing budgets, keeping track of expenditures, organizing work and meeting dead lines.
Approximately nine percent of the civil servants have the equivalent of a bachelor degree or
above. These civil servants are mostly employed as departmental managers (digente). The university
level education of the managers is generally good but few have had specialized administrative or
management training. Most managers consequently have weak project and personnel managemeat skills.
The limited capacity of managers to effectively organize their departments, and mobilize and motivate
their staff, is one of the reasons for the relatively low productivity in the Government despite high levels
of overing.



ANNEX 4
Civil Service Reform
Page 18 of 20
The above skill deficiencies were confirmed in interviews with personnel managers in the line
ministries. In addition, the personnel managers noted that several ministries are introducing computers
for planning, budget and word processing purposes but few employees have had training in computer
skills. Managers are now faced with the need to train their staff in informatics skills from data entry and
word processing to data-base and project management.
Administrative Training Plan. The Government has proposed a training plan for three levels of
administrative staff in the line ministries: managers, supervisors and administrative support personnel.
The plan proposes training in communications and computer-related skills for administrative support staff
and first-line supervisors, and training in project management, budget and accounting and personnel
management for supervisors and managers.
The proposed training would be done locally at the Center for Administrative Training and
Development (CENFA), which was established in 1984 for the purpose of raising the administrative
capacity of civil servants. CENFA already offers a set of basic administrative training programs, taught
by civil servants, but the Government proposes to adjust the program offerings to tailor them better to
the skill deficiencies of administrative staff. IFAP also offers a number of relevant training programs
(e.g., English language, typing and secretarial skills) but their programs also need to be revamped to be
more in line with the government's skill needs.
The details of the proposed training plan are shown in Annex 9 (Modernization of the Civil
Service). The training appears to be relevant and affordable and would improve the efficiency and
productivity of the civil servants if properly executed. CENFA and IFAP will require short-term
advisory services to be able to develop and conduct this kind of training. However, not all training needs
to be delivered by the two institutions. A significant portion could be offered by private proprietary
training centers, particularly in the areas of computer-related skills and project management.
The Government has agreed to prepare a strategy and action plan for the civil service training
program with a timetable for courses to be offered during the first year of the project. It has also agreed
to name a high-level official in the MPA's Human Resource Directorate as the coordinator of all civil
service training in management, professional, technical, and admininstrative skill areas that cut across
ministries. It is expected that this official will require short-term advisory services (4-6 person-months)
since there is at present no one responsible for the training function in the MPA. The MPA's training
coordinator will work closely with the personnel management specialists provided under the Human
Resources Management component to develop a medium- and long-term strategy for to strengthen the
training coordination and management function in the MPA and to forge tight links between training,
promotion and career development. Since developing the training function within the MPA will take
time, it is anticipated that the MPA may seek to create an advisory committee, comprising members of
the training delivery institutions, the private sector and relevant ministries, to advise it on the
development of standards for curriculum content, testing and evaluation, certification and placement and
monitoring.
Tralninf and Sunport for the Voluntary Departure Program. The size of the civil service has
grown dramatically in recent years (para. 2.7), especially in the lower-skill categories, and now exceeds
the requirements for staff in most line ministries. The excess staff are primarily found in the lower



ANNEX 4
Civil Service Reform
Page 19 of 20
ranks of the civil service and they generally have low levels of education. The majority have not
completed primary school. To bring the staff levels in line with the requirements, the Government
intends to offer a Voluntary Departure Program (VDP) to reduce the number of lower-level staff in three
categories - auwilar, operarlo, adinbsratdo - by roughly 1,700, or 50 percent of all the employees
in those categories. An early retirement option may also be offered and involuntary termination of
contract with benefits is being considered only as a last resort. The civil servants who opt for the VDP
would receive a lump sum payment and monthly subsistence payments for up to three years.
In addition to the monetary incentive package, the government is considering offering VDP
participants one or more of four possible training and support options: (a) a primary education completion
package; (b) a lower-secondary education completion package; (c) a one year subsidized employment and
on-the-job training package; and (d) a business advisory package for qualifying candidates.
The implementation and financing of the VDP program would be assisted under the project and
consultants would be recruited to develop the stratey and work out the details of the various packages.
The broad outlines for the training and support packages would be as follows: a provision of up to a
maximum of three years of primary education for those who have not completed primary, and up to three
years of secondary for those who have completed primary. An estimated, 1,000 civil servants are
expected to qualify and apply for the first package and about 300 for the second. Both primary and
secondary completion programs would be offered after regular school hours, taught by regular and
qualified school teachers on a voluntary basis in existing schools all over the country. The curriculum
would be the standard one and so will examinations and tests. Participating civil servants would pay
small monthly contributions which would be refimdable at the end of a successfully completed school
year.
The subsidized employment option would be available to civil servants who have been able
themselves to find an employer who is witling to employ them on a minimum salary and provide on-the-
job training in return for the monthly VDP payment. The employment would have to be bona-fide,
governed by a contract guaranteeing at least twelve months of continuous employment and on-the-job
training of at least five hours per week. Adherence to contract terms would be subject to inspection by
specially appointed inspectors.
The business advisory package would be available to civil servants who are able to produce a
business plan that meets minimum standards of viability, affordability and sustainability. Civil servants
whose business plan meets those stadards could elect to receive specified (minimal) levels of assistance,
support and training fom business counselors - ideally recruited on a voluntary basis from the business
community - in three areas: marketing, basic finance and book-keeping for small businesses. Civil
servants with qualifying business plans would be allowed to convert the monthly payment portion of theit
VDP package into a block grant for the start-up of their businesses.



ANNEX-4
Civil Service Reform
Page 20 of 20
Project Description: Civil Service Trainine
Project Component. To strengthen the administrative skills and capacity of civil servants in the
participating line ministries and assist the civil servants who would opt for the Voluntary Departure
Program, the training under this component would include:
(a) For the Ministry of Education and Sports, five local training programs for: 18 managers and
supervisors in planning, budgeting and accounting and word processing; eight administrators and
inspectors in informatics and supervision; and, for 36 support staff in building maintenance.
(b) For the Ministry of Fishery, Agriculture and Rural Development, local training programs for:
36 managers in planning, HRD and project management; 48 supervisors in informatics and
management of agriculture and public works projects; and, 180 administrative staff in
informatics, communications, and accounting.
(c) For the Ministry of Health, local training for: 24 managers in budgeting, HRD and Project
management and a seminar in Policies and financing of National Health programs; 84 supervisors
in maintenance, and accounting; and, 48 administrators in informatics, and communications.
(d) For the Ministry of Infrastructure and Transport Fical training for 24 administrative staff in
HRD management, and Communications skills.
(e) For the Ministry of Public Administration local training for: 4 managers in HRD and Project
Management; 8 supervisors in HRD Management, and public administration management; and
for 6 administrators in informatics, and communications.
(f) For the Ministry of Tourism, Industry and Commerce training for: 6 managers in Project and
HRD Management and a National Workshop in Tourism Promotion; 12 supervisors in Project
Administration, and Communication Skills; and 12 administrators in informatics, and
communications.
(g) For civil servants in the Voluntary Departure Program education and training for; 1,000 in
basic education; for 300 in lower secondary education; for 300 in on-the-job training as part of
subsidized employment. In addition, an estimated 200 may make use of the business advisory
program.
The bulk of the proposed training Is available from private proprietary training centers and from
modified courses at IFAP and CENFA. Training would be offered in the evening after work.
Responsibility for coordinating the training and developing annual training plans for the civil service
would lie with the Training Coordinator in the MPA's Human Resource Directorate.

REPMUBLC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACfTY WUILDING PROJECT
CmL SERVICE REFORM COMPONENT
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR THE
DESIGND OF  Yl VI,UgS  DEPARTURE PROG;;
BACKGROUND AND OBJECTIVE
The Goverment of Cape Verde intends to reduce the size and modify the stfing mix of the civil
service to levels which are financially sustainable, more productive, and compatible with the new role
of the State in the economy.
To the extent possible, Government wants the staffing reduction to be voluntary. For this purpose,
i intends to offer a compensation package sufficiently attractive tD induce potential beneficiaries to leave
seure jobs, and look for employment opportunities outside he public sector. It is also considering
accopng   measures such as support for demand-driven tainig and retraining schemes, and for the
creation of small and micrc 4aterprise by former civil servans, to facilitate redeployment.
'Me Ministry of Public Adminion is preparing a detailed strategy for the implementation of a
Vo1unv Departure - _ gnm (VDP) targeting the three lowest categories of personnel of the public
administration (Auxiliar', "Operario' and uAdministrativo'). Financial resources permitting, the
objective is to induce the departure of rouglly 1,700 staff in these three categories. A computer
simuation model has already been developed to cost the VDP, based on alternative packages and numbers
Of departees.
The role of the consultant will be to test the responsiveness of the target group to various possible
compensaton packages and, through an inthe-field survey, to identify the accompanying measures which
might facitlitate reentry into the labor market of personnel in the target groups.
TASKS OF THE ICONSULTANT
lhe consultant wil:
- Meet with the MPA task force charged with planning the desigp of the VDP to become
thoroughly acquaied with the rationale for the program, Its objectives, the various packages
proposed by government and the simulation model used to cost them, and all other relevant
InformLtion;
- Build and pre-test a questionnaire designed to evaluate the responsiveness of the target group(s)
to different types and levels of incentive packages Oump-sum, monthly payments, targeted
training, or any combination);
- 100 -



Civil Service Refonn
Page 2 of 3
- Build and pre-test a questionnaire designed to evaluate the responsiveness of the target
group(s) to different types and levels of incentive packages (lump-sum, monthly
payments, trget  traing, or any combination);
- Select a representative sample of personnel in the target group, and conduct interviews,
based on the above questionnaire;
- Prepare basic education and skills profile of the personnel in the target groups;
- Assess, through a field survey, potential demand in the fornal and informal sectors for
the type of personnel targeted under the program;
- Analyze the result of both the interviews and the labor market survey, with a view to:
 identifying the least cost/best result combination of monetary and non-monetary
incentives required to convince the number of employees targeted under the program to
voluntarily leave public administration;
* evaluating the potential for retrenched staff to set-up small and micro-enterprises, and
identiying the type of advisory and training assistance which might be offered to
facditate their start-up;
* recommending the tpe of training to be offered to potential beneficiaries to prepare them
best for existing and emerging job opportunities in the private sector;-
-Assist the Ministry of Public Administation with the prepation of the details of the
VDP progam, including the level and type of the final financial and training package
that the Ministry should recommend to the government, the criteria for participation in
the program, the deailed costs, and the program implementation procedures and
monitoringlcontrol mechanisms.
DURATION OF THE ASSIGNMENT
The level of effort for this assignment is estimated at 2 person-months. It will require some
travel within Cape Verde to test the labor market.
QUALICATIONS REQUIRED
ITe study will be conducted by local consultant(s) selected under IDA's procurement procedures.
ITe consultant(s) must have strong quantitative analysis skills.
- 101 -

Prior practical experience is mnndatory in:
- the sampling and design of interview questionnares,
- the conduct/supervision of intemews in the field, and in
- the conduct of trang needs assessment.
Ihe consutant(s) must have prior experience in technical, professional and/or vocational
trining, and be thoroughly familiar with the formal and informal labor market in Cape Verde.

ANNEX 4-D
Civil Service Reform (TORs)
Page 1 of 2
REPUC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBUC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
CIVIL SERVICE REFORM COMPONENT
TERMS OF REFEIUNCE FOR THE DESIGN
AND IMILEMEWAIK0N OF- A HUMAN RESOUIWE MANAG5;ESSTME
OBJECTIVE OF TIE ASSIGNMENT
Although systems and procedures of personnel *managemento in the Civil Service in Cape Verde
are fairly advanced by Sub-Saharan African standards, they still remain basically static instruments better
suited to administering rather than to managing and developing the human resources of the public
administration.
As part of the broad objective of modernizing management of the civil service, the Government of
Cape Verde has identified organization and manpower planning as a critical area to be addressed. The
changing role of the State requires a significant adaptation of the civil service, both In terms of functions
and structure, and in terms of the necessary human qualifications.
Accordingly, a methodology and instruments conducive to quantitative and qualitative analysis of
the present and forecasted demand and supply of personnel in public administration are essential to
provide the data required for the development of comprehensive strategies and action-plans in the areas
of: (i) recruitment, (ii) training, (iii) productivity improvement, (iv) career and succession plans, (v)
redeployment and (vi) alternative sources of service.
TASKS OF THE CONSULTANT
The Consultant will assist the Ministry of Public Administration (MPA), in its role as manager of
the human resources of the civil service, with the design and implementation of modern human resources
management systems to foster an adequately match between the demand and supply of personnel, and to
identify actions to be taken to fill the gap, if any.
Specifically, the consultant will:
Phas  :
- assist the Ministry of Public Administration with the design and/or adaption of instrun.ents and
methods in the areas of ogization dein & manpower planning, pmsnnel evauaton, caree
and succession planning, and training needs  sessment
- recommend the selection of one of the PC software packages which exist on the market to
manage organization and human resource planning and control;
- 103 -



ANNEX -1)
Civil Service Reform (TORs)
Page 2 of 2
- develop a titne-bound action-plan, including annual milestones, to implement the new instruments
and methods of human resource management in the civil service; and,
Phase 2;
- provide in-house practical training, including hands-on workshops, in the use of the new
instruments to the staff of the Ministry of Public Administration, personnel officers in the
Ministries and other Government Agencies. Tley, in turn, would train supervisors and managers
throughout the Public Administration;
- assist the MPA develop and deliver briefing sessions for the top management team in each
Ministry, to explain the new system, and build commitment for implementation; and
- act as a catalyst, in close collaboration with the MPA, for Ministries to launch organizational and
management reviews, guided by the objectives and priorities of the government public serctor
reform program.
DURATION OF THE ASSIGNMET
The level of effort is estimated at 2 person-months for each phase of the assignment.
QUALIFICATIONS REQUIRD
The assignment calls for a Personnel Management Specialist, with at least 10 years experience in the
field. The specialist should have a high degree of fluency in Portuguese and/or French.
The consultant must have had direct line management experience as Director of Personnel, preferably for
a large private company. Ideally, the consultant will also have experience in managing personnel in public
sector organizalons.
Tne consultant must have a good klowledge of existing personnel management software packages for
micro-computers.
Proven ability and experience of the consultant in training personnel management officers is required.

REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBIC SECTOR REFORM & CAPACITY BUIING PROJECT
DECENTRALlZATION AND MNCIPAL SFRENGTHNIN
1. BACKGROUND
(a) Historical Evolution
The first local administrative units ("freguesias") were established soon after the colonizaion of
Cape Verde in 1462. At the local level, administrative responsibilities were then exercised by the
religious authority of the parish. In the course of colonial times, religious and administrative
responsibilities were separated.  Local adminstrative authority became vested in the 'Conseihs'
managed by locally elected "Camaras Municipais' (Municipal Councils) which had some degree of
autonomy from the central government in the management of local affairs.
Shordy after independence, the local administrative structure inherited from colonial times was
reorganized and local representaivity abolished in favor of a highly centralized one-party government
system. All members of the newly created local administrative entities ("Deliberative Council* and
"Administrative Secretariat%, both headed by a "Delegate for Internal Administration") were directly
appointed by Central Government. Tbe role and source of legitimacy of the "Delegate for Iternal
Administration", who alone possessed real exective power and authority in local affairs, was recognized
filly when his tite was changed to "Government Delegate".
At the same time, Central Government starWd assuming an ever growing role in all facets of
economic and social life and, with the geographical dispersion of the population of Cape Verde,
representation of central government ministries were established in the local districts. Existence of the
two parallel structures proved to be a source of conflicts at the local level. Deconcened unis of
central ministries soon gained power over the local administrative structures, as they received and
managed far larger human, physical and financial resources. lbis situation led, in most cases, to a
gradual withering away of the influence and activity of the local administve structures which,
notwithstanding the fact that they spoke on behf of central government, were also providing a bouom-up
channel of communication between the population and the Govermment on local issues and concems.
Decentralization of a wide range of local power; and responsibilities to the Municipalities,
managed by locally elected representatives and executives, figured prominently in the program of the
party which came to power as a result of the first multi-party democratic electoral process in Cape Verde.
Following the presidential and legislative elections at the beginning of 1991, democratic Municipal were
held for the fst time since Independence on December 15, 1991. The elections through universal and
secret ballot created: (i) a municipal assembly in each district, whose members are elected by proportional
representation; and (ii) a city council, whose members, including the mayor, are elected on the basis of
the majority of the vote. The whole process elicited an immensely positive response from the population.
- 109 -



ANNEX S
Decentralization
Page 2 of 18
(b)Policy Framework for Decentaization
The objectives of Cape Verdes decentralization policy are essentially five-fold: (i) to enable
access to political and economic decision-makers for a majority of Cape Verdians; (ii) to increase direct
popular participation in programs directed to meet "basic needs"; (ii) t abolisb the parallelism between
Central and local government units at the municipal level, especially vis-a-vis the functional areas tageted
for decentralization by Decree, Law 52-A/90 (health & sanitation, urban infrastructure, social services,
etc.); (iv) to restcture the allocation of resources and increase the efficiency of resource utilization; and
(v) to Improve managerial and admnstrative efficiency by providing for flexible adaptation to unique
circumstances which, in different locations, might affect development efforts in diffaent ways.
Cape Verde's decentralization efforts are consistent with its overall economic reform program.
An important feature of that broader policy framework are principles concerning the changing roles and
responsibilities of the Government of Cape Verde (GOCV) and the expanding role of the private sector
(both in the sense of private commercial enrepreneurship and voluntary community initiatives). Ihe
fundamental instional aspect of Cape Verde's overall economic policy framework is that the public
sector is shiftig from altempts to control economic bebavior through inappropriate (market-adverse)
regulation andlor through direct and dominant participation in the production of goods and services to
the more limited role of providing an enabling environment for private sector economic development and
competition, thus improving economic efficiency and promoting sustainable growth. This requires the
public sector to limit and reorient its role to: (i) establishing appropriate policies and regulations for the
efficient and effective operation of market mechanim; CH) developing human resources; fiii) faciing
the construction and maintenance of appropriate physical infrastrucure; and (iv) facilitating the provision
of appropriate (i.e. demand-driven & quality) services.
Cape Verde's economic policy framework provides implicit criteria for evaluating the appropriate
role of Govermnent in the economy. Ihus, while the GOCV is responsible for financig 'public* goods
an services, it need not be directly involved in the actual production of the goods and services it
finances. From a pracdcal perspective, 'publice goods and services are understood to be those which
are deemed to be high priority for economic growth and public welfare, which the private sector cannot
reasonably be expected to provide in sufficient quandty (and at an affordable costs). Distinguishing
between financing and producing public goods and services help to identify those functions which ought
to be reassigned to the private setor. t also helps to assess the potential impact of alternative
assignments of functions to local governments to achieve economic policy objectives.
(c) Igal F:ramewvork for DecentI&WaoU
Title IV of the recendy adopted Constitution provides the framework for the new decentralized
political and administrative organization of the State. It enshrines the creation and staus of the
Municipalities as autonomous public entities, representative of the local population for prosecution of
loeal interests. The constitution also creates a Regional Affahirs Council which must be consudted prior
to the approval by the National Assembly, or the Government, of legislation or regulations having an
iVpact on the Municipalities, including regading local finances and the national and regional development
plans. The Consiuton stresses the need to promote solidarity among municipalities, and to reduce, as
far as possible, the regional imbalances in national development. It also recognizes that Municipalities
are entitled to receive technological, material and human support to carry out their responsibilities.
- 110 -



ANNEX-
Decentralization
Page 3 of 18
The Constitution clearly spells out the respective areas of responsibility, and the authority of the
legislative, executive and judiciary branches of govermment and recognizes the principles of separation
and intdependency of powers. The Govemment is the supreme agency of Public Administration,
which defines, leads and execute general internal and exteral policies, and is responsible before the
National Assembly (N.A.). Members of the N.A. are elected for a S year's mandate, and the N.A. has
exclusive competence in areas such as the electoral laws, issues of sovereignty, and the Municipal Base
Law. With regards local goverment, only the N.A. can legislate on issues such as the creadon or
modification of Municipalides, on the electoral process, on the organization of decentralized government
units, and on taxation.
Government, through the Council of Miister, can legislate on issues regarding its own
organization and modus operandi, and pass decree-laws in affairs not exclusively reserved to N.A., or
on issues for which it has received a special legislative mandate from the N.A. (implementation of the
privatization program for instance). Government Members (Ministers and State Secretanes) are
competent to exercise administrative and executive functions within their Mmistries.
The Judiciary is organized in a Supreme Court (along the US model), Courts of First Instance,
Tribunal de Contas (equivalent to the Auditor's General). In addition there are other courts such as a
Military Court and Fiscal and Customs Courts.
Subsequent to the Presidential and Legislative elections of 1990, Decree-Law n° 52-A/90 of July
$, 1990 (Legislagao Autrquica), built upon the framework for decentralization defined in the Municipal
Basic Law (Law ne 471111/89) passed in 1989, but never actually implemented. It created the Municipal
Assemblies (Assemblela Municipal) as the legislative branch of local govermnent, and the Municipal
Council (Camara Municipal) headed by a Mayor (Presidente da Cfmara Municipal), as the executive
branch of local government. his law also defined the role and responsibilities of the Municipalities with
regards: (i) their power to legislate in domains which are not of the Central Government or N.A.'s
spheres of competence, (ii) their local regulatory powers; (iii) their executive powers; Civ) their
patimonial, fiscal, fmancW and administradve powers; (v) their power to approve local developmen
plans and to participate in the elaboration and implementation of national development plans; (vi) their
power to define and execute local development projects; (vii) their power to establish, within the
franework of the Law, incentives and other mechanisms susceptible to attract investments and human
resources required for the development of the Municipality; (vii) their power to run or to supervise the
activities of public services (such as water and electricity supply) within the Municipalities; and, (viii)
their power to establish relations of cooperation with other.domestic or foreign Municipalities.
(d) Relationships between Centrl & Local Goveune
Bfflgiol Planning. The Ministry of State for Economic Coordination (Ministerio de Estado e
da Coordenaclo Economica) is responsible, inter alia, for the preparation and coordination of the
implementation of the National Development Plan. The role of Municipalities in the design of the
regional development plans is recognized by Law. However, very few of them are equipped to
constructively dialogue with the Directorate General of Planning (Direc;1o Geral de Planeamento) on this
issues.

Financial and Fiscal Control. The General Inspection of Finance (InspecCio Geral de Finan;as -
IGF) in the Miisy of Finance is the centu   orgaism of fiscal and financial contol. Among other
fimcions, k controls the paimonial and fiancial management of the Municipalities, including those of
the municipal services, municipalized enterprises and municipal associations. This control is exercised
by the Admsrative Public Sector inspection (Inspec;Io do Sector pdblico Administrativo - ISPA). In
addition to financial control, the Court of Accounts ("Tribunal das Contas') audits and certify the
accounts of the Local Governments.
P.,rsonnel Management. The Ministry of Public Administration and Parliamentary Affairs
(Mi ostro da Administraglo Pdblica e Assuntos Parlamentares), through the Directorate General of
Public Administration (Direcgo Geral da Administracao Pdblica), manages the civil service. For the
time being, a single employmett statute covers all civil servants, whether they work in central or local
adminitrtons.
While Decree-Law 52-A/190 provides the legal background for decentralization, the acua transfer
of responsibilities and powers from the Central Government requires a series of "D6cres d'Application"
(Regulations). These will be issued gradually in the context of agreements between central ministries and
municipalities defining the extent and modalities of transfer of fnancial and human resources. This
process is essential to ensure that Municipalities are indeed equipped to handle their new responsibilities,
and take over the provision of services from the deconcenuated agencies of central ministries in an
orderly and effective manner.
Role of Ministry of Inte_na Affar. On behalf of Government, the Ministry for Iternal
Adminstraion (MIA) is in charge of assisting the local and centr government units concerned organize
the transfer of fimctions and resources from deconcenrated agencies of central Ministries to the
Municipalities, and of supervising the timely implementtion of decentralization. However, much remains
tD be done to ensure that the process is conducted in an orderly manner with due regards to the financial
and human capacity of the Municipalities to face their new responsibilities. In particular, the Directorate-
Genea for Local Administion (Direcogeral da AdministragUo Local) in the MIA is responsible for
the evaluation, coordination and promotion of those measures conducive to the reinforcement of local
adminio, and the strengthening of the relationships between Central and Local Administration, and
between Municipalities themselves.
The MIA also exercises an 'adminstrative tutelle' on the Municipalities, as it must ensure that
local governments act within the powers they are granted by Law. The definition of "tutelle" is not too
clear, however, and de facto, some municipalities still wait for approval from central government on
certain major decisions Cm the areas of municipal budget and investmens plans, for instance).
(e) l l Ognztn of LocalG ne
Officials of local governments are elected for four years, by direct, secret and universal ballot
out of plurinominal lists. Municipal Assemblies have between 13 (m small municipalities with less than
10.000 inhabitants), and 21 members (for those who have up than 30.000 inhabins). Municipal
Council, inluding the Mayor, range from S to 9 members.

Legpslative branch. As the legislative branch of local government, the Municipal Assembly
debates on all but day-to-day issues affecting the Municipality, and control the activities of the Council
which is the executive branch. Municipal Assembly are required by Law to meet at least twice a year,
in February to review the Accouns and Annual Report of the Council, and in November to approve the
Budget and Plan of activity for the next year. The Assembly is free to organize additional meetings, as
required. Decisions are taken at the majority of the vote, assuming that a quorum is reached. lhe
Municipal Assembly reviews, and votes on, all proposals submitted by the Council regarding the
organization of municipal services, the municipal investment and operating budget, the program of
activities and services to be provided by the Municipality, and the staffiong.
Exeuiyve branch. 'he Municipal Council is an executive collegial organism headed by the
Mayor, which organizes and supervises the execution of local government responsibilities (pelouros).
It meets twice a month, and can only deliberate with the majority of its members present. Municipal
Councils look over: the organization and management of municipal services, finances, planning, ubansm
and construction, and local regulations within the mandate received from the Municipa Assembly. 'Me
Mayor has day-to-day authority to manage the municipal services, and to run the municipal
administaion.
Municipalities have finacial and patrimonial autonomy, i.e. power to approve and execute
budgets, to levy local taxes (within the general Fiscal Law passed by the N.A.), to realize investments
(although the coordination mechanisms between Local and Central Adminisions with regards local
investment are not yet clearly established) and to borrow (although this last issue is not yet propedy
defined). Municipalities can decide to operate industrial or commercial activities (water supply; electrica
energy production and distnbution; passenger and freight transport; workshops),- or to contract out the
provision of these services to private enterprises.
*1 Retadonhip betw Ieenloa ,eovenmn and their Constients
Loca organs of government have clearly demonstrated their commitment to public participation
in local affais, and to the transparec of their actions. It is clear that most locally elected officials have
been extmely concerned to provide the maximum information possible to the general public on local
afiirs, and have gone to great length to make themselves available to their constituents. Meetings of the
Municipal Assemblies are usually open to all, and the public is encouraged to intervene for questions and
aoswers sessions, most mayors reserve at least two half-days a week to meet, on an individual basis, with
private citizens, socio-economic groups (formers, fishermen, traders, etc) are encouraged to meet with
the Municipal Councit on a regular basis, and a number of information builetins are now published
regularly by the Municipalities, notwithstanding severely limited resources.
In addition, some municipalities are themselves engage: in a process of deconcentration, as they
are responding positively to, or in some cases actively encouraging, the creation of infra-mumcipal
organisms with the objective to ensure active and voluntary cidzens' participation in public activities, and
to bring local adminuration cdoser to the population it serves.

(g) Relationshipe with Donor Agencies. NO0s.
Municipalities have been 'adopted  by Bilater  Donor Agencies and NGOs that direct their

these actions to Improve resource allocadon within national priorides. This coordination should be

2. ISSUES TO BE ADDRESSED
(a) Financial Issues
Sources of Revenue
Cape Verdian municipalities are financed by differt sources of income, both collected by te
Local Goveruments or transferred by Central GovemenL  'Me so called 'municipalized taxes' are
actually collected by the Central Govemrnment, through Its regional agencies, but automadcally transferred
to the local ones, on a monthly basis. The reason for this Is that they were formerly Ceit .I Government
taxes that were passed on to the municipalities after the elections, tO give thew .-me financial
Independence, but since the local governments do not have the capacity to levy these X& s, they are stll
being collected by the local agencies of the Dlregao Geral de Contribuicoes e Impostos - DGCI, of the
Finance Ministry. In 1992 there were 7 of them: Local Development Tax, Urban Property Tax, Rural
Property Tax, Poperty Transfer Tax, Car Registron Tax, Fire lnsurance Tax and the Sugar Cane
Production Tax. In 1993 the tax on sugar cane ceased to exist with the creation of the Industri Tax at
the Central Goverment level. This should reduce the revenues for some municipalides, specily Ribeira
Grande and Paul, in the Island of Santo Antao, where sugar cane is an iaportant crop. On the other
hand, a Tourism Tax is being created, at the Central Govermnt level, and 30% of its revenues should
be saed with the municipalities in which they wil be collected.
The income from the municipalized taxes, according to 1992 budget figures, accounted for 11%
of the total municipal revenues, or 14% of their recurrent revenues. They were specially importat oly
to the large municipalities like Praia (24% of total and 27% of recurrent) and Sao Vicente (21% of total
and 26% of recurent renes).
At the municipal level a large number of charges, duties, fees and fines are collected, resulting
from local regulaions, grand licenses, registtions, etc. Among them are charges on the occupation
and use of space on markets and fairs; use of public  ahous and butchery; authorizaton for the
use of public space, street vendor, and use of commercial publicity space; burial, grave and tomb use in
mmuicipal cemeteries; commercia licensing in general; grant of legal authorization for private occpation
of public domain, due to constuction work and building use. Also grouped in this revenue category are
the charges for the rendering of some public services by municipal organisms and workers. The revenues
from the above, in 1992, accounted for 10% of total municipal revenues, or 13% of their recurrent
revenues.

Another Important item of locally collected revenue refes to patrimonial inoome. Under this
category are grouped the revenues fom interest, dividends, profits, and ren. The income fxm
dividends and property rents are usually the most significant items, and this caegory of revenue is
specily important for the municipalities of Prala, Sao Vicente and Sal - these three municipalities alone
account for 84% of the total patrnidal revenue. For the 15 municipalities as a whole they accoun for
6% of tW  revenues or 7% of the recutrent ones.
The transfer revenes are essentially received from the Central Govemment through the Fuiancial
Assistance Fund (FAP), and only on an eventual and marginal basis from other sources such as foreign
aid and remittances. They correspond to almost 23% of the total revenues or over 28% of the recurrent
ones.
The present FAF structue was established by the Local Finance Law (Decree-Law N-101-0190)
and has corresponded since then to 6% of the total net tax revenue of the Central Govenment, that is,
not including the revenues from the municipalized taxes. The amouns foreseen in each year's budget
are guaraneed to be transferred, but if the tax revenue goes beyond that, the municipalities receive more,
in accordance to what was actually collected and in the same proportion as previously established. Ibis
riterium  has created some problems for the Central Govenment since the municipalities receive every
month 1112 oftheir FAF quota, and in some months the amoun that is actally collected runs below that,
and the loca govenments demand their budgetary quota, but in the monfts t  revenues are higher, they
want to receive the proportind distribution.
The distrbution crkeria of the FAP among the 15 municipalities are very objective, aldwuglh they
may change from one year to the other as part of the budgetary amangements that are approved by the
National Assembly.
'Me distribution criteria in 1992 was: 60% equally distbuted to each municipalIt, 20%
cording to population; 5% according to area; 3% inversely proportional to the revenues of the
municipalized taxes; 3% acoording to an isolation index ta benefitted only the 6 more distant
municipalities, considering Prala, Sao Vicente and Sal as refrence; and 9% as a coWect  index to
prevent municipalities from losing revemne in comparin with the previous year. For 1993 the
distrution criterium changed and became: 50% equitable; 30% according tO population; 5% according
to area; 5% proportionally to the share of young population (under 14) in each munIcipality; 5%
according to the number of parishes in each municipality; and 5% for the correction index. Although
objective criteria are used to distribute the FAF among the municipalities, changes should not be as
frequent as they have been, since it is an important revenue source for the smaller municipalities that have
to be more stable to allow a better programming and budgeting. Besides that, objective rules can not
change frequently, otherwise they will lose their objecdvity.
The most important source of revenue for the municipalities as a whole are the service charges,
e-Tring to the vaious services provided by the loca authorities. Among them, the water and electricity
services are the most important ones, except in Sao Vicente, Praia and Sal where they are rendered by
a public enterprise (ELECTRA). The other services are related to paking lots, cemeteries, makets and
fairs, use of public machinery and equipment, and compensation for services rendered to the public or
to government agencies. Since the plan of acounts presentv in use Is not very clear, some of the above
service charges, except for the case of water and electricr services, can also be regsterd in the
