category of duties, fees and fines. Tibs revenue item accoutted for 24% of the total municipal revenues
In 1992, or 30% of the recuren revenAues.
Abe sum of the above and other miscellaneous revenues correspond to the recurrent revenues that,
according to the available budget figures for 1992, amount to over 80% of total revenues, for the 15
municipalities altogether. Of this recurrent revenues, 58% is locally collected and the remaining 42%
correspond to transfers, essentially from the FAF or the municipalized taxes.
'he capital revenies derive from the sale of investment goods such as properties, equipment,
machines, vehicles or animals; capital transfers ftom the Central Govermment; revenues from financial
assets; or loans and other credit operations. For the municipality of Mosteiros the significant item of
capital revenue relates to capital ansfers, since it was newly created, in the case of Praia the revenue
f*om financial assets is significant, although roughly 50% of the income comes from the sale of real
estate, and in Santa Cruz the capital revenue derives almost exclusively from loans. Otherwise, the most
Imta  source of capital revenue, by far, comes from the sale of real estate by the local authorities.
On average, the capital revenues accounted for 19% of the overall revenues, of which 64% corresponds
to the sale of real estate. Althougb, on average, for the 15 municipalities the revenmes from the sale of
rd  ete accouted for 12% of total revenues, high values were observed for Santa Catarina (44%),
Tarf  (18%) and Sao Vicente (17%). These are proportionally high amounts that are not Ukely to
withstand for too long, as the stock of municipal land properties wfll diminish.
From the available budgetary inormation for 1992, formal borrowing does not seem to be a
sWficant item for the inancing of local goverments, since in that year only Santa Cruz and Maio relied
on them, and the total debt revenue corresponded to only 2% of the total revenues of the 15
mnicipalities as a whole.
Information on the level and structue of the revenues of the 15 Municipalities in Cape Verde,
Including tasfers from cent  government, is presented in Aonex 5-A.
Muidpal Expenditres
For the sake of analysis the genera administrative expenditures, whenever possible, were
separated from those relaed to urban services and works, and from the water and electricity services,
since the last two are specific activities that are very important for most Cape Verdian municipalities.
Ibis was done from the budgetary figures dtat are classified by agencies and economic categories, since
there is no functional classification of epditures at the local level in Cape Verde. Since the iternal
organization of the mudcipalities is not necessaily alike, the grouping of the expenditures in these 3
categories was a difficult task. Only the expenditwes unequivocally related to water or electricity were
grouped under one category, the same being done wi the ones related to urbm planning, services and
works. AU the others were oonsidered as general adminion expenditures, which compises not only
the purely  minisatve actions but also those in the fields of social promotiou, education, tourism,
ral devdopme, etc.
For the 1S municipalities as a whole, the general administration (GA) accoun  for 74% of total
expenditures, urban services and works (USW) for II% and water and electricity services (WES) for
15%. Of the rrent expendibures, 71% correspond to GA, 10% to USW, and 19% to WES. As for
- 116 -



ANN
Decentralization
Page 9 of 18
capital expenditures, 8.% are done by GA, 12% by USW and 7% by WES. As a whole, recument
expenditures correspond to 68% of total expenditures, of which 40% to wages and 28% to other recurrent
expenditures. Capital expenditures, therefore, account for 32% of total expenditures. This should be
considered a good performance index in any economy, but what stands out is the fact that USW and WES
account for only 19% of the capital expenditures. In the case of WES, one possible explanation is the
fact that most of the production and distribution services in Praia, Sao Vicente and Sal are not locally
provided. Besides, drilling of wells and major water impounding are done by Central Govermnent
agencies, thus leaving the municipalities mainly with maintenance activities. A similar situation should
be occurring with the electricity services, with the municipalities spending mairdy on maintenance and
fuel.
Wage bills are proportionally higher in the larger municipalities of Praia and Sao Vicente, while
at the same time investments are significandy lower.
Ihe above mentioned figures for the 15 municipalities as a whole indicate that the expenditure
structure is adequae, in the sense that the budgetary commitment with the wage bill is low (40%) and
the capital expenditures, that mostly correspond to investments are high (32%).
The service charges esdmated to have been collected in 1992 (CV Esc. 241 million) roughly
cover the overall costs of the Urban Services and Works and of the Water and Electricity Services (CV
Esc. 253 million. This does not necessarily means that these activities do not run on deficit, since there
are many different service charges besides the ones that specifically correspond to these services.
Nevertheless, it indicates an overall cost-recovery policy.
It can also be observed that the locally collected revenues corresponding to Duties, Fees and
Fines; to Patrimonial Income, and to the various Service Charges amount to CV Esc. 400 million which
rougbly corresponds to the overall wage bill of CV Esc. 397 million. In a cetain way, that leaves the
tansfer revenues and the municipalized taxes, that are not actually collected by the municipalities, to
cover the costs corresponding to the other recurrent expenditures, as well as for the investnems, since
the capital revenues of CV Esc. 190 million are significandy lower than the capital expenditures (CV Esc.
311 mUlion).
Information on the level and structure of expenditures of the 15 Municipalities in Cape Verde is
presented in Annex 5-:
Dependency and Suffciency
There has been a significant growth in the transfers from the central to local goverments in the
last three years. In 1992 the municipalized taxes and PAF transfers grew 37% if compared to the
transfers made in 1991, and there was a 33% increase from 1992 to 1993, 15.2% in the municipalized
taxes and 42.1% in the FAF, compounding a 82% increase in two years. The municipality of Pal
obtained an increase in its transfer revenue of only 47% as compared to Praia at received 115%. ITis
was essentially due to the changes in the distribution criteria of the FAF. A comparison of the transfers
in 1992 and 1993 is presented in Annex 5-C.
- 117 -



Decentralization
Page 10 of 18
The municipalized taxes account for roughly one third of these transfers and probably will not
increase significanty in the fte if they continue to be collected by the Central Government as they are
now. The reason for this is the fact that the Central Governuent has the burden of collecting these taxes
but receives no benefit from them, except a very small (0.5%) service commission.
ITe Financial Assistance Fund - FAF transfers presently correspond to 6% of the central
governmeat tax revenue, not considering the municipalized taxes. Although it is not a higb percentage,
it is very difficult to believe that the Central Government will be willing to significantly increase this
amount, unless new responsibilities are transferred to the municipalities.
The financial dependency of the municipalities on the central goverment is appareny not so
high, since for the 15 municipalities as a whole the municipalized taxes and transfer revenues correspond
to roughly 40% of their recurent revenues, although in the case of some municipalities such as Paul this
Is close to 70%.
This dependency is not larger because, If in one hand, the central govermnen transfers are not
so high (only 6% of its net tax revenes), on the other, the locally collected revenues are also low, which
brings up the question of sufficiency of funds for the financing of the local governments.
The average annual per capita recurrent revenue for the 15 Cape Verdian municipalities amount
to CV Esc. 2,305, that corresponds to approximately US$ 30, a very low value, considering that it is
only 4% of the country's per capita income (US$ 750). That revenue is ceraiy not enough to allow
the municipalides to finance all the services they legally should provide.
When new responsibilities ane passed on from the Centrl Govemment to the municipalities,
according to what is established in the country's legal fam=ework, there wM be a great increase in the
financing needs of the local governments th, for some time, can only be addressed by the increase of
tasfers. These additional transfers, however, will probably be limited to the present cost of the
deconceonated services presently rendered by the Central Government. In the future, the financing of
the improvement of the locally rendered services can only rely on the increase of the municipal fiscal
To increase locally ^ollected revenues it is first necessary to build up the managerial capacity at
the municipal level and that dtnands time and investments in training, technical assistance and equipmenL
The poliical will of the loca autites is also very important to achieve this, since it is always easier
to depend on the Central Governuent, and put all the blames on it for not having enough revenues, than
to tax their own contuents, neigbs that in small communities lmow each other by the name. The
results should not be  pected in the short run.    mus be undtersood that the improvement in the
municipal managerW  capacity is not onty needed to increase the local fiscal effort, but will be essental
for efficiently managing the significanty Increased trsfered funds that should follow  the
decentalization process.
AcoomXlailty and Efficdency
Increased local fisad effrt has the benefit of reducing the municipal dependency on central
govemment decidons that are often taken without considering the real local needs and constraints.
- 118 -



ANNE D
Decentrization
Page 11 of Is
Conversely it will increase the responsibilities towards the local constituents. That shall give more
political autonomy to the municipalities and increase the interest of the local population in directly or
indirecdy participatLg in government decisions, which leads to increased accountability and efficiency
in the allocation of public money.
For that purpose it is necessary to have adequate planning and budgeting instuments with
sufficient transparency to permit social control, as well as adequate monitoring and auditing of the ffscal
process - both revenues and expenditures. That means that they should be simple enough to permit the
average citizen to visualize where the money is coming from and where it is going, but also clear and
precise to enable for adequate auditing.
In the present situation the municipalities must submit yearly their budget proposals to the
Ministry of Internal Administration to be pre-approved, and very frequently they are inadequately
prepared and have to suffer several corrections. The plan of accounts presendy used does not help in
making the public accounts more transparent and provide almost no information on the local goverment
programs, projects and activities. In fact the only break-down given is according to the municipal organic
displacement (cabinets, agencies, servicesetc.) and economic categories of expenditures (salaries,
materis, equipment, interests, etc.). There is no cost accounting, so it is not possible to verify the unit
cost of the various services provided. The same, of course, holds for the balance sheets. Ma means
that formal auditing can be made but no adequate control and monitoring of the efficient use of public
money.
The Ministry Of tration provides help and orientation only in the preparation of
the budget, to assure that it is presented in the form determined by law. It makes no further follow-p
of the budget execution. The local government accounts, like the budget, are approved by the Municipal
Assembly in open hearings, where the public is allowed and can make questions and proposals about
them. After the approval, the balance sheets are sent to the capital, together with all the supportig
documens, to be audited and approved by the Accounts Court. This Court has a very formal and
legalistic approach in the auditing process but does not have the mas in terms of human resources,
facilities and equipment to perform a thorough audit. The lack of adequate reguations on various
subjects such as public contatng also impairs their activities. The recenty created Inspecao Gera de
Finansas, a finance inspecting agency of the Minstry of Finance, has the responsibilities for int
control and could, according to the law, inspect the municipal finances. Presently this is not happening,
although they collaborate wih the Accounts Court in other matters.
The newly elected mayors (Presidents of the Municipal Chambers) are also making an effort to
make their administrations more transparent to the public. They all hold public heaings with their
constitents at least twice a week; fix accounts, fmiancl reslts, bidding documents, reports, etc., in
bulletin boards in the City Hall; and most of the municipalities are now publishing a short bulletin, on
a monthly or quarterly basis, with infibrmation on their administration.
It is understood that in the decentralization process, that only now is begining to take place, the
services dtat are presenty rendered by the various deconcentrated agencies of the Central Goverment,
located in the municipalities, will be handed over together with their respective failities, equipment and
budgets. It is not clear yet how the personnel will be transferred, if it will be compulsory or not That
is the policy line presendy in course for the transfer of the Social Promotion serices, although no
- 119 -



AN=X i
Decentralization
Page 12 of 18
*Protocole d'Accord' has yet been signed with any municipality. Tbe signature of a "Protocol d'Accord-
with each municipality for the transfer of each service, establishing the physical and financial basis of the
tasction should be the procedure followed for the other services to be decentlized, to avoid the bad
experience of the first decetalization attempt made by the Ministry of Infrastucture and Transportaton.
This means that if everything goes as planned, the municipalities should not have, in a first moment, a
significant increase in their financing requirements, since the corresponding budgetary transfers fom the
Central Government should address this question.
One of the virtues of decentraization is that local decision-making, due to the proximity of the
direct beneficiaries, is usually more efficient. But on the other hand, this proximity will result In new
demands for the improvement in both quality and quantity of services rendered to the local population,
that a better and more efficient management alone will not be able to address, creating the necessity for
additional financial resources.
The only reliable source of additional financing that will ensure that, with the decetralizadon,
the locally provided services will be significantly improved, shall come from the increase in the municipal
fiscal effort
In a fist step the municipalities should be able to collect by themselves the municipalized taxes,
specially the Urban and the Rura Property Taxes. According to municipal officis the ptoblem wih
these taxes are not the rates but the assessment of the property value (tax base), besides the factthat many
of them are not registered and pay no taxes at all. According to technical advisors in the municipality
of Santa Cruz, for instance, it is estimated that the revenues from these two taxes can increase more than
ten fold, if a correct aessment of the property value is made. According to them, at the present moment
there are properties paying only CV Esc. 80 per year, a "ridiculouso amount in their opinion, given its
size and probable value. In 1992, the foreseen revenue with the Urban Property Tax in the budget of
Santa Cruz was of CV Esc. 1.6 million, only 3.5% of the recurrent revenues. Finally, a strong argument
presented by several municipal authorities is that only themselves have the necessary information to make
adequate assessment of the property values and the knowledge of the existing properies. All building
activides should receive licenses from the municipaliy and for that a small description of the project must
be presented and, futhberm, the water and electricity services, usually provided by them, is also a
good source of information on the existence of properties.
The Property Transfer Tax (SISA) wfll probably need cges in the legislation to be more
adequately collected. Since there are no First Class Notary's Office in every municipality (only in the
lager ones) most of the property transfers can not be controlled by the smaller municipalities, and when
the taxes are collected are usually in favor of the municipality where the Notary is located, and not
necessay the property.
It was observed the relatively high revenues provided by the several existing service charges,
duties and fees, dtat constitute a good indicator of the cost-recovery efforts of the local govemn.
Their large number, though, suggests that there might be excessive bureaucracy that could hamper private
economic activity. As soon as revenues from broader-based taxes increase, it will be most convenient
if a thorough review of the existig harges, duties and fees is made to reduce their number and review
and standardize their values, to mitigate the obstacles to private business and avoid excessive tax burden.

(b) 
Mbe lack of adequawe staffiDg at the municipal level is a known issue that since 1990 has been
addressed by the Govenment of Cape Verde hough two Decree-Laws to facilite and flnancy
stimulate the mobility of central government civil servants in working in the mnicipalities or t be
tranferred to them. T'e first one, enacted in 1990, presented a package of provisions to sdtmulate cidv
servants to serve In posts located in the municipatides other than the capital, such as automadc one post
promodon and pay increase and priority for spouse reallocadon, if also a civil serant  Te second one,
enactd a£ter the mwicipal elections, establishes a framework for the transference, on a pennanet or
seconded basis, of cental govnment civil servants to the municipal governments themseves, witb the
same stmulating provisions. The result is that it is always more convenient for a central pvermet
chi  servnt stationed in a municipal government, on a seconded basis, to remain with his original ties,
inad of becoming a municipal civil servan, since there will be no finacial gains.
The geneal statutory regulations for the civil servants are the same at both levels. Altough they
ae now two different categories, they have the same classifications and wage leves, which makls eati
the tmnrence possibities.
Even so, the difficulties for properly staffing both the deconcent_ted servces ad municipal
govem   ts are great. The smaller municipalities, other than Praia and Sao Vicente, have ltle to offe
both ir pofessional terms and prvate Iffe comfort, which the financial inceatives may not be enough to
offae.
For the techicians and qualiffied personnel the availability of proper housing condidons and
adequate work environment are conditions as important as wage levels. Both of these conditions ae
usualy nonexisting in the small municipalities.
The municipal governments permanent staff or cadres are usually small, and their staffig needs
are complemented by contracted workers, on an eventual or permanent basis, seconded central
pvenment officials, that benefit from the above mentioned financial incentives, and foreign adviso
From the Mistry of  enal  nisa ion alone there are 70 of their technical staff members
seconded to local govemmens at an anu  cost of CV Esc. 13.1 million. According to the law, tho
Contal Goverment should be reimbursed for their wage-bill, although apparently this is not the currt
pracce.
In the municipality of Sso Vicente, for Instmce, the local staff is composed of 196 cWv servants
(cadres), 240 contacted workas on a permanent basis and 11 seconded officials from the Minisy of
Internal Adminaton. In Porto Novo there are 35 civil servats (cadres), 9 seconded officials from
Mk, 36 contracd workers on a permnent basis and 6 part-time contracted technicians, of which I s
an nginewr, I architect, 1 topographer, 2 designers and I electricity techmician. Out of Porto No
cadres only 2 staff members concluded their primary education, and most of the others only studied up
to the 2nd gadel That means tha al th technical support available comes from seconded offas and
part-time hired staff.

Although there is no complete data on the municipalities staffing, both in teras of number and
level of formal education or training skldls, their needs of more qualified personnel are high, according
to what was said by all mayors during the visits to a s4mple of municipalities.
Since for most municipalites their core of civil servants is relatively small and their financil
means are tght, taning has been supply-driven, in the sense that their only alternative is when
oppornities of free or low cost training arise.
Another Issue that was raised during the interviews wh the mayors was the result of the
decenalization process conducted by the Ministry of Infrastructure and Transportation, in which
relatively large number of (contracted) workers were transferred to the municipalities. Although they do
not belong to the municipal cadres, there was an increase in the overall staffing, and most of these
workers are not qualified and belong to relatively high age brackets. The engineers and most of the
qualified personnel previously working in these services returned to their headquarters in the capital.
Since most of the workers were contacted in their places of origin, the mayors are reluctant to fire them
due to the social (and political) problems this might create. In fact, according to the mayors, it is a
cunt practice not to fire the workers contracted on a pemnanent basis, although since they are not civil
servants this could be more easily done.
According to the interviews with the mayors, the only way to tackle the problem of insufficient
qualified personnel is to provide financial incentives, adequate housing and workplace environment, which
involves proper facilities, equipment and training opportunities.
(c) Issues of FaWilities and Equipment
The December 1991 elections brought to power, for the first time in the history of Cape Verde,
elected mayors that since 1992 are facing the enormous challenge of governing in an envion_m   of
srcity of all means. The revenues are not adequate to render the services that the local population
demands from them, the staffig is frequently not sufficient and certinly lacks the skills that are
necessay for performing their duties. When it comes to the materia means, the situation is not differeat.
For most of the municipalities, specially the smaller ones, there is not even enough space in the public
buildings to hold-the minimum staff needed, not to say about the adequacy of the avaiable facilities.
Except in the case of the larger municipalities of Praia and Sao Vicente, in which the needs are
not so dramatic, all the others are severely ill-equipped. The municipalities of Sao Filipe and Boa Vista,
for instance, do not have a single photocopy machine, and have to use (and pay for) the machine of the
local office of the Bank of Cape Verde. Computers are nonexisting in more than half of the
municipalities, although all of them, wth the exception of Santa Cruz already have facsimile machines.
The few Items of office machines that are available has usually resulted from donations from NOOs and
other interationd donors. After all, for a municipality like Paul, buying a simple computer system (USS
4,000) would result in the commitment of 4% of the total investment budget for 1992 (US$ 100,000).
Even so, it is amazing the effort tx the smaller municipalities make to maximize the use of what
they have, and through improvisation try to compensate their shortfalls, delivering the best services that
are possible. During the field visis it was witnessed their improvising abilities for tuning out a

3. PROJECT DECPTION
The projec covers sevea  actions in order to pemit the muniiides to effiietly and
efiectivly take hold of not only their present responsibilities but the new ones that wil follow In tho
mework of the present political power division, set forth in the Consdtion, in which the
mcipaities are responsible for all that is related tO local population needs.
Griven the fact that the municipalities are in a severe shortage of means of al natures, it Is
necsay to gradualy buid their institudonal, manageri  and financial capacit, acording to a
cowdor  d plan, set forth in phases, that can permit the nesary susnabilky in the fc     Pdorities,
as wel as their sequencing, must be  tablished in order to ensure the capaciqy bulchng process.
Tbe spport program for decalization should establish actions both at municipal and centr
govrmen levels. The ibur field of action are: a) Instuional Buiding; b) Admi    o  and
_uqemwi; c) Hman Rsources; and d)  terna  Organization.
ntitukna Buildting involves three sets of actions: a) the establishment of guidelines for the
dcenization process; b) the strengthening of municipal associations to assure a Ujdcr mg W ta
thou  a dsang basis will allow for a raional and economically sound use of the scarcedy avalle
tehnial sUppo0t and equipment, with the creation of intermuncipal tenical support offices MSOs);
ad c) spport for the intra-municipal decoetron, that can permit a closer conact betwe  th
municpa auhrities and the populaion of the various villages and/or cIzens' groups, local community
ssocitions, etc.

Administration and Management involves the following three areas: a) planning and budgeting
systms, i order to assure the needed tansparency and accountability for the scal control and
monitorng of the fiscal process; b) fiscal effort, in order to assure the susnabiity of the
decenrization process and the financing of improved locally rendered services, involving the difent
means to increase locally collected revenues; and c) purchasing procedures, in order to obtain unit cost
reduction and effective expenditure controls.
Human Resources involves 3 areas: a) provide the municipalities with staffing in accordance to
their new responsibilities, and the viable actions that can be taken to attract and retain qualified personnel;
b) training of the existing staff and the future cadres at different levels and in various areas, in accordance
to a comprehensive program; and c) establishment of human resources management procedures to permit
the adequate use of one of their scarcest resources and to assure its permanent development.
Internal Organizaton involves 2 areas: a) organic restructring to permit the most adequate means
of organizing the various municipal activities, that could eventually be autonomous services or privately
contracted ones; b) material means to ensure that proper equipment and vehicles will be available, in a
minimum quantity, to the local authorities for the implementation of the proposed structures, systems and
aoos.

(a) Action-Program and Timetable for Implementation
The Project objectives and design take into account the issues and needs to provide support to

components and cost estimates are given below, as well as presented in Annex V.
(b) Toeuc; Assistance and Studiess
Four man areas where technical assistance and studies should be concentrated to ensure the
necessary sustainability in the future are: Institutional building; administration and management; human
resources; and inernal organization. For institutional building and human resources the project will fund
consultants to prepare specialized studies. Technical assistance will be provided to all municipalities to
help them implement management systems and procedures in planning and budgeting; local tax collection;
and purchasing techniques. The draft terms of reference for the studies and the technical assistance are
prented in Annex 5-D, and the estimaed cost amouns to US$ 718,000.
(c) Trining En
The training program is designed to train about 150 people in management, organization,
computes and operaonal activities related to the municipal responsibilities. Some wDIl receive fonnal
taining in specialized institutions in Cape Verde such as CENFA, others will be trained in the
municipalities themselves in locally held courses, but the majority will receive on-the-job training throu
the tchnical assisance provided under the project

REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACrfY BUILDING PROJECr
Decentralization Component
TERMS OF REFERENCE
ESTABLSHMENT OF GUIDELINES FOR THE TRANSFER
TO MUNICPALrTl
OF DECONCENTRATED SERVICES OF CENTRAL GOVERNMENT
BACKGROUND AND OBJECTIVE
Ihe promotion and strengthening of modem democratic local powers has lately become a
cornerstone of the socio-economic development strategy of the government. 'Me decentralization process
stared in eanest in 1991 with the first multi-party municipal elections, and the passing of a Law which
spels out the municipal responsibilities and powers. However, much remains to be done to implement
the provision of this law and, in particua, to ensure an orderly transfer of responsibilides and resources
from central to local governments.
The Ministry of Inunal Affairs has initiated discussions with representatives of municipal
governments for the transfer of some tasks which have traditionally been assumed by central government
line Ministries, such as social promotion, basic health, road maintenance, etc. However, the
municipalities have suffered a bad experience in the first attempt at decentralization, as they took over
new responsibilities without an adequate transfer of human, physical and financial resources. It is now
recognized th, pror to any further transfer, a general framework for the decentralization process must
bejoiny designed by the central and local govermments. This framework would establish the principles,
modalities, resources and timetable for action.
The purpose of this study is to document this framework in close collaboration with the Ministy
of Intenal Affairs and the Ministry of State for Economic Coordination (which mcludes the Finance
Ministry and the Planning Department), with the line Ministries concerned, and with representatives of
the municipalities.
TASKS OF TBE CONSULTANT
Tbe Consultant will assist the Ministry of Intemal Affairs and the Municipalities with the design
of standard procedures aimed at organizing in an orderly manner the transfer to the Municipalities of
acdvites which are presendy assumed, and resources controlled, by deconcentrated services of the central
govement.
- 134 -



ANNEX5S-D
Decentraization
(ros)
Page 2 of 14
Specifically, the consultant will:
-  evuate which acivitiesreources should be transferred to which Municipality In priority, based
on an assessment of the Municipalities' management capabilities;
- prepare a time-bound acdon plan for the orderly and firuitfil decentralization of responsibilities
and resources;
- recommend the procedure leading to the signature of a rPmocole d'Accord' beween the
Municipalities and the central government Ministries for the transfer of the latter's deconcentrated
activities, with a view of ensuring that the Municipalities receive the human, physical and
financial resources required to assume their now responsibilities;
-  document the format of a stadd "Protocole d'Accord'; and
- conduct a training seminar for representatives of central and local governments on the issue of
decentralization of deconcentrated services, and private provision of public services.
D:URATION OF TIM ASSIGNMENT
The level of effort is esdmated at 2 person-months.
QUAIFIUCATIONS REQUIRD
The assignment calls for a Specialist in Municipal Msnagement, with at least 10 years of experience in
the field. 'he specialist must have a high degree of fluency in Portuguese.
The consultant must have some direct line management experience in tie organization and delivery of
municipal services, preferably in the fields of civil works, and/or basic health services.
Exposure to experiences of private provision of public services, and faniarity with contrtng-out
arrangements would be an additional advantage.

BACKGROUND AND OBJECTIVE
The Central A      ion believes it is necessary to creae four agencies of the Ministry of
Iernal Affirs that would deliver services to four groups of Municipalities.
The assignment has the objective to assess the necessary conditions for the creation of inter-
micia Technic  Support Office(s) TSO) that will permit the radonal use of scarce technical and
human resources by small Municipalities.
The assignment would be conducted in close coUaboration with the Mayors and municipal
managers (Vereadors) of the small Municipalities, and with representatives of the Ministry of Intrnal
Affaim.
TASKS OF THE CONSULTANT
JeM  1: Identificatfon of the TSO's O   Dnizaon Setup
Durig the course of the assigmnent, the consultant will determine wbat are the necesary
conditions for having these lSOs controlled by the Municipalities themselves, what are the options for
the lines of subordinadon so that the TSO become hierarchically dependent of the Municipalities they
service, what is the real number of TSO hat are needed; where they should be located and which
Muncipaities sbould they serve; what functions should they perfom; what are the needs in tenms of
human resources and equipment; what operatng costs are likely and what are the alteraves for
fnancing.
DURATION OF THE ASSIGNMENT
The level of effort is estimated at 12 person-months.

TERMS OF REFERENCE
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE IN
WRNAIMMRE PLANNING AND IMPEMENTATION
BACKGROUND AND OBJECTIVE
Decentalization entails signiicant additional costs to the Municipalides which, at the national level,
will not be completely offset, at least initia1ly, by a parallel decrease In centr government savings. In
an economy such as Cape Verde, is makes it  all the more necesay t    ensure that the municipalities
maage, ther resources with maximum effectiveness and efficiency.
Municipal investm  Is at an early stage of development, but will increase with decentralization. At
pret,   those municipalities where it exiss at all, the investment planning horizon is one-year, and
there appeas to be some confusion betweea capita investment costs, operation costs and maince
coss.
Investment planning for services cannot be separated fom technical and economic evaluation of
alternatives. Increasingly, municipalities will be responsible for new rather than for on-going projects,
and it will be necesary for them to ensure that new projects and services are provided in the most cost-
effective manner.
In the fture, municipalities should bN equipped to:
- consider alternative technical solutions to detenmine the most cost-effective solutions;
- consider phasing of alternatives to avoid overdimensioned projects and conserve scarce resources;
-  include full costs, paticularly depreciaion, in the analyses of projects; and
-  evauate the financial and economic retun of altenadves.
Whie such analyses are cleary necessay for large projects, even in smaller municipalities technical
and eonomic evaluation are essential. The impact of infrastructure investmen on municipal budgets are
prportionally high regardless of municipality size, and the need to ensure *value for money is equally
Impora to small as well as to larger municipalities.

REPUBLC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACrrY BUILDING PROJECT
EN2TIllOALDEVELOMN: SIMAT-EGY TO) INCREAS E lMIC PARTWIPATIO
1.           The Government of Cape Verde (GOCV) is responding to two recent developments: first,
the need to rest  the economy in the face of a slowing of economic growth, growing budget deficits and
increasing inflation; and second, mounting pressures to accelerate the demo ion process which
began with the 1990 elections. To deal with the increasingly complex economic environment, worsenig
balance of tade and etal debt, the GOCV has embarked on a strategy to buid a much stronger, but
more focussed, markt-faciitating public sector. The strategy rests on two pillars. On the one hand, it
sees to Wm     the capacity of the Goverment in economic and fiscal management coupled with a
progam to moderne public administion and Improve the legal euviraonent for private business; and
on the other hand, to reduce the role of the Goverment in the economy and foster private sector led
investment and growth. To deal with the demand for faster democratzation and public participation, the
GOCV has began a proces of delegating more power and responsibilitiesto the municipal govenments,
and has initiated effrts to strengthen the capacity of the press to better inform the public about
government policies and programs to raise the awareness and involvement of the public in Government,
and to make the Government more accountable to the people by reporting on policy decisions and the
performane of government programs.
_Mp a Eoonomic ad ial Manant an Moernizina Pbic Adminilstration
2.           The Government of Cape Verde has achieved high levels of economic growth through
pdent economic and fiscal management and public investments. In addition, it has made subsial
iwsnes      in the social sectors which have resulted in levels of education, health and social services well
above the average in the Africa region. Much of the economic gowth was led by public investmenss
since the private sector is small and laking in entrepreneurial capacity and capital or assets for
borrowing. 'Me relafively small role and limited initative and capacity of the private sector led the
Govermet to crate an ndtetventonist   exeive public adminiton to manage the economy and
public investments, develop infucture and provide social services. It provided the rationale for the
cion of 36 public sector etrprises in power, water, and telecommunication utilities, light
_1,CU        ship building, food production and processing, and markime and air traspor
3.           Atpresent, the publicsector, includingpublic enteprises employs more than 19,000 civil
serVas. Tne machinery of goverment comprises thirteen ministries, two responsible for economic,
financi and fisca affais, and the others for defense, infrastructure, utlities, agriculture and fishery,
commerce and industries, and health, education, culture and sports. These ministries also mage the
36 public sector enterprises.
4.           Pagry to cope with the management requirements of this large public sector, and pardy
for political reasons, the number of civil servce in the Governmet minstries grew dramatically over the
- 154 -



ANINEX 6
Institutional D&v.
Page 2 of 4
1985-90 period, increasing fom 7,800 to 11,300 employees. Growth bas been concentrated in the lower
echelons ofgoverment: office staff and support workers with low educational qualifications. Only 1.2%
of the civil sevans have completed secondary school or twelve years of schooling, and less than 5%
have completed undergraduate univesity education, or 17-18 years of schooling. University trained
manager, tecbnical and professional staff are in short supply. Consequently, the capacity of the civil
service to manage the economy and the large public sector is weak
S.           The weaknesses in the public administration have recently become more apparent in the
face of a deteriorating economic performance, declining public investments and demand for maritime and
transport services, and worsening performance of the public enterprises. Faced with this situation, the
Goverment has felt it necessary to undertake a comprehensive program to improve the quality of its
economic and fiscal management, the efficiency of the public administration and the privatization of
public sector enterprises. Specifically, the Govermnent has embarked on a program to refom the
ministries responsible for economic and fiscal planning and management and has created a new ministry
to oversee and coordinate economic policy and development, the Ministry for Economic Coordination.
It has also reorganized the Ministry of Finance and is in the process of strengthening the capacity of the
planmng, budgeting and statistics departments of that ministry.
6.           In addition, the Government has prepared a plan to modernize the sectoral ministries
through a reform of public administration that focuses on retrenching excess lower-level staff, improving
personnel management, and training civil servants. Moreover, in 1992, the Government launched a
program to privatize public sector enterprises with assistance from the IMA-supported Privatization and
Technical Assistance Project (Cr. 2377-CV.
7.           The privaizaon project will help expand the private sector through the purchase of
public sector enterprises and assets by private entrepreneurs. To facilitate this process and to promote
private sector investments and grwth, the Government is also reforming the regulatory environment for
business. T'e reforms will streamline labor legislation and simplify regulations for entry and exit of
businesse. The development of the private sector will be further assisted by the establishment of a
commercW banking system and financial market for trading treasury notes, and eventually stocks and
bonds.
8.           The plans and proposals for improving economic management, reforming the civU service
and improving the legal and judicW system, are in various stages of preparation or completion. The
quality of the proposals is very good. Ihe statistical data base is weak and needs tD be improved and new
mechanms for coordinatmg and monitoring economic development programs and external assistance
need to be developed. Finally, the planning and execution of the reform program require support and
commitment from political parties, parliament and civil servants. To deepen and broaden this
commitment the Government has begun a program to improve relations with the press and give it better
access to infomation on the reform program so that the Government can reach a wider audience and
facilitate public discussion and scrutiny from constiencies and the public at large. Plans are also
underway to assist the print (newspapers) and electronic media (radio and television) in getting easier
access to Govenument officials and infmation, and in acquiring modern printing and broadcastng
tecbnologes.
- 155 -



ANNES 
Institutional Dev.
Page 3 of 4
Re=nding to the Demand for Accelerated Democratization
9.           Cape Verde has been at the forefront of Africa's political transition in the 1990s. hne
1991 elections brought victory for the opposithon party in races for the Presidency, National Assembly
and municipal councils. The political scene now comprises parties formerly in the opposition, the party
that ruled from independence until the recent elections, and several independent political groupings. The
transition of power was peaceful and the program of opening up the economy, which was relaunched by
the new Government, is taking place within a frame-work of constructive dialogue and debate, with the
active participation of the press.
10.          The new Government came into power on a platform of economic reform, employment
generation, a reduced role for the Government, delegation of power to local Government, and the
fostering of private initiative and enterprise. The economic reform program has is off to a sound start
and is expected to be sustained by the efforts to strengthen economic management, modernize public
administration, privatize public enterprises, promote private sector development and improve the legal
and judicial system. Increased public and private investments in infrastructure and social services, and
more generally, the promotion of private sector investment and growth are expected to generate real
growth in employment.
11.          A program for decentralizing government functions and services to the municipalities has
been prepared by the Ministry of Internal Administration. It calls for more equitable distribution of
resources to municipalities on the basis of transparent criteria. It envisages a much greater role for
municipalities in the development and maintenance of utilities and infrastructure and the provision of
social services. However, the capacity of the municipalities to manage economic and socW development
was seriously affected by the political transition when the appointed presidents and members of muicipal
councils were replaced by elected ones who owe their positions less to technical merit than political skill.
Also, prior to the elections, municipal staff played only minor roles, with virtually all government
functions and services managed and controlled by the central administration. Tbe municipalities thus are
sorely lacking in the human and material resources to take on the larger role and responsibilities presently
envisaged for them under the decentralization plan.
12.          The Goverment is keenly aware of these weaknesses. It has begun to identify the needs
for stngening the municipalities through a process of consultation and discussion. Several of the
larger municipalides have already prepared comprehensive proposals regarding their new roles and the
resources and capacity building required. The analysis of the proposals is underway and plans for the
allocation of resources and for the recruitment and training of municipal staff are being developed.
13.          In the process of decentizing functions and services, and transfrming the role of the
municipalities, two issues are of special concern to the Government: (i) reladons between the
municipalities and the central administration; and (ii) the relations between the municipalities and the
various groups and constituencies in the communities. For that reason the decentralization plan contains
proposals to develop common procedures for fiscal and financial functions and social services delivery
to ensure the efficient exchange of information and adequate provision for monitoring and auditing.
14.          At the same time, the municipalities must be responsive in their new roles to local
political forces and meet the needs of local communities: The Government believes that regional
economic and social development must be driven by grass-roots participation in the identificadon of
priorities and the formulation of social and economic policies and programs. The Government is
- 156 -



ANNE
Institotil Dev.
Page 4 of 4
therfore encouragn  the muwicipalities to foster local associaons and organizatons that develop
community self-help programs and define local projects catering to the needs of the poor and less
privileged groups in the society, particularly women and youth.
15.           To generate grea  communit participation in public afirs, the Government plans to
encourage municipalities to develop stronger ties with the press. More frequent press briefings, greater
access to information and assistance-with strengthening the capacity of the press to report on community
affairs are being discussed with the municipalities.

3.                     Labor legislation is of relavely rocent origin; but, like the Commdc   Code,

any desir for growth and development  Like the CoImmrcial Code, the exist   labor legislation is

an fxed-tm contacts, which were practically inapplicable. The follow-up and overall revisions would
be supported under the proposed project.
4.   QOer Legislaji  Prvsions. Provisions contained in a number of other legislative texts have also
been cited as possible obstacles to growth and developmen. These are the Code of Civil l;edue and
the leisation gwanig notarial acts, and the companies' and property registers, aUl of which are
cnsidered as Important tools of economic development. The Code of Civil Procedure is reatvely old and
outdated, as are the procedures that it prescribes. The legislative provisions governing notarial acts, as well
as those reating to the companies' and property regsters, are unduly complex and rigid, and have the effect
of delaying and comlicating coerci and business transactions.
B) u3le   btigna
S.    The Ministry of Justice and Labor shares responsibility for the administration of the justice system
with the Procazez Gdnkad or PGs Office and the tribunals. The Ministry of Justice consists of four main
Directorates, including one Directorate which is responsible for studies, legislative drafting, documntation
and informadon (DGELDI) and another which is responsible for notaia services and companies, property
and other registries (DORN). The other two Directorates, which are responsible for judicial matters and
priso services, respectively, are not relevant fbr preseot purposes and are not discussed any futher herein.
6.        L      DGELDI has overl responsibility to udertak law reform, and to provide advice and
iance generally to all other Govement Mnistres on legislative proposals. In practice, for lack of
adequate capacity, it has never been in a position to fillly perform this role. I is headed by a Director-
Geneal, who unfi recently, was assisted by a couple of junior lawyers, none of whom had any paricular
eertise in the legislative drafting process, for which DGEIDI was created in the fir plae. Also, most
of the legal officers of the Ministry of Justice were degree holders, without any post-graduate professional
qualification. Accordingly, their stats is somewhat lower than that of magisa; so are their wages and
beneft. Due to budgetary constrain, they opeate in tight and difficult conditions. They are poorly paid
nd r-epped to perfom the critical responsibilities that are vested in them. Not surprisgly, it has
proved difficult to retain staff in the Diretorate on a regular basis. Most of them simply left for better and
more lucradve prospects or contined to work on a part-ime basis. The Government bas recendy taken
swps to strengthen the capacity of DGELDI, by assiging more lawyers and experienced magistres. The
Govrment bas also began to address the problem of high rate of staff wnover in the Ministry of Justice
as a whole and, in paricular, vwthin DGELDL Magiate can now be redeployed from the dti s and
the PGs Office to ftll up posions or to perform tasks wifhin DGELDL  Under the proposed project,
DGELDI would be provided with appropriate materials and equipment to be in a position to discbarge its
functions (para 3.1.2).
7.     BGRMI. DGRNI has eclusive responsibility for the provision of notarial services to the public at
large. It has responsiblityto authenticate business    actions, including thoserelating to the incorporaion
of companies. The Directorate doubles up as the companies and property register. It is also responsible
for civil status acts and for the pwvion of personal identifcation cards.
8.    Because DGRM  performs all its services manually, not surprisingly, it constitutes a major
bottleneck for business and other tasactions. Notwithstanding the brave attempts by its personnel to
expedite the procssing of transactions, unavoidably, there are delays both in the provision of notaral
services and in the subsequent process of regisraion. Irrespective of any attempt to streamline the process

Leg and Judicial
Page 3 of 4
of establishment of companies, there is a dear need to computeize the sysm  and, thereby, achieve
substatal gains in the processing time for business activities.
9.    Iibuna&. 'he courts' system consists of an appellate jursfsiction and a jurisdiction of first
instauce. lhe Supreme Court exercies appellate jurisdiction, whist fir instance jurisdiction belongs by
and large to the regional and sub-regional courts. In Prala, as in Sao Vicente and Santa Cawina, there are
separate judges for civil and crimina cases. In all other courts of first instance, the same judge is
competent to hear and dispose of all cases, civil and crimial alike. There is preselly no spWcialized
jurisditdon for commercial or indusial cases.
10.   Judges as a rule are not properly trained, particuarly for commercial and business law cases. They
are poorly paid and generally ill-equipped to perform their functions. As other Government employees,
ihey have experienced the harsh realities of drastic cuts in Govemment budget. Although their conditions
are marginally better than those of legal officers of the Ministry of Justice, many continue to leave for the
bar and other more lucrative prospects.
mL TE PROPOSED PROJECT
A) Law Risn
11.   Annex 9 presents in matrix fom, the major activities to be undern under the proposed project,
the  cted outcomes and the timing targets. The Government has decided to proceed with a full-scale
revision of its commercial and labor legislation, and other related legislation, which have a direct impact
upon commercial and business activities in the couny. To this end, it has been taking major steps to
strengthen the capacity of the DGELDI to undertake law reform and to provide advice and guidance to other
Government Ministries on legislative proposals.
12.   Thus, under the Project DGELDI will be provided with appropriate materias and equipm    that
it requires to be in a position to discharge its functions. Speciaized short-term advisory services wil be
made avalable to DGELDID as and when needed, to help supevie the legsative draftng activities, and
to tra DGELDI legal staff.
13.   To ensure continuity in the legislative reorm process, the Government has take measures to
contain the high rate of staff turnover in the Miniy of Justice as a whole and, in parar, within
DGELDL Magistra  can now be redeployed from the tribunais and the PG*s Office to fill up posiins
or to perform specific tasks within DGELDI. In order to enhance the caeer prospects of legal officers of
the Ministry of Justice, the Govermment will, under the prqject (see training). take measures (e.g. ameig
rules governing the recitment of magistrates, localy-sponsored training programs combining practical
traing and courses at the Center for Judicial Training to be cated) to allow  them to qualify as
magsraes otherwise than by havig to follow a profsional tning program in Portugal. Other means
of atacting competent legal staff to occupy key positons in the Miistry of Justice would also be reiewed.
14.   In order to supervise the process of revision of the various pieces of legislation, the ooordinat
commttee has been expanded to include a wide cross-section of busies representatives, business law
experts, labor unions, employers and other interested officials. Ihe expanded committee's purpose is to
help review draft strategy papers and legislative proposals, as and when they are submitted to it by
DGELDI; the committee will not be exptd to draft or iniiae legislation.

Role of the Project Coordintat
Page I of 2
REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUIBLC SECrOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
ROLE AND RESPONSEBHITIFS
Th 7e role of Project CDoordinator (PC) is geneally to ensur that the various components of the
Proect are implemented in a timely, coordinated, and coherent maimer with the objectve of meetin the
stated objective of the project at a rapid pace. To enable ft to fulfill this objective, the PC will
specifcaly undertake the folowing actions:
d deine, on an annual basis, a program and a schedule for implementation of the various
component  of the Project, including the targets and the ways of monitorig
-  ensure that all the comiponents are carred out in an action-oriented manner;
c emur that the Project Components Managers maintain up-to-date records regarding the
status Of implmenato of their component of the Project with a view to faciitating
problem-solving, and preparing quartedly progress reports and making them available on
atlmely maner totheinszitutions involved in the Project, as well astol]DA. The
Project Component Managers wil be responsible to prepare quarterly and other reports
for submission to the PC for coordination; and
-  review, on a quarterly basis, the status of implementaton of the Project with a view to
adopting corrective measure if necessary, and bringing to the attention of the RAIM
and IDA any problem/issues arising therefrom which may impede, in a substantial.
manner, the imlmnainof the Project.
2.    Under the oabdminstatv supervision of the Minster of Sotat for Economic Coordination the
Project Coordintor (PC) is the Secrtary of the Reform Advisory and ImlmnainMonitoring Board
(RAIMB). In this regard, the Project Coordinato will:
-  develop and animate the coordination mechanims and procedurm required for a timey
implementation of all components of the Public Sectr Reform and Capacity Building
program of the Government;
-  regularly monitor the progress achieved under the various comiponents, and report, as
necessary, to RAIMB on issues having an impact on the achievement of the objectives
of the program; and
-  recommend to RAIME any course of action likely to facilitate the achievement of the
objectives.
- 202-



ANEXM 
Role of the Project Coordinator
Page 2 of 2
3.    Ihe project Coordinator also centaizes and maintains the Information base on the stus of the
iplementtion of the Public Sector Rebrm and Capacity Buiding program, in addition to beng
responsible for all admintive funedons related to the project. In this rpect, the responsibilities of
the Project Coordinator will:
-     undertake centrally, for all components of the project, the procuement, disbursement,
acoouning and auditing activities, in line with the guidelines and requirements of IDA-
financed projects;
-     define procedures to ensure: (i) an adequate coordination of the programs and action
plans of the various units involved in the project, such that all actions bear the approval
of appropriate levels and authorities as applicable; (ii) that the program is Implemented
to schedule;
-     define reporting procedures and formats for communication by the various units of the
informaton regarding the implementation of the project, such as detailed work programs,
progress report, financial data and other supporting documents; and
-     consolidate and communicate this bimation In such a format and at such irvals as
may be agreed upon with IDA for the purpose of supervision of the project.

ANNE 12
Role of Project Component Managers
Page 1 of I
REPUBIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECT
ROLE AND RESPONSIBILITIES
OF THE PROJECT COMPONENT MANAGERS
1.   eTh role of the Project Component Managers (PCMs) is generally to ensure that, each in hisher
own sector, the actions required to achieve the objectives of the Public Sector Reform and Capacity
Building program of the government are carried out in an orderly and timely manner.
2.    Under the direct supervision of his/her Minister, each PCM will specifically undertake the
following actions:
-     on an annual basis, prepare and submit to the Reform Advisory and Implementation
Monitoring Board (RAIMB), for review and approval, a time-bound action program for
the implementation of the various actions included in the program in hisher sphere of
responibility;
-     forward, in a timely mamer, all request for procurement of goods and services required
for the implementation of the action-program agreed for his/her component to the Project
Coordinator (PC), in line with the financing available under the PSRCB project or other
sources;
-     monitor the quality and timeliness of the work performed under his/her component, both
by civil servats and by outside experts;
-     organize and dbair meetings, as needed, with all persons in charge of taking acdon under
his/her component of the program;
-     report, as appropriate, to his/her Minister on the progress of his/her component of the
program, and on any implementaion issue which cannot be dealt with at his/her level;
-     participate in the meetings of RAIMB, and bring to its attention any problem/issues
which may impede, in a substantial maimer, the timely achievement of the objectives of
his/her level;
-     mainain complete and up-to-date records on the status of implementation of hislher
component of the program; and
-     prepare quarterly progress reports and forward them to the Project Coordinator, in the
format and by the date prescribed by the PC.

ANNB 13
Role of the RAIMB
Page  of 2
REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBUC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACffY BUIDING PROJECT
ROLE AND RESPONSIBILITIES OF THE
AEFRX       W =IOR A NDP IEMbiT  ANTIUOBN BOR M B3m  
1.    The Government of Cape Verde (GOCV) intends to create a Reform  Advisory and
Implementation Board (RAIMB) to provide a high level advisory group to the Miister of State for
Economic Coordination and the Minister of Public Administration in the context of the Implemenion
of the government's Public Sector Reform and Capacity Building progam.
2.    The RAIMB wUIl consist of the Minister of State for Economic Coordination as Chairman, the
Minister of Public Administration as co-Chairman, the Project Coordinator (PC), as Secretay, the Project
Component Managers (PCMs), and such representatives of the public and private sector selected on the
basis of thek high tecnica competence and experience as the Government may wish to appoint on this
Board.
3..  The RAIMB will be responsible for advising the Chairmen and, through them, the Govmnnt,
on all actions required to ensure a successful and timely implementation of the Public Sector Reform and
Capacity Building program of the Government in pursuit of the following broad objectives:
reform and moderization of the civil service, through the implementation of (i) an
improved human resource planning and management sysm, (ii) a voluntary departre
program, and (iii) a comprehensive training program directed at managers, admnistrtr
and support staff in public administration;
development andlor creation of the capacity to participate in economic policy-making and
to manage local affairs through a program of decatralization and municipal
strengthening;
-     support and improvement of economic management capacity through: (i) resttuing
of the national accounts and establishment of a multi-year rolling investment program,
(hi) strengthening the capabiities in economic policy analysis, external debt and public
finance management, and aid coordination, (>il) improvement in the conication af
economic policy, Civ) development of modem  it-govmen  comicao
nfrmation and decision systems;
-     reform and modernization of the legal and judiciary systems tough (i) revision of ihe
commercial and labor legisladon, ii3 specialized training in business law for
Ciii3 establishment of specialized jurisdiction to dispose of commercial disputes, (iv)
strengthening the capacity of the Ministry of Justice to draft, revise and update legislaton
on a routine basis, and (v) technical modernizadon of the judiciary, including through
computerizion; and
_ 205 _

improvement the maageme  of public assets and reform public procurement througb
(0) selective reson of the legislation, ()iatng of tcical staff in dhe of public
pouman and asset management, and (iiD c uizaton.
4.    Acordingly, the role and responsibilites of the members of the RAIMB will be:
to provide guidance to the Miisters of Ste of Economic Coordination and of Justice
and Labor on the dieto  and pace of the overall progam, and on the prposed
measure and priorides of each component, as stated in the Project Component managers'
yearly or quarterly action-plans;
to review the implementation progess achieved in each component, and to advise the
Miistes of Stte on changes to be introduced, if needed, to ensure effectiveness,
efficiency and timeliness in program implementation; and
to prepae and submk to the Ministers of Stateregular reports on the degree of
achievement of the objectves of the program, and to recommend adjustment, if
necessary.

Supervision Plan
Page 1 of 2
REPUBLIC OF CAPE VERDE
PUBLIC SECTOR REFORM AND CAPACITY BUILDING PROJECr
SUlPERVISION PLANV 
1.    IDA SUPERVISION  INPUT
The staff input indicated in the table below is in addition to regular supervision needs for review
of progress reports, procurement, and audit actions, correspondence, etc. It is also in addition to the
supervision time being provided to the Privatization TA Project (Cr. 2377-V), which is closely linked
to civil service, decentrization and legal reforms components in particular.
2.    BORROfflm!S CONTRIBUTIMN TO SU-PERVISION
(a)    Progress reports will be submitted by the Ministry of Economic Coordination no later
tan 30 days after the end of eah of the following quarters: March 31, June 30, September 30, and an
annual report will be prepared for each calendar year of project execution and furnished within two
moonths of that year end (pars. 3.10 SAR).
(b)   The progress reports should cover all aspects of project execution;
(c)   The progress reports will be prepared on the basis of the periodic assessments on project
implementation submitted by Project Component Managers in each of the agencies
involved in the project;
(d)   Anmual audits of the project accounts will be submitted no later than June 30 of each
year; and
(e)    The Project Coordination Unit will be responsible for coordinating IDA supervision
missions.
3.    ANNUAL PR,OGRESS REV
Not lat than October 31, 1994 and thereafter, not later than October 31 of each subsequent year,
the Borrower and IDA would undertake a joint review of the Project or as the case may be not later than
October 31, 1996, a mid-term review, during which they snall exchange views generally on all mattes
relating to the project and the peformance by the Borrower, and in particular review (J) progress
achieved during the curren year, and (i) the proposed budget and work program for the coming year
including (a) a statement of monitable objectives and a program of activities to be undertaken to achieve
those objectives, and (1) detaied budgets and financing plans, including a schedule of proposed
procuement cont   to be financed by the credit, and training plans. The Quarterly Progress Reports
shall serve as the basis for the Annual Progress Review.
